0 to $250M ARR in 12 Months: Genspark & the Global Knowledge Worker | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·21:18·13 views·2026-07-22
Wen SangCo-Founder & COO, Genspark Brian BaumannFounder, NYSE Wired
TL;DR"In the past, we human beings, we are the production engine.
Wen Sang shares how GenSpark scaled from $0 to $250M ARR in 12 months by building an agentic AI workspace that orchestrates over 70 frontier and open-weight models to automate busy work for a billion-plus knowledge workers. Explosive Revenue Growth: Zero to $50M ARR:...

TL;DR: Wen Sang shares how GenSpark scaled from $0 to $250M ARR in 12 months by building an agentic AI workspace that orchestrates over 70 frontier and open-weight models to automate busy work for a billion-plus knowledge workers.

Company Growth & Scaling Metrics

  • Explosive Revenue Growth:
    • Zero to $50M ARR: Reached within 5 months of launching the individual product in April (driven by 2 million users in 45 days via product-led, bottom-up growth on X and YouTube).
    • $100M ARR: Reached at 9 months.
    • $250M ARR: Reached at 12 months, alongside raising approximately $645M to $700M in funding.
  • Enterprise Expansion:
    • GenSpark for Business: Launched in November (right before Thanksgiving) after employees brought individual usage into corporate settings and demanded enterprise features like centralized billing, SOC 2 Type 2, and SSO.
    • Customer Base: Reached 6,000+ business clients globally within 6 months across industries including system integration, consulting firms, advertising agencies, consumer brands, and manufacturing.

Founding Team & Origins

  • Pedigree and Background:
    • Wen Sang (Co-Founder & COO): Automotive engineer by training with a PhD from MIT (2013); previously built and ran a vertical SaaS company for municipalities and commercial real estate for 8–9 years (backed by Coast Ventures and Y Combinator) before selling it in 2022.
    • Eric: Early team member at Microsoft Bing working with Satya Nadella; prior to GenSpark, built a voice AI and deep learning company from 0 to $5.5B in value alongside co-founders K and Ray.
    • Ray: Spent 14 years at Google building early search ranking technologies.
    • K: Spent ~8 years at Google building search ranking technologies.
    • Lendroy: Enterprise tech leader who spent ~10 years at Google before building teams at Pinterest, YouTube, and TikTok.
  • The Hard Pivot:
    • Initial Product (Late '23 / Early '24): Originally built a next-generation personalized AI search engine that grew to 5 million users.
    • The Pivot: Realized search had a lot of buzz but little monetization and that Google would replicate basic search; asked why people search (to get work done), and pivoted in early '25 to leverage newly emerged reasoning capabilities to build the first version of the AI workspace.

Product Architecture & "Mixture of Agents"

  • Multi-Model Orchestration:
    • 70+ Models Integrated: The platform sits on top of and orchestrates over 70 frontier models (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google) and open-weight model providers (Fireworks, Fell, AWS Bedrock, GCP).
    • Specialized Routing: Assigns tasks to the model best suited for them—GPT models for reasoning and planning, Claude models for coding and HTML rendering, Google models for images, video, and artistic work, and open-weight models for fast, cost-effective Python scripting and visualization in Jupyter Notebooks.
  • Full-Stack Automation Features:
    • GenSpark Superagent: Acts like an FSD (Full Self-Driving) system for knowledge work; users command outcomes rather than manually typing, formatting, or building models cell-by-cell or pixel-by-pixel.
    • Agentbase: A newly launched capability that lets users command GenSpark to build lightweight custom software applications like CRMs, ATS, and lightweight ERPs tailored to their exact preferences and data workflows, reducing traditional SaaS bloat.

Enterprise Adoption & Workplace Impact

  • Death by 1,000 Pilots: Enterprise companies struggling with IT buying 20 to 50 seats of dozens of different tools found success through bottom-up employee budgets ($50–$100/month) that allowed successful AI use cases to bubble up organically.
  • Executive Leadership: High-profile adoption—such as CEO Miguel at a major global system integrator—proves that when leadership uses AI as a daily power user, it fundamentally changes organizational culture.
  • Real-World Use Cases:
    • Bank Loan Consulting: Dallas-based bank loan consultant Scott uses GenSpark to perform market analysis, build full financial models (P&L, balance sheets, cash flow scenarios), and generate presentations in hours/days instead of weeks/months, allowing him to cut out outsourced analysts, retain all margins, and serve 5x more clients.

Notable Quotes

"In the past, we human beings, we are the production engine. Um, we get onto, you know, all kinds of work, may it be building a financial model, crafting a document, building a presentation. We do it hands-on... Now, with GenSpark, you don't do that anymore." — Wen Sang
"The world of everybody kneeling before one AI god is long gone now. We're seeing a lot more open-weight models, the floor is rising, and we're continuously seeing... high performance frontier models coming out." — Wen Sang
"What we're building at GenSpark is not the next generation of software, no. We're building the next generation users of the software." — Wen Sang
I'm great, Brian, how are you? I'm doing wonderful. It is very bright in here, to be honest. All right, when you're the co-founder and CEO, COO, you guys have gone from zero to 250 million in 12 months, you raised 700 million dollars roughly. 645. Roughly. What's going on over there? What's happening? So, before I get into that, Brian, a little bit about the company, I guess. My name is Wen Sang, and I'm the co-founder and CEO of GenSpark. We are a Palo Alto-based technology company, and we build AI agents for knowledge workers. What that means is, in the past, we human beings, we are the production engine. We get onto all kinds of work, may it be building a financial model, crafting a document, or building a presentation. We do it hands-on; we craft the document word by word, we build the presentation pixel by pixel, and we build the financial model cell by cell on spreadsheets. Now, with GenSpark, you don't do that anymore. You just command GenSpark Superagent. You say, "Hey GenSpark, research immediate stock, get me all the sentiment from Wall Street, get me projections for the next 3, 6, 9, 12 months, and tell me if it makes sense to buy 10 million dollars worth of it and give me the best, the worst, and the most probable scenarios." It will just spin up all the agents to get onto it for you, so that you could work like a Jamie Dimon. You don't have to type hard to get work done. You don't see Jamie doing that, right? That's what we do. And to what you said, what happened there? Yeah, what happened? We launched our product back in last April, and it was for individuals. Within 45 days, I think we got to about 2 million users. It was completely product-led growth, bottom-up, and the moment we pushed our product out on our X portal, on our YouTube channels, people from all over the world just started signing up. Within 5 months, we broke 50 million dollars of ARR. It's a subscription model and, 9 months, 100 million dollars ARR; 12 months, 250. Wow. That's really fast. Where did the founding team come from? What were you guys doing beforehand? And then, what made you start this company? Thank you, Brian. I was an automotive engineer by training. I got my PhD from MIT back in 2013. After that, I built a vertical SaaS company. We sold software to municipalities and commercial real estate owners. I tried raising money in Boston, couldn't make it happen, so moved to Silicon Valley and got backed by Coastal Ventures, Y Combinator, and a bunch of other good people in the Valley. I ran that company for about 8 or 9 years and sold it in '22. My other co-founders—we have altogether six co-founders—I'm the least technical guy on the team. My co-founder Eric was an early team member at Microsoft Bing. He worked with Satya Nadella back in the days, and right before GenSpark, he built a company together with K, our CTO, and Ray, my other co-founder, and Justin from 0 to 5.5 billion dollars worth, leveraging deep learning technologies and voice AI, the last generation AI. For most of them, we met at Google. Except Eric, we're all kind of connected before, through college or through work. Ray was at Google for about 14 years; K was also there for about 8 or so years and built some of the earliest search ranking technologies. The team knows intrinsically how the internet works. Lendroy, my co-founder who is on enterprise tech, he was at Google also for about 10 years and then went on to build teams at Pinterest, YouTube, and then TikTok. So you guys do have the pedigree. Was this lightning in a bottle or did you guys pivot? Well, we had a hard pivot, actually. The company was founded late '23, early '24, and the first product we built was actually a search engine, the next generation search engine. What I mean by that is when ChatGPT came out, it was just clear that search is no longer going to be one keyword where everybody sees the same thing and you just browse through 10 different results trying to find the most useful information. Everybody's going to get personalized information. We built that and we grew that to about 5 million users. But then, very quickly, we realized, you know what, the future of search is not going to be about search. Everybody's going to do search that way, including Google. There was a lot of buzz but not a whole lot of money, so we were like, "Okay, what do we do now?" We asked, "Why do people need information? Why do we need search?" Well, we need information to get the work done. So we asked, "Can we push the AI steps further and actually get the work done?" That's when the reasoning capabilities of the models really came through at the beginning of '25, and we just took advantage of that, built out the first version of our workspace, and yeah. Nice. So you're going after a billion-plus knowledge workers. How does GenSpark actually change the life of knowledge workers? To what we talked about earlier, our view is that too many of us have our talent, our time, and our energy trapped in busy work. Each of us has to use 20, 30, or 40 tools to really get through the busy work to get the real business done. That is a waste of human talent. We basically wanted to help all of us to skip through that so that we could be creative and strategic. We could focus on getting the business done instead of just copying and pasting the same piece of business context from a meeting note to a Slack message, to an email, to building a sales proposal. I'll give you an example. One of our power users is a bank loan consultant named Scott. He helps restaurants to secure bank loans. He would go into bank meetings with research he'd hired someone to do, and financial models he'd hired some other CPA firm analysts to build, to convince the bank to give this restaurant 1 or 2 million dollars for a patio project. He would get paid 30 or 40 thousand dollars, and he would pay out of his pocket to the people who count foot traffic, build financial models, and build the presentations—roughly 15 to 20 thousand dollars. That's how he did it in the past, and it would take weeks, if not months. Now, he just says, "Hey GenSpark, here's a restaurant, here's the zip code, research this, and then give me the full market analysis I could use to convince the bank." He says, "Hey GenSpark, build me the full financial model of the payment plan, and here are the historical P&L, balance sheet, and cash flow statement of this restaurant; build it out for me, give me the best, worst, and most probable scenarios." He says, "Hey GenSpark, build me the full presentation." He still walks into the bank meeting together with his client and secures the bank loan. Now he charges his client a lot less, but he makes all the margin because he doesn't have to pay the other folks. He gets this done in hours or days, not weeks or months, and he gets to serve five times more clients. He gets to make a lot more money and makes everybody a lot happier. His attention is on the clients and the banks; it's not on fixing the format of a presentation. That's the kind of difference, and that applies to solo dev shops, marketers, and everybody. This is talking about individuals. How does this look like when it scales across an enterprise? 100%. When we first started, the platform was really built for individuals, and then summertime last year, a lot of corporations started reaching out, basically telling us, "Our employees use your product individually, they really love it, and they want the company to pay for it." At that time, we said, "Okay, you can just swipe the corporate card." They said, "No, no, we need centralized billing, we need SOC2 Type 2, we need SSO, we need all the enterprise stuff." So, that's when we built GenSpark for Business and released it in November, right before Thanksgiving. Six months in, we got to about 6,000 plus business clients from all over the world. Today, we work with marketing teams, CMOs, and CROs from industries like system integration, consulting firms, advertising agencies, consumer brands, and manufacturing. What's working and what's not within the enterprise? One thing that we heard a lot about last year is enterprise companies initiating pilots with AI. There are so many tools on the market, a ton. Everybody's buying 20 seats or 50 seats, just going through pilots. There's a "death by 1,000 pilots," right? So it's difficult. Instead of commanding a dedicated team to try all the tools, what we saw that really helped was a bottom-up approach. They put money behind employees saying, "For all of our employees, you can use AI tools—50 or 100 bucks a month—and for the good ones, you let them bubble up." Eventually, we saw a lot of adoption. One of our clients, a major global system integrator, saw their teams using us and pushed this to their leadership team. Their CEO, Miguel, became a power user. He showed his leadership team how he was using AI, and we saw the power come through that model right away. When your CEO is a power user of AI, the whole company just changes how they view this. If you want organizational change, you better lead by example. We were at your office three weeks ago; you did the Nvidia example for us, and all of a sudden, I get a phone call from your agent scheduling a lunch for the next day. I thought that was really interesting. A hot topic this week is Sovereign AI. How do you position yourself in that? Are you a competitor to Anthropic, Google, OpenAI, or Codex, or are you complimentary? Where do you fit in that ecosystem? I love the question, Brian. Look, the reality is that right now it's a "wild west." Everybody's doing everything, let's be honest. It's great. Why? If you compare the usage of AI and the internet today, no one is not touching the internet around the globe. But for AI, we saw the chart—the majority of humanity has not really touched AI yet. It's not in every aspect of our lives. It takes a holistic, collective effort to make AI available for everybody. For us, we work very closely with all the Frontier Labs—OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, and all the open-weight model providers like Fireworks and Fell. We work with all of them, and we're some of the top partners for them. We also work with Microsoft, AWS Bedrock, and GCP as our platform partners. The way we work with them is that we released the world's first "mixture of agents" tech architecture. We sit on top of 70-plus models and we orchestrate them to command the perfect model to work on the right thing at the right time. They're all good at different things. GPT models are better at reasoning; Claude models are still better at coding; Google models are better at image, video, and artistic stuff; and open-weight models are often cost-effective or fast. For you to build a presentation, GPT models might help you reason and plan, Claude might write coding or HTML to render slides, Google models generate images, and if you want to visualize data, our GenSpark Superagent is commanding an open-weight model to write Python coding in a Jupyter Notebook to visualize that data before sticking it back in. Working with everybody and taking advantage of all the greatest models is how we do it. But more importantly, I would add one thing: many of our Frontier Labs focus on pushing the envelope for intelligence. They want their model to solve a partial differential equation or become the next scientist, which is fantastic and important. Intelligence is the raw horsepower. They build amazing engines and Formula 1 racing cars. But to drive that, you have to be skilled; you have to have a special set of skills to maneuver it. Not everybody can take the full power out of that. We understand that AGI is already here; it's just not everywhere. How do we put it in the hands of everybody? We built the "Model X FSD." You don't have to learn how to drive at all. You just tell GenSpark what you want or where you want to go, and we deliver you to the destination. You don't even need to touch the steering wheel. Over the last couple of years, we've heard doom and gloom about employees and jobs not being there. You're going after a billion-plus market. Are you making their lives better, or are you canceling jobs? Damn, Brian, that's a tough question. There's a lot of fear, and it's all natural. When someone tries AI or GenSpark for the first time and sees the AI doing something they're familiar with so well that they think, "What am I going to do?" that feeling is natural. AI is here to stay, and it's getting better every day. But if you look back at history, a thousand years ago, many people were washing their laundry by hand. Today, nobody does that, and nobody complains that the laundry machine took their job. The fundamental shift of jobs is happening, but it is not about AI wiping out all the jobs. It's about helping the people who decide to jump onto it, embrace it, and become good at it. They will make these folks faster, put them at the front and center of their companies as the AI experts, and they'll get a lot more exposure. That is no different than any other technological revolution: electricity, internet, PC. It's the same. If you have the initiative to become good at it, you're going to do very well. I'd say, brace for impact, but get prepared right now. I think for me, it just makes me more efficient. You just launched Agentbase. What is it, and what market are you going after? We've all heard the phrase "SaaS is dead." Agentbase is basically something you can command GenSpark to build for you. I don't mean the whole Salesforce platform, but today we use Salesforce internally. Why pay full subscription costs for software when you probably only use 5% of it? It's so clunky that you have to hire consultants just to interact with the product. Why not ask your agent to build it out exactly to your preference? Agentbase is exactly for that, whether it's CRM, ATS, or lightweight ERP. It's your data, and you decide how it flows through your workflows. Why shouldn't you be able to have full automation from email, phone calls, and meeting notes all in one place without you touching it? We're running low on time. One thing that you believe about AI that many in this room may disagree with? I believe the world of everybody kneeling before one "AI god" is long gone. We're seeing more open-weight models, the floor is rising, and we're continuously seeing high-performance frontier models coming out. Everybody will have optionality, and the real value is not going to be in the models as much. The value that is going to be created is from context. The know-how, the connections with other human beings—that is where the value lies. What we're building at GenSpark is not the next generation of software; we're building the next generation of users of software. The future is not going to be each of us juggling 30 or 40 tools. We don't need to get certified for Excel anymore; that's not the world we deserve to live in. We should be good at working with other human beings, assessing opportunities, and understanding risks, then letting agents handle the detailed work. GenSpark came from wanting to see a world full of humanity's creative sparks. We're the directors; AI is the actor. I know we're out of time, but if anyone is in New York City next week, we're hosting an event with GenSpark at the New York Stock Exchange. If you're unable to come, go to LinkedIn, find "NYSE Wired," and we'll do a deep dive with Wen before we actually do the event. That's right. NYSE and GenSpark, next Tuesday in New York. Thank you. Thank you.

NVIDIA & Wistron: Reindustrializing America With Advanced Manufacturing

NVIDIA(2.2M)·15:05·7.6K views·2026-07-22
Jensen HuangFounder and CEO, NVIDIA Simon LinChairman, Wistron
TL;DRJensen Huang and Simon Lin discuss how the buildout of AI supercomputers and factories—such as Wistron's new Fort Worth facility producing NVIDIA GB300 and Vera Rubin chips—is driving a multi-trillion-dollar reindustrialization of the United States.
Wistron's U.S. Facility:...

TL;DR

Jensen Huang and Simon Lin discuss how the buildout of AI supercomputers and factories—such as Wistron's new Fort Worth facility producing NVIDIA GB300 and Vera Rubin chips—is driving a multi-trillion-dollar reindustrialization of the United States.

AI Infrastructure and Advanced Manufacturing

  • Wistron's U.S. Facility: Producing the NVIDIA GB300 Grace Blackwell Ultra Superchip and will produce the NVIDIA Vera Rubin Superchip in Fort Worth, Texas.
  • Scale of the GB300 Supercomputer:
    • Contains 1.5 million parts
    • Weighs 2 tons
    • Priced at $4 million
  • Reindustrialization Impact: U.S. manufacturing has added several million jobs over the past couple of years across construction, plumbing, electricians, chip plants, packaging plants, computer system plants, and AI data centers/factories.
  • Supply Chain Integration: Taiwan serves as NVIDIA's largest trading partner and a vital supplier for building resilient U.S. manufacturing capacity alongside partners like TSMC and Wistron.

Generative Computing vs. Retrieval Computing

  • Shift to Generative Models: Traditional data centers stored pre-recorded information (photos, videos, files) for retrieval. Modern AI "factories" process generative models that reason, write stories, generate images, create tables, and produce answers dynamically.
  • The "Token" Economy:
    • AI factories manufacture trillions of tokens (numbers that translate into words, poetry, images, and songs).
    • Huang compares the economic value of digital tokens to entertainment spending (e.g., a $24.99 movie translating to billions of tokens).
  • Chip Demand Surge: The transition from retrieval computing (growing at 25% per year) to generative computing supercomputers has increased chip demand by roughly 10x.

Industrial Transformation and the New CAPEX Layer

  • Energy and Sustainability:
    • AI is a major driver for the energy industry, enabling sustainable energy investment and power grid upgrades without government subsidies.
    • Modern AI factories are energy efficient, using closed-loop liquid cooling systems that recirculate water (using roughly the water of a swimming pool with no loss).
  • Every Company as a Technology Company:
    • Industries traditionally characterized by low CAPEX (such as law firms) will transition to higher CAPEX models combining human intelligence (coffee and hamburgers) with digital intelligence (electricity and tokens).
  • Societal Infrastructure: AI factories will become foundational infrastructure akin to roads, railroads, and agriculture, generating a new layer of economic activity valued in the trillions of dollars.

National and Corporate Sovereignty in AI

  • Sovereign Intelligence: Huang argues that no country, company, or society can outsource its culture, intelligence, or knowledge.
  • Model Diversity: Societies should utilize off-the-shelf closed models for general tasks while cultivating their own open and closed models to preserve national and corporate intelligence.

Notable Quotes

"Without the partnership of Taiwan, without the partnership of TSMC and Wistron, uh we wouldn't be able to do what we are doing today. So so for that, I'm incredibly grateful." — Jensen Huang
"This is the first time in human history where we could invest in sustainable energy without contribution from the government, without subsidies, because the market dynamic would itself would invest in sustainable energy." — Jensen Huang
"No country can outsource its intelligence. No company can outsource all of its intelligence. No society can outsource its culture." — Jensen Huang
I want to thank you and Wistron, our partner and friend for over two decades. We started this AI journey with friendship, with your friendship, and building these incredible systems. Because of AI's build out, the largest infrastructure build out in human history, the Taiwan ecosystem and industries and companies are doing incredibly well. Taiwan may be the United States' fourth-largest trading partner, but Taiwan is my largest trading partner. As the largest company in the world, Taiwan is my largest trading partner. And also, in my very first meeting with President Trump, he told me that in his administration, he would like to reindustrialize the United States. He would like to expand the manufacturing capacity and to increase jobs in the United States to reshore the supply chain so that we have a resilient supply chain. Without the partnership of Taiwan, without the partnership of TSMC and Wistron, we wouldn't be able to do what we are doing today. So, for that, I'm incredibly grateful. Manufacturing is an essential pillar for every economy and every country. And manufacturing jobs, as you know, in the United States in the last couple of years have now practically increased by several million people. Building chip plants, packaging plants, computer system plants like this, and of course AI data centers and AI factories all over the United States, is allowing the United States to really reindustrialize for the first time in a long time. It's creating jobs in plumbing, construction, manufacturing of course, and for electricians, and so many different types of jobs here in the United States. In fact, we have so few ourselves that you've imported many from Taiwan to help us. If you go to Arizona today, Arizona looks like Taipei because the great cuisines of Taipei are now in Arizona. I would not be surprised, ma'am, that Dallas-Fort Worth is going to have some of the best beef noodle soups in all of America. So, all of you here, helping start the manufacturing capability here in the United States, your partnership and your investment are really valuable to us. I want to thank you. Thank you. I think that I have some questions to ask Jensen to steal a lot of things from his brain, and then there's no one better. Can we just sit? We were safer standing, but no, this is okay. Anything can happen now. This is okay. Anything can happen. Well, let me start with the fact that we're looking forward for the next 5-10 years. How do you envision the global AI landscape evolving, particularly since we are now talking about the transition from data centers to AI factories? What Simon just said is really important to observe. The data centers of the past were called data centers because they stored pre-recorded information. We would take pictures, we would take videos, we would type down stories, we would describe the news, and we would store it in data center files. And then when somebody calls upon it on a phone, it would retrieve the information and present it to you. That retrieval-based computing model is now being replaced by a generative model where we generate intelligence based on your question. So instead of doing a search, we now just ask it a question and it would go off and do the search, it would read the stories, it would reason about the best way to present the answer to you; maybe it even writes a story, generates an image, creates a table, and answers you according to the nature of your question. It applies intelligence. That intelligence has to be processed mathematically in a neural network, in a machine that's back there. That's the GB300, the most powerful AI supercomputer in the world. It has one and a half million parts inside. These robots have to assemble one and a half million parts. It's two tons, $4 million, and we're producing them like phones, right here in Wistron, and so we crank it out in volume, because the world needs all of these machines to drive the intelligence infrastructure. AI is highly misunderstood. People see AI, and the user interface of AI of course is the chatbot, the web browser, that website. However, inside, it's important to realize AI is actually a whole new industry. AI is incredibly important to the energy industry. Look at all of the energy stocks, look at the energy companies; they're all doing incredibly well. This is the first time in human history where we could invest in sustainable energy without contribution from the government, without subsidies, because the market dynamic itself would invest in sustainable energy. We can upgrade the power grid. All of that is possible because the market has the incredible capacity to fund it. So, the energy sector is being reinvented. The chip industry, of course, as you know, we don't have nearly enough chips in this future of generative computing. In retrieval computing, it was about the right size, growing about 25% per year. All of a sudden, we need supercomputers between you and the file that's in the data center. So, the amount of chips that we need probably increased by 10 times. That's the next layer. The layer above that is infrastructure, the data centers. This is the new factory, the new factory of the future. These factories, the modern versions of them, are incredibly energy efficient. It's sustainable. It barely uses as much water as a swimming pool. All of the water is recirculated through passive energy and thermal cooling systems. All of the liquid is recirculated, none is lost. These modern factories are incredibly sustainable, incredibly energy efficient, and it's good for the environment. What creates around it is large amounts of jobs. It's now created several million manufacturing jobs, construction jobs, electrician jobs. Above that is where the AI model is. That's the layer that everybody sees. But the most important layer ultimately is how that AI technology, the AI models, are diffused into all of the industries: healthcare, transportation, manufacturing, finance, health, education, and scientific research. The diffusion of the technology into the industries, that's ultimately where the big win is. AI is every aspect of that and we're in the middle of transforming it all. When you talk about tokens, actually AI factories manufacture tokens. Now tokens have become a huge industry already. People spend money to use hundreds of thousands of tokens and they really enjoy it. My second question is when we move into... It's so strange, we say tokens have value, and most people have a hard time understanding that. What comes out of that machine is trillions and trillions of tokens. These are numbers; we call them tokens but they're basically numbers. These numbers become words, they become poetry, they become answers to your questions. It becomes an image, a song, it can write whatever you want it to be. It's generating all of this content. It's not illogical that tokens have value. In fact, humanity is largely token-driven today. When you watch Netflix, every one of those pixels came from a token. This weekend I paid $24.99 for a movie and I don't know how that translates to a few billion tokens, but I enjoyed it. Humanity is largely driven by tokens today, and now we have tokens that are not movies; we have tokens that are intelligence. Very simple idea. My second question is when we are moving to an era where AI is adopting and the computing intensity is scaling up exponentially, how will this shape the future industries? Well, every single company will be a technology company in the future. Today, a law firm is low CAPEX. The only energy they need is coffee and hamburgers. They use calories to translate into intelligence. In the future, it will be a higher CAPEX industry. I just gave you an example of a very small industry, legal. In the future, their intelligence will be a combination of hamburgers, coffee, and electricity. Those electricity inputs will generate tokens which will be part of their intelligence. Every single industry in the world is fundamentally grounded on intelligence—financial services, the insurance industry, the healthcare industry, you name it. That intelligence can no longer be produced by cows and grass alone. Steaks will still be extremely important—don't get me wrong, especially here in Texas—we'll still need lots of cows and corn, but it'll be augmented by tokens. When you think about it from that perspective, you realize just as agriculture is a fundamental infrastructure of society, in the future, these AI factories will be a fundamental infrastructure of society. Doesn't it make sense? Today we have roads, agriculture, and railroads; in the future, we'll have AI factories and the internet. Electricity comes in, it gets manufactured, tokens get generated in the factories, and what gets sent out goes out on the internet. It's the exact same idea. This is going to be a whole new layer of society. It will be applied to every single industry. It will be a new layer of CAPEX. This layer of industry is probably worth trillions of dollars. It impacts almost all the industries. Every single industry will need chips. Everybody needs intelligence; everything is built on intelligence. How can communities take advantage of their unique culture and intelligence to build a better AI system? Everyone should—every company, every society, every country—use off-the-shelf intelligence whenever they can. Whenever you can, watch a Hollywood movie, we need it. That's right. So, whenever you can, use off-the-shelf movies, off-the-shelf intelligence, off-the-shelf music. But whenever you must, or you desire, create your own. No country can outsource its intelligence. No company can outsource all of its intelligence. No society can outsource its culture. You cannot take your culture, the intelligence of your country, your company, yourself, and think that you can outsource all of it. You want to import as much intelligence, culture, and knowledge as you can, but you also want to generate your own, and you have to cultivate your own. You cannot outsource that. That's why we need closed models, off-the-shelf closed models, but we also need open models. Every single country, every single company, we need closed and open models. You cannot be deprived of either one. This is a very good lesson for all the communities and countries. I think today we have very short time, but I believe that when we talk to Jensen, he always gives us the vision that from the macro view, the whole AI will evolve into the whole industry and then narrow down to communities and countries. It's a great vision. I think that we are going to enjoy the next era of AI life. I hope that we will be happier and healthier. Thank you, Jensen. We will be happier, we will be healthier. Thank you. Thank you, Simon, for your partnership. Thank you, Wistron. Thank you, everybody. Thank you.

Vibe Coding for the Next Billion | Michele Catasta, Replit | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·16:47·19 views·2026-07-22
Michele CatastaPresident & Head of AI, Replit Max CorbaniFounding Partner, commit
TL;DRReplit President and Head of AI Michele Catasta and founding partner Max Corbani discuss how AI development environments and vibe coding are transforming 60 million non-technical users into software and agent builders.
Background and Mission: Michele Catasta started hacking...

TL;DR

Replit President and Head of AI Michele Catasta and founding partner Max Corbani discuss how AI development environments and vibe coding are transforming 60 million non-technical users into software and agent builders.

Journey to Vibe Coding and Democratizing Software

  • Background and Mission: Michele Catasta started hacking open source at age 16 in Italy and spent 15 years pursuing the idea of making machines write code, eventually doing a PhD in deep learning at EPFL in Lausanne and applying Transformers to source code at Stanford.
  • Early Shipping Strategy: Catasta recommends that builders ship AI products as soon as they have basic reliability, noting that Replit generated thousands of versions of a simple cat image web app before achieving end-to-end reliability.
  • Replit Agent Launch: Replit shipped its AI agent in September 2024—six months before Andre Karpathy coined the term "vibe coding."
  • Current Scale: Replit has crossed half a billion in ARR, serves over 60 million users, and is targeting $1 billion in revenue by the end of the year.

User Base and the Rise of the Agent Manager

  • Citizen Developers: Replit's adoption spans 1.5 billion Excel-trained knowledge workers across departments like HR, go-to-market, and support who need to build software solutions to automate work.
  • General-Purpose Product Strategy: Rejecting the temptation to target a single narrow persona during product-market fit, Replit maintained a general-purpose agent philosophy to serve diverse departments.
  • The Agent Manager Paradigm: Catasta notes that since 2025, every human is becoming an "agent manager" whose daily job involves prioritizing tasks, running multiple agents in parallel, validating results, and delegating work instead of executing steps manually.

AI Models and Open Source Strategy

  • Shift to Heterogeneous Models: Replit initially relied entirely on closed-source frontier models to avoid capital-intensive training, but the strategy has flipped as open-weights models become impressively capable for vibe coding.
  • Task-Specific Intelligence: Catasta emphasizes that intelligence is not one-size-fits-all; Replit maps specific models to specific user tasks using a mix of self-fine-tuned models, open-weights models, and closed frontier models.
  • Industry Benchmarks: Replit recently launched *Vibe Bench*, a new benchmark specifically designed to measure vibe coding capabilities.

The Future: From Apps to Autonomous Agents

  • Disappearance of Traditional Apps: Catasta predicts that traditional software applications and UIs will slowly disappear as humans shift from manual data input to discussing, planning, and delegating end-to-end tasks to agents.
  • High-Level Goal Execution: Replit's next evolution will allow users to specify high-level business goals—such as achieving a 5% conversion rate on a SaaS app—and let autonomous agents handle the execution, testing, and modifications.
  • Workplace Impact: While some job displacement will occur during the transition phase, agents will upgrade daily human work by removing tedious tasks like triaging emails and Slack, enabling leaner companies and new business creation.

Notable Quotes

"If there is someone out there who cares, do it because you're going to start to get feedback, as long as it's not completely janky." — Michele Catasta

So, your job is more trying to figure out, 'Okay, I'm going to prioritize these 10 different tasks. I'm going to run several agents in parallel.'" — Michele Catasta

>

And what I mean by that, we built applications, we built UIs for the last decades because that is the only way that we've been used to interact with computers... The future that I foresee happening, maybe much faster than a lot of people expect, is, we're going to start to build way more agents than actually applications." — Michele Catasta

I do a ton of support work myself, like, I love to talk to our users because fundamentally they tell me what they want. AI and Jobs series: How entry level jobs are changing in an AI era

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·21:56·639 views·2026-07-22
Peter BrownPartner, PwC
TL;DREntry-level jobs are transforming as AI automates routine tasks, with employers increasingly prioritizing applied skills, human judgment, and AI fluency over traditional degrees.
Productivity and Work Redesign: According to PwC's Global AI and Jobs Barometer, organizations in...

TL;DR

Entry-level jobs are transforming as AI automates routine tasks, with employers increasingly prioritizing applied skills, human judgment, and AI fluency over traditional degrees.

The Impact of AI on Entry-Level Roles & Work Redesign

  • Productivity and Work Redesign: According to PwC's Global AI and Jobs Barometer, organizations in AI-exposed sectors that proactively redesign work see a threefold increase in productivity.
  • Task Automation vs. Human Skills: Routine cognitive and repetitive tasks are heavily automated by AI. However, demand for human skills (judgment, problem-solving, critical thinking, relationship building) has experienced a twofold growth rate in AI-exposed sectors.
  • The "Seniorization" of Skills: Employers are asking entry-level workers for seven times greater "seniorized" skills—competencies historically developed in the first three to four years of a career—upfront.

Hiring Shifts and Report Findings

  • Resilient Hiring Trends: Dire headlines regarding entry-level job destruction are not yet backed up by data; many organizations are intentionally increasing hiring to protect future leadership pipelines. One organization cited in the WEF/PwC report increased entry-level hiring by 25% to ensure future leadership.
  • Shifting Employer Criteria: Data from the WEF and PwC report shows that 81% of employers prioritize applied experience, compared to only 34% prioritizing degrees.
  • PwC Evolution: Peter Brown noted that when he joined PwC, there were 17 to 18 entry-level entry points, which has now been streamlined to about five, shifting focus from specific badges and universities to potential and skills.

Global Survey Insights

  • PwC Hopes and Fears Survey: As the largest global survey of workers covering some 55,000 workers, results show that younger workers are more optimistic than anxious regarding AI, though they still navigate broader pressures like the cost-of-living crisis.
  • The Skills vs. Domain Expertise Balance: While domain expertise remains necessary, employers increasingly demand "AI fluency"—requiring workers to experiment with AI tools and build agility.

Practical Advice for New Graduates from Industry Leaders

  • Focus on Outcomes, Not Tasks (Karina Cortez, Cornerstone OnDemand): New grads must focus on wisdom and strategic outcomes rather than rote tasks; focusing purely on tasks risks obsolescence as efficiency tools automate routine work.
  • Lean Into Fear (Karina Cortez): Graduates should embrace uncomfortable opportunities and "say yes" to challenges, noting that personal growth stems directly from stepping into the unknown. Cortez herself takes "vibe coding classes" to stay current.
  • Embrace Non-Linear Career Paths (Maria Flynn, Jobs for the Future): A first job is rarely a dream job or a permanent destination; drawing from her own early experience as an entry-level government worker in Philadelphia with five other women, Flynn advises young workers to treat every role as a learning vehicle to identify skills and preferences.
  • Maintain Passion and Agility (Peter Brown, PwC): Brown encourages graduates to pursue their passions, reject traditional job titles in favor of skills development, experiment with AI tools, and aggressively build interpersonal leadership and teamwork capabilities.

Notable Quotes

"The organizations getting ahead are fundamentally breaking down work and looking at, what is it the outcome they're trying to achieve? What's the role of technology, what's the role of the human being?" — Peter Brown
"If you're a new grad, [and] you are focusing just on the tasks to be done, you are going to get left behind from the people who are actually able to level up, if you will, and really think about the outcome impact." — Karina Cortez
"Where you start is not going to be where you finish... your first job is not going to be the job you have forever. But yet, every job, including that first job, can really give you a wealth of information and experience and skills that you can build upon." — Maria Flynn

That's the fundamental question for leaders. How do you create that experience for entry-level workers to create those skills which traditionally have been developed through, if you like, the apprenticeship of, you know, doing the work and sitting in a room and watching and listening? How do you, how do you recreate those experience, and focus on the learning required such that in five, seven, 10 years time, we've got leaders coming through the pipeline to take on the mantle within the organizations around the world? I think it's, I think there's a responsibility of business leaders, of policymakers, of educators to actually make sure we intentionally design those experiences, such that the young people can, can gather them and gain them, such that they do have the confidence when they go, go into the world of work. We know from our research that, on entry-level skills, employers are asking for seven times greater what we would call seniorized, skills. Skills that historically you'd have developed in the first three to four years. They're asking for those up front. So, how do you create those? It's not going to happen by osmosis. You've got to be intentional about how you do that. My encouragement to young people is, you know, again, take, take heed from, from history. You know, after every sort of revolution in the world of work, there's always been a net increase in jobs. They're just different jobs. And I've said for a long time, you know, you can, you can, protect people, you can't protect jobs. Jobs will change, they'll evolve. I think the difference in, in the current era is the speed of that change. It's happening so, so quickly. But I think my, my encouragement to young people is, you know, take, take, take, take, take, take, a, b, be optimistic around the opportunity AI brings, but recognize that, whereas in the yesteryear, organizations would hire for, if you like, domain technical expertise, I think now, yes, you need domain expertise, but you also need what I call AI fluency. So, for young people, take any opportunity to work with AI, to experiment with it, to create the agility, the adaptability. But the other ingredient that employers are also also seeking are those, you know, people with those human skills, the ones I mentioned earlier on. So, for me, taking any opportunity where you're leading, where you're organizing, where you're bringing people, you know, a group of people through some, through some kind of journey, we have to have, you'll have to learn to have difficult conversations, manage conflicts, learn to build relationships, learn to speak and communicate clearly. Any opportunity to do that, be that at school, or at university, or at college, you know, I'd, I'd embrace with open arms. From the experience of PwC, what are some of the ways that experiences could be redesigned for entry-level workers? Well, I think, you know, we, like everybody else, are on this journey, right? So, I wouldn't for one minute say we've got it cracked, but we have made great progress in terms of how we are intentionally redesigning the experience and work across all our organizations. We have to continually evolve, develop, reinvent, to maintain relevance to our, to our customers. When I joined PwC, I think there were at least 17, 18 ways you could join at the entry-level perspective. It's now down to about five, and it will change again. Again, when I joined, it was people from certain degrees, and that there were, that there were, there were certain universities that we, we hired from. Again, we've, we've been, we're much more diverse, inclusive in terms of how we do that in terms of the the people we hire. And we hire very much more now for, potential and skills, as opposed to badges and career titles. And, and then we've been very intentional around the experience our colleagues have when they join in those early years. And I, I think it's a much broader experience, which, if you like, in terms of we know from, from the Hopes and Fears survey again, workers want flexibility, and they want to be able to apply their skills in a variety of contexts, not just in one particular area. And I think by, by the way we've changed our experience allows for that, so to create much greater agility in the workforce, which I think it works for the, for, for our colleagues, but also as a business it works, because it means we can actually apply, you know, the right skilled people in the right place at the right time much more consistently. Hmm. You're the co-author of the new report that we published with the World Economic Forum and PwC. And I was wondering, what are some of the findings that surprised you the most in that report?
