Venki Ramakrishnan: The Science and Hype of Living Longer | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(126.0K)·47:58·2.5K views·2026-07-22
Venki RamakrishnanNobel laureate in chemistry and author Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management
TL;DRLongevity hype overstates our ability to extend maximum human lifespan past the 120-year ceiling, but health span can be improved through moderate living, sleep, and exercise.
While tech billionaires invest billions into anti-aging ventures and cellular reprogramming, aging is...

TL;DR

Longevity hype overstates our ability to extend maximum human lifespan past the 120-year ceiling, but health span can be improved through moderate living, sleep, and exercise. While tech billionaires invest billions into anti-aging ventures and cellular reprogramming, aging is an analog process driven by molecular and DNA damage that remains fundamentally difficult to hack.

Defining Aging and the Human Lifespan Ceiling

  • Aging Definition: Gradual loss of function: Sir Venki Ramakrishnan defines aging as the gradual loss of function across molecules, cells, tissues, and the entire body due to accumulated damage and changes over time.
  • The 120-Year Ceiling: Average life expectancy has doubled over the last 150 years (rising from roughly 50 to 80), driven by public health, vaccinations, nutrition, and medicine tackling early-life infant mortality and infectious diseases.
  • Maximum Lifespan Limit: The maximum human lifespan ceiling has barely moved; only one person has ever exceeded 120 years, and there is no physical or chemical law guaranteeing we can extend lifespans by hundreds of years.

Cellular Damage, Repair, and the Hallmarks of Aging

  • DNA Damage as a Driver: Molecular deterioration: Damage to DNA and mitochondria—caused by environmental exposure and normal cellular living—is fundamental and drives downstream dysfunction.
  • Cellular Senescence: Cancer defense mechanism: Damaged cells enter senescence, failing to function normally and secreting inflammatory compounds. While this is an early-life cancer prevention mechanism to eliminate mutating cells, accumulated damage overwhelms the body's repair mechanisms with age.
  • Organ Aging: Different organs age at different rates depending on individual genetics and lifestyle factors like smoking or drinking.

The Science and Hype of Cellular Reprogramming

  • Cellular Reprogramming: Reversing biological age: Inspired by John Gurdon cloning frogs via nuclear transfer and Shinya Yamanaka introducing four genes to revert cells into pluripotent stem cells, researchers are trying to wind back the developmental clock.
  • The Human Barrier: Safety and control challenges: Implementing reprogramming safely in humans is extremely difficult because viral vectors or transgenic approaches produce heterogeneous results (some cells reprogram while others do not), and some involved genes are oncogenes capable of causing cancer.
  • Animal Model Reality: While reprogrammed mice look healthier by various criteria, the interventions have not actually increased their overall lifespan.

Billionaires, Longevity Startups, and Tech Bias

  • The Tech Industry Bias: Digital vs. Analog: Tech billionaires who made fortunes in their 20s approach mortality as a software problem to be hacked, failing to recognize that biology operates in the messy analog world.
  • Altos Labs and Funding: Backed by tech figures like Yuri Milner and Jeff Bezos with a $5 billion budget and top Nobel laureates, Altos Labs officially aims to extend health span ("die young after a long time"), though investors also bet on monetizing breakthrough longevity treatments.
  • Skepticism of Single-Subject Trials: High-profile biohackers like Brian Johnson (spending $2 million/year) lack control experiments, mixing treatments that make it impossible to scientifically isolate what is working.
  • Skepticism of Fads: Cryopreservation (freezing a thin mouse brain slice is the closest milestone achieved; freezing a live mouse and thawing it remains science fiction) and young blood transfusions are dismissed as high-markup hype without rigorous validation.

Simple Habits for Health Span

  • Caloric Restriction: Metabolic recycling: While strict caloric restriction causes constant hunger, eating moderately and controlling weight turns on pathways that recycle defective molecules and organelles. GLP1 drugs show potential for metabolic health, though side effects like muscle loss require safe administration.
  • Sleep: Repair cycles: Getting adequate sleep (Ramakrishnan aims for 8 hours) is vital because much of the body's cellular recycling and repair happens during the sleep cycle.
  • Exercise: Tissue regeneration: Combining weight training and cardio (such as cross-trainer elliptical work mimicking cross-country skiing) stimulates tissue regeneration and counters functional decline.

Societal Ethics and the Two-Tiered Future

  • Generational Turnover: Societal stagnation: Doubling lifespans combined with dropping fertility rates would halt generational turnover, leaving entrenched older populations in control of wealth, power, and political systems.
  • The Multi-Tiered Society: Disparity in years and power: Advanced longevity treatments risk creating a two-tier society where wealthy individuals buy extended lives and deeper power advantages, widening the 10-to-15-year life expectancy gap already seen between rich and poor populations (e.g., in the US and UK).

Notable Quotes

  • > "Plan A is simply to try not to die. Plan B is to try to believe that even if you die, your whole body will be resurrected and you will go to some paradise. And plan C is that maybe your body will decay but you will have an immortal soul..." — Sir Venki Ramakrishnan
  • > "The people who are funding it are interested in two things. One is they think there's a lot of money in aging... but the other strategy is deep down they think, well, maybe these guys will crack the problem and, you know, maybe we'll end up living not forever, but maybe a much longer time." — Sir Venki Ramakrishnan
  • > "I think they have that bias whether it's conscious or not. They do have the bias of looking at the entire world as if it's a software problem... and the world is not digital the world is analog and I think they simply don't want to want to confront that." — Sir Venki Ramakrishnan
  • > "Everything is not about living longer. It's about enjoying life while you have it." — Sir Venki Ramakrishnan
Hi everybody and welcome to In Good Company. I'm Nicolola Tangan, the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. Now longevity is one of the hottest topics in the world right now. Billionaires are pouring money into it. You know, when they were young, they wanted to become rich and when they are rich, they want to become young. The wellness industry is selling it and opinions about how to live longer are everywhere. So we wanted to find out what is real, what is hype and how close are we to living forever. To help us answer this, I'm joined by Sir Venki Ramakrishnan, the Nobel winner in chemistry, former president of the Royal Society and author of the incredible book, Why We Die. Venki, a warm welcome. Thank you. You start your book with the pharaohs of Egypt who believed they could transcend death. Why did you begin there? Well, the pharaohs are an interesting story because humans try to avoid death by a variety of strategies. Plan A is simply to try not to die. Plan B is to try to believe that even if you die, your whole body will be resurrected and you will go to some paradise. And Plan C is that maybe your body will decay but you will have an immortal soul that will, you know, outlast you and you can occupy other bodies and so on. So now, many thousand years later, how close are we to living forever? I think there's no physical or chemical law saying that our lifespan has to be what it is today. I mean, if you look at it today, we can expect at the most to live to be about 110 to maybe 120 years. Only one person has exceeded 120 years. But saying there's no physical law doesn't mean anything because, you know, there's no physical law that says we can't eventually colonize other galaxies. But if you look at all of the difficulties involved even in, you know, going to Mars, you realize that it's incredibly hard. And so I put this long life extension, hundreds of years, in that category, you know, which is highly unrealistic today, despite what you may hear from various people promoting hype. Before we kind of dig deeper, how do you define aging? Aging is, I would say, the gradual loss of function of our systems. That is from our molecules, cells, tissues to the entire body. It gradually loses function due to accumulation of damage and changes with time. And that's how I would define aging. Now, it has external manifestations. You can't walk as fast, you are not as strong, you are more susceptible to infections, you know, so there are many external manifestations, but underneath it are these accumulated damages to our cells, tissues, and ultimately it comes from our molecules. Well, you are 74. I can't see so many external manifestations in you. Well, I am lucky because I have dark skin in a northern climate. So that gives a superficial, you know, illusion. But if you were to look at the inside of my body, you would find it's quite old. Well, we should talk about that later. But it seems like two ideas are getting a bit blurred, right? So extending life means slowing the aging process and then rejuvenating cells means making old cells young again. So what is the difference between the two? Okay, so most anti-aging strategies have to do with preventing damage or slowing down the damage and dysfunction. But one class of strategies involves trying to get cells to go backward in development. The way to think about it is that a fertilized egg can develop into every kind of tissue in the body. That's what it does, right? If you look at the early embryo, it has many cells, but each of those cells could become any type of tissue. Those are called pluripotent stem cells. But as the embryo develops, the stem cells become specialized. So some cells can only make cells of the blood system, others can only make cells of the nervous system and so on. This process normally never goes backwards, except in real life it does. For example, a child born of old parents, like 30-year-old parents, it starts the clock from zero, right? In fact, the child born of a 40-year-old woman is not older than the child born of a 20-year-old woman. So, at some point, there is this resetting. The resetting is not completely perfect, but because there's a lot of selection involved in birth, any cells that are defective simply don't make it to a full-grown child. So that's the process that people are trying to reverse. The first proof was when John Gurdon took a skin cell from a frog, took the nucleus and put it into an egg, and could grow a completely brand new frog that was a clone of the original frog. This showed that you could actually reset the clock in a skin cell and make it develop into a whole new frog again. And then Yamanaka showed that only introducing four genes into some cells can make them go backwards all the way to that early form of a stem cell. And that's the logic people are trying to use. Now, of course, you don't want to make all your organs go backwards all the way back to pluripotent stem cells because that would be a big, confused mess and you would get tumors and so on. But people are asking, can you make this program go backwards just a little bit so that the cells still maintain their identity? The skin cell stays a skin cell, the muscle cell stays a muscle cell, or the liver cell stays a liver cell, but it has gone slightly backwards in development. You can think of it as a way of trying to reverse the aging clock. That is an exciting area, but making it work in humans in a safe and effective way, it's not clear how long it will take. What is actually going on inside our cells as we get older? Well, many things happen. One is that our molecules get damaged, and a primary source of damage is if you damage your DNA so that it is problematic. It results in two things. One is the cell can sense the damage and it can send the cell into a program called senescence where it doesn't function normally and in fact secretes inflammatory compounds. In early life, this is a cancer prevention mechanism because if you have damaged DNA, you don't want that cell to hang around because it may mutate into a cancer cell, right? So this is a mechanism to get rid of these cells where damage is sensed, but if the damage persists, it can also alter the genes that are part of the genetic program, and that can also cause dysfunction. Now, this kind of damage then results in the cell itself not regulating itself properly. The organelles in our cell called mitochondria, which have their own DNA but also interact with the rest of the cell, they also can get damaged quite a lot. So you can see these kinds of damages occur due to all kinds of things. Exposure to chemicals—even water alone—can cause DNA damage, which was discovered by Tomas Lindahl for which he won the Nobel Prize. So just the act of living causes damage, but we have sophisticated repair mechanisms that constantly repair the damage, but at some point the damage starts accumulating with age. Those mechanisms are never perfect. Talking of which, a century ago most people died when they were 50 or before, and today the average is roughly 80. You mentioned one person has made it to above 120. So why has that ceiling not moved? There is a difference between the average and the ceiling. Life expectancy has doubled in the last 150 years, but that's mostly due to improvements early in life. For example, infant mortality has gone down, many infectious diseases can now be cured, and accident rates have been reduced dramatically. So all of those things mean that we can live to an older age. So the average has gone up. But even in the 1500s, Michelangelo lived to be almost 90. So it's not that nobody lived to an old age in the olden times; it's just the average was low. We solved it by public health, vaccination, nutrition, and medicine. Those are the four big things. But as you get older, then aging starts to kick in, and that we've not actually made a lot of progress in. The maximum lifespan has not actually changed a lot. It's changed a little bit because people do live longer and so more of them make it to over 100. For example, the number of people over 100 is increasing everywhere. I think the Prime Minister typically in the past sent letters to everybody turning 100 and then suddenly it became just too much work. Right. In Japan, they produce more diapers for old people than for babies. Yes. That's a whole other problem. What is happening is society is getting older, but fertility rates are going down. So society is becoming skewed towards an older population. But how do we crack this 120-year barrier? What do you think? Will we crack it? I think if you slow down aging, it's unlikely that you'll get huge gains. What you will get is more and more people reaching, say, 100 or beyond. I think the way that people might be able to crack 120 is by this kind of reprogramming, which is trying to get cells to essentially reverse their biological age by resetting some of these clocks. But although it's been demonstrated in animals, there are real problems getting it to work in humans. Why? Because the way that they introduce it in animals is either they have transgenic mice which turn these genes on or off. By the way, we don't know if these genes are really safe over the long run; some of them are oncogenes, for example, which can cause cancer. The other way to do it is by packaging these genes inside a viral shell—these are called adenoviral vectors—and then you introduce them. The problem is that these genes have to go into all of the cells in the tissues of interest and do it sort of uniformly. All the evidence says that, for example, when they target a pancreas, only some clusters of cells in the pancreas are reprogrammed and other cells are not. In mice, this still causes some benefit, but you can see if you want to do it in humans, you have to be able to do it in a much better controlled and safe way. I don't think it's around the corner as far as humans are concerned. And we also don't know—for example, in mice, it didn't actually increase the longevity of the mice. These reprogrammed mice just looked healthier by various criteria than younger mice, but it didn't increase their lifespan. Different organs age at different rates. What organs age the quickest? I think it would vary quite a lot depending on the person. You could imagine, for example, if somebody's a heavy smoker, maybe their lungs are aging faster; or if somebody's a heavy drinker, maybe their liver ages faster. I just don't know. One of the more interesting papers on it simply showed that if you apply most standard markers, then different organs in people had different biological ages. Talking of these markers, these hallmarks of aging, what are the most important ones? I think you typically talk about 12 of them. I don't think you can say one is more important than the other. As a molecular biologist, I think modification to DNA is fundamental and really drives a lot of the rest. However, they're all important in their own frame. For example, damage to mitochondria is a thing in itself, and loss of stem cells is a factor in itself. Of course, you could say underlying it all, maybe the primary cause is damage to our DNA and the response to that DNA damage. How is AI changing the pace of discovery? I think AI is very, very good at recognizing patterns in large data. For example, in my field of structural biology, it has really revolutionized the ability to predict structures just from the sequence that you can get from sequencing the gene. But with aging, it's a complex, multifactorial process. It's not just one thing. There are all sorts of things that are interconnected, and how to make sense of that is not clear, and what sort of data you would feed to AI to train it is also not clear. However, what I can say is that AI has been advancing so dramatically that it's hard to predict anything about it. What are the kind of things you can do now in your lab which you couldn't do? Well, I mentioned you could predict structures. Another thing you could use it for is for looking for patterns in genes. For example, if you sequenced lots of centenarians, you could then ask AI to look at the genomes and ask, "Does any pattern emerge here?" Demis Hassabis, who won the Nobel Prize for AlphaFold, he predicts that within 10 to 15 years there won't be any illnesses left. Any what? That there won't be any illnesses left. Yes, I know Demis. I actually know him quite well, and in fact he asked me to be on the advisory board of one of his companies, Isomorphic Labs. I think he's a brilliant guy. However, I would say in this case, maybe he's being a bit overoptimistic. The reason I say that is not because AI won't give us clues about potential drugs or even causes of diseases, but I think going from there to having treatments is a complicated process. It doesn't happen in the digital world; it happens in real people. You have to have real medicines which you make. So all of that stuff is in the analog world, and I think that is going to take longer. Do the tech people just think that life is a software that can be hacked? Yes, I think they have that bias, whether it's conscious or not. They look at the entire world as if it's a software problem. The world is not digital, the world is analog, and I think they simply don't want to confront that. Why is there suddenly so much money going into longevity research, like tens and tens of billions? I think there are two reasons. One is the one you pointed out about Japan, which is true of all societies. All societies are getting older, and governments and health agencies are extremely worried. How do we deal with societies where a huge fraction of the population is quite old and there's a smaller and smaller fraction of the population that's of working age that can support them? So the solution is to put money into aging research. The goal of this is not what you described earlier of living for a very long time, but rather to make life as healthy as possible—increasing healthspan rather than lifespan. That's a very widespread reason; everybody will sign up to it. But the other reason is that there is a group of people who just have these grandiose visions. It's all based on the fact that they made billions of dollars in their 20s before they were mature, and they simply think that everything is just going to go their way. These people don't want to die. I mean, why don't they want to die? Well, they love their lives, they like the feeling of control, they think, "Oh, we solved the problem of payment transactions, so we should be able to solve death." Why are they all men? I do think that they're all middle-aged men, often married to younger women, by the way, which is a strong incentive to want to live longer. I think it's some male thing, you know, of wanting control and so on. You're right, and I do point out in the book that they're mostly middle-aged men. Tell me a bit more about these men. What else do they have in common? They like control. They like power. They like wealth, and they're used to having everything their way. If they want to buy an island, they can buy an island. If they want to buy off a government, sure, they can fund some politician's campaign and then suddenly the regulations disappear. I'm being a bit cynical, but you can see this in operation in the US today. I think they're used to that kind of power. They also have these grandiose visions; they feel like we're the only intelligent species in the universe and therefore we need to populate the universe. There is a fantastic book by Adam Becker called *More Everything Forever* about these people who just want more of everything. I don't know if they really mean it or if they're saying that so it sounds like a noble cause, but the real reason is they simply want more power and they don't want to die. Now, one of these Bezos-backed ventures is called Altos Labs, right? Five billion dollars, a whole bunch of Nobel winners. I should say that one of the driving forces behind it was actually Yuri Milner, another tech billionaire. What are they betting on? Officially, their stance is that they're not interested in extending life, but they want to extend health. Rick Klausner, their chief scientist, said, "Look, our goal is not for people to live forever. Our goal is for everybody to die young after a long time." My immediate reaction was, if somebody is young, why would they suddenly die? It's a bit of a paradox. I think what you'll end up doing is postpone that slow decay and decline to a later stage in life. But anyway, that's their stated goal. I have a feeling the people who are funding it are interested in two things. One, they think there's a lot of money in aging, so if you hire the best scientists—which they did, some of the top scientists in the field went to Altos because of the resources and the salary—they think that if they hire some top scientists, they will come up with useful stuff that then we can monetize in the longevity business. That's a standard investment strategy. But the other strategy is deep down they think, "Maybe these guys will crack the problem and maybe we'll end up living not forever, but maybe a much longer time." We have a few other players who tried to crack it: Kico, Nu-Liit, Bryan Johnson. What are your reflections? I do know of Bryan Johnson, who is another tech billionaire who spends $2 million a year apparently on his own longevity. He monitors his aging, he does all sorts of treatments, and so on. He seems a pleasant enough guy from watching his interviews, and I think it's fine if he does that. He looks pretty young for a guy in his late 40s, but my son is almost 50 and he looks just as young without any of those longevity treatments. The trouble with Bryan Johnson is there is no control experiment. It's one guy who's mixing up all sorts of different things, and how do you judge whether something is working or not in one person when you mix everything up? Let's move tasks. Stem cells kind of deserve their own chapter here. You say in your book that an 80-year-old has two-hundredths the amount of stem cells compared to a newborn. What are the genuine breakthroughs in this area? The big breakthrough in stem cell research will come from reprogramming. If they can implement reprogramming safely in humans and demonstrate it—first of all, they need to do a lot more research on animals. It has worked in mice, right? It has worked in mice, but remember it's a very limited experiment, and even in mice the reprogramming is highly heterogeneous. There's one interesting experiment being done in Boston about trying to regenerate eye tissue by injecting these factors directly into the eye. Maybe this can restore damaged or aging tissue, as many diseases in old age cause retinal degeneration. That has been approved for clinical trials. But again, many things that work in mice fail in clinical trials. That's almost the norm. You have to wait and see what happens. Hundreds of clinics already sell stem cell injections. When scientists found out that if you connected an old and young rat and exchanged their blood supply, the old animal seemed to benefit from the blood of the young animal, immediately there were companies starting to sell young blood at a huge markup to rich old men. Do you think there is something to it, or is it just...? I am highly skeptical. Another area is cryopreservation. Basically, you freeze yourself and hopefully you wake up later. This is currently in the realm of science fiction. It's not because cryopreservation itself has no basis—we can freeze eggs, embryos, and even small larvae of worms. The method of freezing depends on cooling to very low temperatures without the water freezing into ice. Ice expands, so it destroys all the tissue around it. That's why frozen strawberries don't look like fresh ones. Nobody has been able to freeze even a small animal like a mouse. I would say to these people: when you can freeze a mouse and thaw it so that it can run around again, then come and talk to me. Until then, I think of it as nonsense. In the meantime, if we gave you $3 billion to conduct research, where would you put that money? I would put it into three or four areas. One is that we know caloric restriction helps with aging. It's been true in many species. It has some consequences which are not always good, but maybe you can separate those. So, I would put money into caloric restriction pathways. That to me is the most promising short-term goal. Then, we talked about old and young blood. One possibility is to ask what is in old blood and what's in young blood—what are the differences? Third, cells that sense damage and go into senescence. As we get older, the number of senescent cells increases too much beyond our ability to clear them, so there are efforts to target these cells for destruction. And the fourth is cellular reprogramming. I think that is perhaps one of the more exciting long-term goals. In the meantime, we just want some more healthy years. Where do we start? Well, caloric restriction suggests an obvious answer: don't overeat. So, this is basically just being hungry? I don't think you need to be hungry. People on a truly calorie-restricted diet are hungry all the time, which is why I don't do it, nor do I. But you can eat moderately and control your weight. GLP-1 drugs have now shown all kinds of effects, not just for preventing diabetes or for extreme obesity. They could be investigated further. Do you think they will extend lifetime? They might make old age healthier. But they have consequences. For example, you have muscle loss and other side effects, so people need to figure out how to use them safely. If you're going to give it to healthy people, you have to make it safer. But caloric restriction suggests an obvious answer: don't overeat. It works because it turns on pathways that are involved in recycling defective molecules and organelles, and it affects protein synthesis so you don't make misfolded proteins. The other thing is sleep, because a lot of recycling and repair happens in our sleep cycle. People underestimate the importance of sleep. The third one is exercise; it actually stimulates the rejuvenation and regeneration of tissues. How much do you sleep? I try to get about eight hours of sleep a day. If I get less than seven, I don't function very well. There is this saying: when you're young, you sneak out of your bedroom to go to parties, and when you get older, you sneak out of the parties to get back to bed. Why do we appreciate sleep more when we get older? I don't know. Maybe we're just not as energetic and don't have the same stamina as when we were young. I'm not sure we're all night party animals. Sleep is beautiful. How much do you exercise? I ride my bicycle about a few kilometers each way to work every day. Apart from that, I go to the gym and do a combination of weight training and cardio. My knee is not great; I used to be a runner, but now I mostly do an elliptical cross-trainer, which mimics cross-country skiing. It's much better for the joints. What about ice baths, cold plunges, and saunas? The trouble with all these things is they come from one or two observations which may or may not be validated. There's no control experiment. I would be very skeptical of those treatments. I doubt that they do much, to be honest. It does a lot for me. I did both an ice bath and sauna this morning and it makes me feel great. If it makes you feel good, that's probably okay—probably good for you at some level. A lot of aging is related to stress. People who are happy probably have less stress and less damage to their systems. I wouldn't say "go out and get drunk every day," but if a cold plunge makes you feel good, that's great. You should do it. Let's move to the ethics. If we could double the lifespan tomorrow, do we actually want to? I think it would cause huge changes in society, not all of which would be good. For one thing, changes in society require turnovers of generations. Older generations don't voluntarily change society; change is often driven by the young. You might end up with a very stagnant society. As people get older, they accumulate wealth, influence, and power. You will have the same group of people controlling society without turnover, which is not a recipe for a good society. People who are entrenched in power have no reason to change and may not act in the best interests of everyone. I should point out that great discoveries in science and mathematics are almost all done by young people—people in their 40s or younger. Even novelists often write their greatest works when they are younger. I wonder what it will do to society generally if we have everybody living for a very long time, especially combined with a drop in fertility rates where you're not replacing them with young people. During COVID, we treated the old people first. Was that wrong? It wasn't wrong because they were by far at the greatest risk. The likelihood of dying from COVID doubled every eight years of life. If you wanted to save lives, that was not a bad thing to do. The rich people already live a decade longer than the poor. What reflections do you have around that? It creates a serious problem. If you have advances in aging research and treatments that are highly sophisticated and expensive, you can imagine a two-tier society where rich people get all the fancy treatments and live even longer. The disparity in power will be even greater, and their children will also be at an advantage. You may end up creating a multi-tiered society where there is one rule for the very rich and another situation for the rest of society, especially the poor. Let's talk about you. You grew up in India, trained as a physicist, and then switched to biology. Why is physics a good place to start? I'm not sure it's necessary, but it didn't hurt. Physics trains you in mathematics and quantitative thinking. But I know physicists who try to become biologists without really becoming biologists—staying as physicists—and they don't tend to do very well. The best people who have gone from physics to biology are those who stopped being physicists and really learned how to think like biologists. Biology involves a different scale and messy problems where you have to do control experiments. Physics is generally a highly reductionist science. You have to bridge those cultural differences if you want to succeed. Do you think with the advent of AI that having a broad curriculum is more or less an advantage? Having a broad curriculum is always an advantage. One worry I have is that we are going to simply delegate everything to AI, which would be a recipe for making humanity stupider and more ignorant. We should be leveraging AI as another powerful tool in our armory. What did you learn as president at the Royal Society? I learned how to convey science to the general public and to the government. I became president at a particular moment in British history when it had voted to leave the EU, and towards the end of my term we had the global pandemic. I had to deal with serious issues. Being an American citizen who had come to Britain and didn't have a network of people, I thought it might have been a disadvantage, but it made me viewed as someone with no axe to grind—an outsider, but therefore objective. Why is music so important in your life? I've always enjoyed music. It's one of those universal things. I think music is a demonstration of very high cognitive ability. We have evolved so that music has deep emotional and physiological triggers in us. You can change a person's mood with music. Do you think it makes you live longer? I don't know, but it can certainly make the life you have far more pleasurable. Some things you do because they're worth doing; not everything is about living longer. It's about enjoying life while you have it. Are you afraid of dying? I think we all are at some level. My father and my brother-in-law both died in the last year and a half. My father was almost 99; when he was told he only had a few days left, he was a little bit sad, but he took it reasonably well. My brother-in-law had a sudden diagnosis of pancreatic cancer and was very rational about it. I hope when my time comes that I'll face it with the same equanimity and rationality. You had a Freudian slip there and said "if you die." Do you believe in the afterlife? No, I don't. I meant when I die. It's been tremendous to speak with you. What an incredible experience. A big thank you for taking the time. Thank you very much for having me.

BIG BET: Cathie Wood goes ALL IN on Elon Musk

Fox Business(3.4M)·12:13·2026-07-22
Cathie WoodCEO, ARK Invest
TL;DR- **TL;DR:** ARK Invest CEO Cathie Wood and guest experts on *Mornings with Maria* discuss how AI, aerospace, and autonomous defense technologies are driving a once-in-a-generation productivity boom and reshaping global competition with China.
Wood argues that SpaceX—leveraging a...
  • TL;DR: ARK Invest CEO Cathie Wood and guest experts on *Mornings with Maria* discuss how AI, aerospace, and autonomous defense technologies are driving a once-in-a-generation productivity boom and reshaping global competition with China.
  • Wood argues that SpaceX—leveraging a 10-year lead in reusable rockets, a 70% share of satellites, and upcoming orbital data centers—is positioned to become the most important company in global history.

AI Infrastructure, Productivity, and the U.S.–China Race

  • Macroeconomic Productivity:
    • Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent notes that the current AI build-up is a once-in-a-generation driver of natural wealth through productivity growth.
    • The administration supports open-source models but warns against IP theft, retaining the ability to sanction overseas models caught stealing from U.S. companies.
  • Assessing Chinese AI Competitors:
    • Cathie Wood views the competition positively, noting that America is at its best when competing.
    • DeepSeek & Communiqué Analysis: ARK's research indicates that Chinese models are less expensive upfront but much less efficient, relying on "throwing power at the AI equation."
  • Wall Street AI Adoption (BNY Mellon):
    • BNY CEO Robin Vince reports that the bank built its own platform named Eliza (after Alexander Hamilton's spouse) with internal agent builders.
    • The bank has conducted 10 to 40 hours of training per employee and currently has 200 solutions in production.

SpaceX: Dominating Aerospace, Telecom, and Data

  • Market Valuation and Holdings:
    • ARK Invest manages $30 billion in total assets.
    • SpaceX is trading below its peak (down 32% in the past month), with $116 billion in shares set to unlock following the lifting of IPO restrictions.
    • Wood’s firm has increased exposure by $80 million since the IPO.
  • Competitive Advantage:
    • Launch Capabilities & Reusability: SpaceX holds a 10-year lead, anchored by its pioneering work in landing and relaunching rockets. While Blue Origin successfully landed a rocket late last year, it did not successfully relaunch it into orbit in the desired manner.
    • Global Telecom Disruption: Telecom has historically been a local business requiring the purchase of domestic telcos. SpaceX bypasses this with a global network, holding 70% of all satellites.
    • Orbital Data Centers: SpaceX is building global data centers in orbit and currently rents out terrestrial data centers to companies like Anthropic and Google.
    • Long-Term Strategy: While making short-term deals with terrestrial telcos (like AT&T), SpaceX ultimately plans to go direct-to-business globally at lower costs.

AI Disruption in Healthcare and Corporate America

  • Corporate Productivity & Talent:
    • Companies are heavily investing in AI for internal and customer-facing workflows.
    • ARK leverages tools like Palantir and actively recruits young, AI-native talent, viewing the technology as a fundamental mindset shift.
  • Healthcare Transformation:
    • Wood highlights healthcare as the most profound application of AI due to the convergence of three technologies: AI drug discovery (collapsing time and cost), predictive diagnostics (identifying diseases before symptoms appear via sequencing), and CRISPR gene editing.

Defense Technology and Manufacturing Leapfrogging

  • Autonomous Maritime and Defense (Ceronix):
    • Ceronix CEO Dino argues that the U.S. does not need to beat China by building traditional 1,200-foot cargo containers or matching legacy shipyards (Port Alpha).
    • Instead, the U.S. will win by out-producing China through autonomous vessels, software platforms, efficient new shipyards, and asymmetric capabilities controlled by software.
  • Leapfrogging Old Technology:
    • Wood compares current manufacturing shifts to how China previously leaped over U.S. infrastructure—adopting wireless directly instead of building landlines, and digital wallets instead of brick-and-mortar banking.
    • The U.S. can similarly leapfrog legacy industrial approaches through autonomous, software-driven manufacturing.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • SpaceX's Historical Trajectory: Cathie Wood predicts that SpaceX could become "the most important company in global history" by combining launch capabilities, global communications, the robotaxi opportunity, orbital data centers, and frontier models (including xAI/Optimist).
  • Multi-Trillion Dollar Opportunities: Cathie Wood predicts that the convergence of robotics, AI models, and space infrastructure will create "multi-trillion dollar opportunities."

China's moonshot is in talks at $50 billion valuation; how do you assess the situation? Cathie: We think that this is good for America—the competition. We are at our best when we are competing. We take a close look at DeepSeek and Qwen, the newest one, and what we are finding is it sounds like they are much less expensive in terms of models but also much less efficient. What is your take on SpaceX since it is down 32% in the last month? Cathie: From its peak it is, not the IPO price. We think this could become the most important company in history, and I mean in global history, because we are talking about not only exploring a new world—the universe—in terms of its launch capabilities and helping others to do so as well, but also a global communications network. This is a global business, and SpaceX is the first mover with 70% of the satellites. And beyond that, we have the global data centers, orbital data centers, so they will be the most economic and will allow Elon and team the opportunity to develop, we think, some of the most sophisticated frontier models in the world at the lowest cost. The secret to scaling technologies is falling costs as units increase. In space, SpaceX has a first mover advantage. It will be difficult; it has a ten-year lead, and the key has been reusable rockets. Ten years ago, it landed its first rocket and sent it up into orbit. No other company has been able to do that successfully; even Blue Origin late last year did land, so congratulations, we are thrilled—we want many players—but it did not relaunch into the orbit it wanted successfully. It did relaunch, though. Maria: So many companies are integrating AI across their internal and customer-facing operations. We spoke with the CEO of BNY, Robin Vince, as he is changing how his bank operates with AI. Watch this. Robin: It is a super-powerful technology. The question is key because the technology is already at such a level that all of us in our individual lives and corporations, the struggle is: how do you get it more inventive so that it is more part of every piece of workflow that we have inside a company? We built our own platform called Eliza, back to our history, Alexander Hamilton's spouse, and we have agent builders in our company. We trained everybody; we are doing 10, 20, 30, 40 hours of training helping people to adopt AI. We've got 200 solutions in production, and we really believe AI can be a superpower for the company and a superpower for the people in the company. Maria: I want your take on AI and how you invest around it. You've said that you think AI is going to be the biggest investment opportunity of our lifetime. What industries do you think AI will disrupt first, and how are you investing around this theme of artificial intelligence? Cathie: The first thing companies are doing is investing in productivity coming out of AI. This, from an active equity management point of view, is important. We are talking to companies; we want to understand how they are deploying AI. We are using Palantir; we are ironically bringing in more talent, very young talent that is AI native, and I do agree that this is a mindset shift. It is very important for every company to take this seriously. But from a higher-level, bigger-picture disruption, yes. If any company clings to the old ways of doing things—healthcare, you featured healthcare on your show, but we think healthcare is the most profound application of AI from a drug discovery point of view, collapsing the time and cost; from a diagnostic point of view, identifying and diagnosing diseases maybe even before we see any symptoms, as sequencing technologies and AI come together and adding in CRISPR gene editing, we are seeing cures for disease. The convergence of those three technologies is going to transform healthcare. Maria: We have been doing a deep dive on defense as well. One other industry seeing a fundamental change is defense technology, how we outpace China, the White House considering further restrictions reportedly on companies using AI models from Beijing. The CEO of Saronic was on; here's what he said. Dino: We don't have to beat China in the traditional sense of let's go out and build what they are building. We are going to beat China in a new way, outproduce China by building autonomous vessels, building the future of the maritime industry, building software platforms, new shipyards that are more efficient than the shipyards the Chinese have, and deterring asymmetric capability—ships that can be built cheaper and at higher rates controlled by software. We don't need to build 1200-foot cargo containers. We can and will be able to, sure, but that is not the deterrence that our military is looking for, and this is what we are going to deliver. Maria: Where do you see the biggest opportunities in defense tech right now, and are you worried about all this money being plowed into AI? Cathie: The government is funding a lot of this, and what will be at the end of the day? I agree completely; it is a bigger point the CEO is making. This is true of all manufacturing, so we are going to do what China did to us with telecom, with telecom in particular. They went directly to wireless. They didn't need to build out their land lines; they did the same with digital wallets, not brick and mortar banking system. Meanwhile, they took our manufacturing sector, what they did to us with those two industries. And we leapfrog China because it has old technology now. Maria: What is your favorite stock? Cathie: Ultimately SpaceX, when they combine—the most powerful—the robo-taxi opportunity, the orbital data center opportunity, and many people are underestimating not only Optimus, which is an AI play, but what used to be xAI, that is the frontier model. There are lots of opportunities, and they are multi-trillion dollar opportunities. That is surprising people, the annualized run rate, 47 billion in six months.

Sovereign AI: Piece by Piece | NetApp, Radiant, Hydra Host, Coatue & More | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·41:35·80 views·2026-07-22
George KurianCEO, NetApp Mahdi YahyaRadiant Aaron GinnHydra Host
TL;DRSovereign AI is emerging as a critical, multi-layered geopolitical trend where nations and enterprises seek independent control over their AI infrastructure, data, and operations.
While the U.S. and China are building dominant full-stack ecosystems, other regions like Europe,...

TL;DR

Sovereign AI is emerging as a critical, multi-layered geopolitical trend where nations and enterprises seek independent control over their AI infrastructure, data, and operations. While the U.S. and China are building dominant full-stack ecosystems, other regions like Europe, Latin America, and Asia are navigating complex trade-offs between local control, cost, and the realities of global supply chain dependencies.

Definitions & Core Concepts of Sovereign AI

  • National and Organizational Control: George Kurian (NetApp) defines Sovereign AI as the ability of a nation-state or organization to independently control the development, deployment, and governance of AI activities, encompassing technology, data, operations, and talent.
  • A Continuous Spectrum: Mahdi Yahya (Radiant) emphasizes that sovereignty is not a binary end state, but a continuous spectrum where organizations constantly make decisions on how much control to exert and how much risk to divest.
  • Data Residency and Physical Infrastructure: Aaron Ginn (Hydra Host) argues that sovereignty starts with data residency, physical data center infrastructure, and local retention of chips and capital rather than relying on foreign hyperscalers.
  • Critical Infrastructure & Utility: Ben Germin (Polarise) frames AI tokens as a critical utility comparable to gas, water, and electricity, making it vital for Europe to avoid dependency on hyperscalers and the U.S. Cloud Act.
  • Data Ownership & Open Models: Max Cook (Coatue) highlights that enterprise sovereignty also means owning proprietary data and managing whether open versus closed models are utilized.

The AI Technology Stack and Supply Chain Realities

  • Control Planes as Critical Control Points: Mahdi Yahya states that controlling the management plane above and below physical infrastructure is the most critical element of the Sovereign AI stack.
  • The Power Bottleneck: Max Cook points out that upstream equipment bottlenecks—such as gas turbines used for power generation (dominated by just three global suppliers, one American)—heavily dictate where infrastructure can be built, with much equipment still flowing to the U.S.
  • Data Control as a Durable Moat: George Kurian notes that NetApp works to give clients complete control to deploy data anywhere—whether hyperscaler, sovereign environment, or on-premises data center.
  • Decentralized OS and Global Deployments: Aaron Ginn shares that Hydra Host provides data center-level OS layers for sovereign clients, telecom firms, and energy companies, enabling them to run NVIDIA hardware without in-house expertise across diverse locations like El Salvador and Panama.
  • The Impact of Export Controls: George Kurian notes that U.S. export controls have spurred innovation, forcing China to develop diffusion-based models and encouraging global competition.

Hyperscalers vs. Independent Infrastructure

  • The Infeasibility of Hyperscalers for True Sovereignty: Ben Germin offers a clear "no" on achieving true sovereignty via hyperscalers, citing Microsoft France's legal testimony before the French Senate that it cannot guarantee data stays within France.
  • The Three Phases of Government Action: Mahdi Yahya outlines that governments move through *rhetoric, policy,* and finally *execution*, noting that most governments have not yet reached the execution phase.
  • Commercial Risk vs. National Security: George Kurian distinguishes between commercial organizations prioritizing intellectual property protection and risk, and governments balancing national security with the need to foster local ecosystems and innovation.

The Economic Cost and Inelasticity of Sovereign AI

  • Financial Duplication: Max Cook and George Kurian note that building sovereign AI requires duplicating data center infrastructure and procuring more semiconductors, which inherently increases costs.
  • Token Price Deflation and Margins: Ben Germin notes that while building AI factories in Munich and Oslo is expensive, token prices are deflationary, leaving sufficient margin across different layers to remain profitable and competitive.
  • Inelastic Demand for Intelligence: Aaron Ginn argues that Sovereign AI is economically inelastic because intelligence is unbounded and governments view *not* having it as a greater risk than any financial cost.

Regional Progress and Global Execution

  • Latin America & El Salvador: Aaron Ginn highlights projects with the President of El Salvador, building Latin America's first black hole data center deployment, alongside massive power capacity in Paraguay (3 gigawatts).
  • France & Europe: Mahdi Yahya notes Brookfield Asset Management commitments, including $30 billion in France and investments in Sweden. Ben Germin details a 10-megawatt AI industry cloud deployment in Germany with Telekom that expanded Germany's AI compute by 50%.
  • Asia: Aaron Ginn notes that China's top models grew from 10% to over 50% of the global top tier, creating an independent eastern ecosystem.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Silicon Independence Timeline: George Kurian predicts that it is "inconceivable" that silicon competitive with U.S. silicon will emerge within the next 5 years.
  • Global Ecosystem Bifurcation (5-Year Outlook): George Kurian predicts that in 5 years, the U.S. will maintain the full stack (chips to apps), while China will establish a robust alternative ecosystem anchored by open-source models and a coalition of adopting nations.
  • Airline Industry Parallel: Aaron Ginn predicts that GPUs and models will ultimately mirror the commercial airline industry, where a couple of dominant global players (like Airbus and Boeing) exist alongside numerous local and regional carrier providers reflecting local resources.
  • Persistent European Dependency: Ben Germin predicts that in 5 years, Europe will still remain heavily dependent on hyperscalers for infrastructure, foreign chips, and foreign foundation models, despite the rise of local star initiatives.

Notable Quotes

"Governments today... go through three phases, there's rhetoric, then there is policy and then there is execution. I don't think we got to the execution phase yet, in any of the different governments." — Mahdi Yahya
"GPUs are regulated on the US government as if it is a missile defense technology... Once the government's decide that something is regulated that way, it does it generally doesn't go the other way." — Aaron Ginn
"We have to come with a gun to the gunfight in the end, not with a knife." — Ben Germin
"Constraints usually create innovation... It fosters different ecosystems. It brings lower cost models into play. It creates alternate chip designs and all of those things." — George Kurian

And as we're going through this shift, it's increasingly recognized that the implications go far beyond technology and affect everything we do, and in particular, relationships between states. We've seen in the recent past, you know, basically government curtailing access to frontier models. My view of Sovereign AI is that it is a result of both the geopolitical landscape as well as people's recognition, a government's recognition of the importance of AI. I think it is, ultimately, the need for either a national government, a nation state, or an organization to be able to independently control the development, the deployment, and the governance of their AI-related activities, and that could include technology, various elements of the technology stack. Only 10 megawatts, so that's the scale of what we are talking about in Europe; it's a giant leap for Germany, but small steps. So expensive there, yes, very expensive. Cool. We're going to run out of time. The last one was about, think about it, five years from now, we assume that this theme of Sovereign AI is really keeping on being increasingly important. How do you see the stack evolving because of this? How is the stack different because of Sovereign AI? And maybe George, I ask you to start on that. I think five years is a long time in this industry, but in general I think the US will still have the full stack—chips through models, data, and apps—and of course operating capabilities. My own view is China will create the other end of the ecosystem, which is, as Aaron mentioned, they have a huge amount of the open source models, and I think they will build a coalition of other nations that want to use those models and contribute to that ecosystem. They have some chips today and they have a huge amount of data that the government can marshal, as well as they have big, huge consumer platforms. So they will be viable. For the rest of the nations, it's my own view that it would benefit them to be incredibly practical about what they want to get accomplished; they probably need to lean on one of these two pillars, borrow certain capabilities from them, innovate, and foster the local ecosystem. Mahdi? I think the concept of sovereignty exists for everyone, except for the US and China. There's no conversation about Sovereign AI there because they own that. So for anyone else, everyone needs to think about the control within that ecosystem. As George is mentioning, the US owns the stack, China has a stack, so for anyone who wants to achieve sovereignty within that stack it's all about how you are going to basically build controls. I agree that I think there's two predominantly ecosystems being developed. I think chips will be everywhere, models will be everywhere; I think this is going to look a lot like the way the airlines industry works. In that use case there's Airbus and Boeing, and then there's some random players. But these are the core two, and I think that will just be prolific. Every country is going to have some resemblance of their own carrier providers and they'll be reflective of their own local resources. There'll be ones that have localized winners—like France is a winner in Europe, British Airways is a winner—but of course there's all these other little bitty winners as well. So I think it's going to look much more like airlines. That's how GPUs and models end up flushing out. Ben? AT&T CEO on Earnings, Mobile Subscriber Gains, AI

Bloomberg Television(3.2M)·11:10·2026-07-22
John StankeyCEO, AT&T
TL;DRAT&T reported a strong second quarter, adding 432,000 monthly wireless phone subscribers and a record 370,000 fiber net adds, driven by multi-year capital investments and a successful convergence strategy.
CEO John Stankey expressed confidence in sustaining this momentum through...

TL;DR

AT&T reported a strong second quarter, adding 432,000 monthly wireless phone subscribers and a record 370,000 fiber net adds, driven by multi-year capital investments and a successful convergence strategy. CEO John Stankey expressed confidence in sustaining this momentum through 2028 while highlighting the company's critical infrastructure role in the upcoming AI era.

Financial Results & Q2 Performance

  • Subscriber Growth:
    • Wireless Additions: Added 432,000 monthly wireless phone subscribers in Q2, beating analyst expectations of 325,000.
    • Fiber Net Adds: Reached a record 370,000 fiber net adds during the quarter.
  • Broadband & Mobile Convergence:
    • Convergence Rate: 42.5% of households purchasing broadband also buy mobile phone service from AT&T (rising to 45% when normalized for the recently acquired Lumen footprint).
    • Customer Benefits: Multi-product customers are more lucrative, exhibit lower churn, and show higher brand affinity.
  • Financial Metrics:
    • Growth manifested in accelerated service revenue growth and accelerated EBITDA growth.
    • Achieved growth in business strategic services for the first time in a long period.
    • Margin performance reached record levels for the company.

Market Positioning & Competitive Landscape

  • The Convergence Strategy: Providing world-class networks in both wireless and fixed broadband covers 98% of a customer's internet needs on a single bill with unified support infrastructure.
  • Pricing and Market Stratification:
    • Rejecting the "price war" characterization, Stankey described the market as stratified.
    • AT&T traditionally played well upmarket with high-performing symmetrical gigabit services and robust wireless plans.
    • Recent focus on value-oriented offerings yielded new account additions at a three-year high, improving down-market capture without sacrificing margins.
  • Competitive Moats (Urban vs. Rural):
    • Satellite services (like Starlink) and cable companies dominate less densely populated and rural areas.
    • AT&T's historical investments focus on dense urban and suburban metro areas, making it difficult for competitors to replicate hard-to-reach infrastructure (such as stadiums).

Artificial Intelligence & Network Infrastructure

  • AI ROI and Internal Efficiency:
    • Disciplined AI investments in software development, customer service channels, engineering applications, and pricing support algorithms are delivering strong returns.
    • Differentiated between table-stakes efficiencies (which get competed away into lower prices or better service) and strategic advantages (like pricing and network yield software).
  • AI Era Readiness:
    • Symmetrical networks (offering as much upstream bandwidth as downstream bandwidth) are viewed as indispensable for future AI workloads, alongside chips, memory, and data centers.

Capital Allocation & Share Repurchases

  • CapEx Cycle Contrast: Unlike tech companies currently spending heavily to raise capital and build out AI infrastructure, AT&T has largely completed its multi-year asset investment cycle.
  • Share Buybacks: The company is accelerating the pace of planned share repurchases.
  • Market Valuation: Stankey characterized the current market as experiencing a temporary "dislocation" and rotation out of AT&T stock due to capital chasing AI tech opportunities, but believes the market will eventually recognize the value of AT&T's indispensable infrastructure.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Sustained Growth: AT&T expects its current execution and growth momentum to sustain itself through 2028 — John Stankey.
  • Satellite Partnership Timeline: AT&T will be able to solve out-of-range connectivity gaps via satellite industry partnerships by this time next year — John Stankey.

And, you know, the interesting part about that is our investment has largely been in urban and densely populated suburban. We haven't pushed real hard in the rural and less densely populated areas. That tends to be more the stronghold of cable companies. I think they'll do pretty well in that space bringing an alternative and competition in there. I think it's gonna be very hard to come into the more densely urban populated areas to compete with that bundle that I just talked about earlier of fiber that is the best product in the market, best performing. It's the lowest marginal cost. Our great wireless density that far outstrips what you can do from direct-to-device and satellite. Years of investment in the hard-to-reach places like stadiums. Everybody who went to a World Cup game knows how important being able to connect in a stadium is to experience what goes on socially and actually in the arena so that you can see a few replays that they're not showing there. We've been doing that for, you know, a long, long time, and it's gonna be very, very hard to catch up on that infrastructure. And I think we're gonna do really, really well competing in metro and suburban areas, which is where our investments have been. And, John, you've talked a lot about how fiber puts you in a really good position in the AI era and how an increasing number of businesses really do need that kind of connectivity and the latency that's so important to improve.
