Fireside Chat with Sid Sheth, Founder & CEO of d-Matrix | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·17:25·7 views·2026-07-22
Sid ShethFounder & CEO, d-Matrix Tony KimExecutive, BlackRock
TL;DRSid Sheth and Tony Kim discuss how d-Matrix's purpose-built inference hardware—transitioning from SRAM-based accelerators to 3D-stacked DRAM-on-compute architectures—addresses the unique demands of the agentic AI era by optimizing for interactivity and energy efficiency.

TL;DR

Sid Sheth and Tony Kim discuss how d-Matrix's purpose-built inference hardware—transitioning from SRAM-based accelerators to 3D-stacked DRAM-on-compute architectures—addresses the unique demands of the agentic AI era by optimizing for interactivity and energy efficiency.

Company Overview & Market Positioning

  • Company Background: Founded in 2019 by Sid Sheth, d-Matrix is a chip company focused entirely on AI inference, operating in a capital-intensive sector for seven years.
  • Current Status: d-Matrix has announced that its products are officially moving into production and are ready for deployment.
  • The Inference Thesis: Analogous to human development (spending 20+ years learning vs. 40+ years applying knowledge), inference represents a vastly larger and more economically critical market opportunity than training.
  • Optimization Metrics: Unlike training (which prioritizes raw performance, data scale, and cluster throughput), inference prioritizes efficiency metrics:
    • Speed: Milliseconds per token
    • Cost: Dollars per token
    • Energy: Throughput per watt and interactivity limits

Architectural Approaches: SRAM vs. HBM

  • The X-Axis vs. Y-Axis Trade-off:
    • *Y-Axis (HBM/GPUs):* Optimized for throughput and batching multiple users, but struggles with extreme speed/interactivity.
    • *X-Axis (SRAM/d-Matrix):* Optimized for speed, low energy consumption, and high interactivity through in-memory compute (minimizing data movement).
  • The SRAM Capacity Constraint: Pure SRAM architectures (like those from Grock, Cerebras, and d-Matrix) face capacity challenges with very large models or long context lengths.
  • Heterogeneous Disaggregated Computing:
    • Workloads are split by running the compute-heavy prefill portion on HBM GPUs and memory-intensive token generation on SRAM accelerators.
    • d-Matrix announced a partnership with NVIDIA for a target customer called Parasale (an inference cloud) combining Blackwell GPUs with d-Matrix Corsair SRAM accelerators.

The Next Generation: 3D-Stacked DRAM

  • The Architectural "Endgame": To overcome SRAM capacity limits without sacrificing speed, d-Matrix is building a "skyscraper" architecture by stacking custom DRAM directly on top of SRAM compute layers, rather than placing memory to the side like HBM.
  • Scaling Layers: d-Matrix's roadmap starts with 1 layer of memory in their next-generation product, scaling to 4 layers, and eventually 8 layers (comparable to HBM stack heights).
  • Bridging the Axes: This stacked approach allows d-Matrix to maintain deep X-axis interactivity while shifting upward on the Y-axis by expanding capacity for more users and larger context lengths.
  • Market Coexistence: GPU-based HBM solutions and stacked-DRAM/SRAM solutions will coexist because frontier models trained on GPUs will continue to require GPU-level capabilities, while cost- and power-conscious deployments will utilize disaggregated architectures.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Sid Sheth: d-Matrix's production version of its 3D-stacked DRAM product will tape out and come to market in 2027, making them "amongst the first companies to release a product of that type."

Notable Quotes

"Training AI is, you know, very similar to training humans. We spent the first, you know, 20 years of our life going to, you know, school and college. But then we spend 40 plus years applying that knowledge... inference was the much larger opportunity compared to training because everyone needs to use it." — Sid Sheth
"When you're trying to do more work, it typically needs more time... And most HBM based solutions today fall on that Y-axis... On the X-axis is speed; you fundamentally need a different type of memory hierarchy to address that piece of the equation." — Sid Sheth
I was in the prior panel discussing scale up, scale out, and scale in. Why don't you introduce yourself and then we'll go into Q&A with what Dmatrix is doing. Yeah. So, hey, by the way, wonderful to be here. This is my first time coming to raise and wow, the energy is something else. At Dmatrix, I’m the founder and CEO. We are an inference chip company and we’ve been around since 2019. So, we've been around for 7 long years; it feels like a very long time in the chip business. It's a very capital-intensive business, and building chips is typically a very heavy lift. We have just announced our products going into production. What that means is our chips are now ready to be deployed. When we started the company in 2019, our emphasis was on building solutions for inference back then. I often like to joke that I used to go to investor meetings, and the first question I typically got was, "What is inference and why do you need to do it?" So, not many people really even understood the importance of inference. But we started the company with that vision. We’ve stayed the course over the last 7 years and here we are, 7 years later, building silicon. We build the subsystems that we co-package the silicon with, and we also build all the software to deploy that silicon. I’ll talk a little bit about how we take the product to market in a bit. I mean, 2019 is four years basically before Gen AI kind of hit the scene. You already had a vision for inference computing then. Obviously, the first early days was the training regime and now inference is front and center, especially with all these agents in the Gen AI workloads. How is the inference computing need different versus the training computing need, and secondly, why do we need a purpose-built specific inference processor versus a general-purpose processor? Those are great questions. So, first of all, inference is not a one-size-fits-all workload. If you really look at it, everyone needs to use inferencing. Our thesis when we started the company was around something pretty simple. I like to give this analogy—I'm going to try this out here too—which is that training AI is very similar to training humans. We spend the first 20 years of our life going to school and college, but then we spend 40 plus years applying that knowledge. Doesn't mean that the learning stops, right? We keep learning new stuff. So there's continuous learning going on, but it's not like you're spending all your time learning. You're fine-tuning your knowledge; you're spending more time applying the skills and knowledge that you learned in the first 20 plus years, and you're monetizing a lot of that. So that's the inferencing piece. Intuitively to us, inference was the much larger opportunity compared to training because everyone needs to use it and everyone needs to find a way to monetize it. When we started, we said we were going to build something that is purpose-built for inference, and it is different from what you need for training. Training is so much about performance and just quickly training bigger and bigger models with more data. You want to scale that out very quickly with big clusters and lots of compute. Inference, on the other hand, was more about efficiency. How quickly can you deploy it? How much can you do with less? How much compute can you do in the least amount of time? How much compute can you do with the least amount of dollars? How much compute can you do with the least amount of energy spent? It's really about efficiency being the big metric. You can't take a solution that was built for performance and then repurpose it and hope that it'll do extremely well on inference, because everybody uses inference differently. That is really what became the thesis around how we started. Now, of course, we have entered the agentic age, and people want to use inference for throughput, for energy constraints, or because they want throughput per watt. They want very fast inferencing, so milliseconds per token becomes very important. Cost is always an issue; dollars per token is always important. How do you optimize around all these different metrics? In some cases, you excel on a few depending on the application, and in other instances, you might do something completely different. You need different types of solutions to come together to make this successful. It appears that we've seen an explosion of companies that want to build custom purpose inferencing compute, and it also appears that it is around arbitrating how one connects different memory architectures to the compute. You’ve got SRAM approaches, HBM approaches, stacked DRAM approaches, and even emerging flash and high bandwidth flash. And then you also mentioned what you are optimizing for—cost, energy, speed. When you put all of that into a giant bucket and align all the different companies, where is Dmatrix's focus in all of that? That’s a question we get very often—where do we fit in? So maybe let me focus the audience on what has become Jensen’s favorite chart that he shows in every keynote, which is the throughput per kilowatt, or throughput on the y-axis, versus interactivity on the x-axis. The message of the chart is simple: on the y-axis you have throughput, which is the amount of work you need to do, and on the x-axis is speed, or how quickly you want to do that. The two of them are typically at odds with each other because when you're trying to do more work, it typically needs more time. That's intuitive. At the same time, if you want to do something really quickly, it just means that you're trading off the amount of work you can do. Most HBM-based solutions today fall on that y-axis. GPUs today are an HBM-based solution; you have compute and HBM memory. Putting those two together has typically meant that you can optimize for compute and you will be able to really do well on that y-axis. So GPUs do extremely well there, especially when it comes to throughput batching using multiple users; it's very difficult to beat the GPU. Now, let's go to the x-axis—speed. You fundamentally need a different type of memory hierarchy to address that piece of the equation, and that is something we did from the early days at Dmatrix. We said we were going to focus on energy and speed. How do we create a new memory hierarchy that would allow us to really run inference fast? We created this approach called in-memory compute, which is putting compute and memory together and minimizing the amount of data that we have to move. This leads to not only improvements in speed but also energy, because you're not moving data around as much. That allows you to go pretty deep on the x-axis. You can optimize your solution for very high levels of what is now called interactivity. In an agentic world, machine-to-machine communication has become so important, and so latency matters a lot. That is why you're seeing huge interest in approaches that use SRAM-based architectures. We are now going from an SRAM-only architecture to an SRAM plus a custom DRAM architecture, whereby we stack DRAM memory not as a separate stack co-packaged with the compute, but sitting directly on top of the compute. We can actually drop data down into the compute. We call it 3D stacking DRAM or 3D IMC in our case. We can drop data down into the compute with extremely high levels of memory bandwidth. It is a fundamentally different way of putting compute and memory together that unlocks that capability on the x-axis to drive to extremely high levels of interactivity, and that is what Dmatrix has focused on. Let's elaborate a little bit more on this. This is very nuanced. On the y-axis is HBM and throughput; on the x-axis is SRAM, speed, and efficiency. Now you're doing stacked DRAM. Is stacked DRAM prime time, and when will that go live? Also, because you're using DRAM and SRAM, how unique is that in the industry? Nvidia bought Groq for SRAM, but this SRAM plus stacked RAM—can you do the holy grail and get both the x and the y-axis? Yes, absolutely. SRAM fundamentally has a capacity constraint; it doesn't scale well. Anytime you hear about an SRAM-based solution, the first thing that comes back is that you may not be able to run very large models, or when you run into very large context lengths, you might have capacity issues. That is why people have now started talking about disaggregated computing for SRAM accelerators. Instead of running the whole workload on the SRAM accelerator, you break it up and run the prefill portion—which is very compute-heavy—on the GPU, while you store a lot of the context on the GPU. Then you run other portions of the workload that are very memory-intensive on the SRAM accelerator. We just announced a partnership today with Nvidia for a target customer called Parasale, which is an inference cloud that is exactly doing that. They're going to deploy Blackwell GPUs with our Corsair SRAM accelerator to essentially do disaggregated heterogeneous compute for very fast token generation. Now, what is lacking in the SRAM solution is really capacity. This is where stacking DRAM on top of compute becomes very important because you're essentially building a skyscraper. You have the compute layer, which is SRAM at the bottom, and instead of having memory to the side, you have the DRAM memory sitting on top. We are planning to start with one layer of memory, then go to four, and then over time eight layers. If you can do that, you're addressing the speed on the x-axis while beginning to shift that curve up on the y-axis. You start shifting up on the x-axis while you're very deep on the y-axis. If we carry this further, you have disaggregated computing using GPUs and this new hybrid DRAM/SRAM compute. Will the world go this way? Will we have truly disaggregated computing where both coexist, and which market do you think gets bigger? I think both will coexist. There will always be applications where running everything on a single accelerator won't make sense because stacked DRAM will never really be able to match HBM capacity. Models will keep getting bigger, and context lengths are only getting larger. There will always be a place for GPU-based HBM solutions because models are getting trained on GPUs. If that is where the models are trained, the deployment capabilities will reflect that. So yes, there will always be a place for a model that is trained on a GPU to unleash capability that can only run on a GPU. But you can always disaggregate. There will be customers who don't need to run the whole thing on a GPU because that's too expensive or too power-hungry. But then there will be other customers with unique applications who don't have very large context lengths or the biggest frontier models, and they will be able to do a lot more than what most SRAM-based solutions are able to do today. Just one last thing: where are we with availability and the stacked DRAM approach? When is it going prime time? The stacked DRAM approach is what most people view as the holy grail, the endgame. We started working on this about three years ago. We went to Taiwan, spent time there, and worked with the supply chain. We've built multiple prototypes with that approach already. We have silicon back, we have exercised that supply chain multiple times, and we are now ready to tape out that production version, which will essentially come into the market in 2027. We strongly believe that we'll be among the first companies to release a product of that type in '27. Our SRAM-based product, which is our Gen 1 product, is already in production. Fantastic. That's exciting. We're looking forward to 2027. You will be the first to market with this stack. We think so. We think we might be among the first ones. Thank you so much. Thank you, Tony. Thanks for having me.

Pat Gelsinger & Elad Raz: Where Chips Go After the GPU Era | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·18:54·33 views·2026-07-22
Pat GelsingerPartner, Playground Global Elad RazCEO, NextSilicon
TL;DRAs AI workloads evolve rapidly beyond standard transformers toward agentic, continuous-learning, and multi-dimensional physical models, fixed ASICs face obsolescence, necessitating software-driven, flexible architectures that can balance high-performance computing (HPC) and AI on a unified chip.

TL;DR

As AI workloads evolve rapidly beyond standard transformers toward agentic, continuous-learning, and multi-dimensional physical models, fixed ASICs face obsolescence, necessitating software-driven, flexible architectures that can balance high-performance computing (HPC) and AI on a unified chip.

The Convergence of HPC and AI Workloads

  • Unified Mathematical Principles:
    • Elad Raz: Emphasizes that at their foundation, HPC (fluid dynamics, security, defense, physical simulation) and AI share identical mathematical principles, with AI workloads actually being simpler.
    • Shared Code Execution: NextSilicon’s Maverick accelerator was designed with a data-flow architecture for parallel code sections, complemented by an integrated RISC-V CPU to accelerate serial code.
  • Legacy Compatibility:
    • Diverse Software Support: NextSilicon's architecture is agnostic to MLOps frameworks, running code written in Triton, CUDA, C, PyTorch, and even legacy Fortran used extensively in supercomputing and scientific workloads.

The Renaissance of the CPU in the AI Era

  • Shifting Compute Ratios:
    • Pat Gelsinger: Notes that historical CPU-to-GPU ratios (1 CPU to 8 or 16 GPUs) are shifting toward 1-to-2 or even 1-to-1 ratios to handle agentic multi-model environments and workload affinity.
  • Workload-Driven Design:
    • Architecture Follows Function: Gelsinger stresses that computing has never been about underlying architecture, but rather how workloads are executed across the software-hardware stack.
  • Agentic Tools Integration:
    • Elad Raz: Points out that as LLMs execute tasks using multiple agentic tools and generate codebases for physical simulations, local memory retention on a single chip running both AI and physical simulation is critical.

The Flaws of Specialized ASICs and Fixed Architectures

  • The Danger of Single-Purpose Chips:
    • Elad Raz: Criticizes the trend of building custom ASICs exclusively for specific architectures (such as the Llama transformer), noting that rapid innovations in MLOps—like attention heads, multi-latent attention, sparse attention, and Mamba models operating in frequency space—render single-purpose hardware obsolete.
  • Absorbed Specialization:
    • Pat Gelsinger: Draws historical parallels to floating-point and media processors, which initially emerged as specialized units before being absorbed into general machine architecture as workloads evolved.
  • Avoiding Compute Islands:
    • Carving workloads up into isolated specialized zones (such as splitting training farms from pre-fill and decode) creates unscalable "islands of compute."

NextSilicon’s Architecture and Industry Validation

  • Development Milestones:
    • Elad Raz: Reports that NextSilicon spent 8 years and completed 4 tape-outs. Their first generation is deployed in national laboratories, and their second generation is in production and installed at the rack level with Department of Energy (DOE) entities.
  • The Arbel RISC-V Spin-Off:
    • Spun off their internal RISC-V chip (named Arbel) from the Maverick accelerator to create a best-in-class silicon core for high-performance serial code execution alongside data-flow parallel acceleration.
  • Capital and Innovation Costs:
    • Elad Raz: Notes that every chip generation costs approximately $150 million to develop, requiring distinct architectural advantages and early testing with scale customers.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Architectural Agnosticism Requirement: Elad Raz states that because chip design takes 3 years and future models cannot be accurately predicted, hardware must be built as an architecture capable of accelerating whatever comes next.
  • Evolution of AI Models: Pat Gelsinger predicts that models will transition from today's two-dimensional transformers into three- and four-dimensional models to accommodate molecular structures and physical AI.
  • Decaying Boundaries: Pat Gelsinger predicts the boundary between training and inferencing will decay over time, giving rise to algorithms that enable continuous self-training.
  • The 10,000x Efficiency Target: Pat Gelsinger asserts that computing infrastructure must become 10,000 times better to make AI energy- and capital-efficient enough to support next-generation chemistry, biology, material science, and human-centric computing, potentially incorporating technologies like cryogenic computing.

Notable Quotes

"Anybody that can predict the AI future 2 years from now is lying. It's just dynamically changing so rapidly associated with it." — Pat Gelsinger
"We have to make AI 10,000 times better than today, right? It isn't going to be a little bit better, we need to make it 10,000x, it is much too energy inefficient, much too capital, uh, inefficient if we're fully going to realize the benefits of AI for humanity's future." — Pat Gelsinger
"Chip design takes 3 years, so you need to predict which model will run 3 years from now. You can't do that, so build an architecture that whatever comes next, you can accelerate." — Elad Raz

Why Ambitious Startup Ideas Are Actually Easier To Sell

Y Combinator(2.3M)·28:42·9.9K views·2026-07-22
James HawkinsCEO, PostHog
TL;DRJames Hawkins explains how PostHog transformed from an open-source product analytics tool into an AI-native, self-driving software platform by increasing founder ambition, automating product management loops, and embracing high-upside market strategies.
Shift to Self-Driving...

TL;DR

James Hawkins explains how PostHog transformed from an open-source product analytics tool into an AI-native, self-driving software platform by increasing founder ambition, automating product management loops, and embracing high-upside market strategies.

PostHog’s AI-Native Transformation

  • Shift to Self-Driving Software: PostHog evolved from providing academic analytics to actually solving jobs for users and improving graphs automatically.
  • The Recursive AI Loop: James Hawkins describes how the system ingests data types like support tickets, video clips, and Slack discussions to ship pull requests automatically.
  • Traction and Metrics:
    • PostHog's newly shipped support product has roughly 1,100 companies using it.
    • A significant chunk of PostHog's own engineering pull requests are now generated via this AI loop.

The Product Engineering Challenge & The Intent Harness

  • The Thousand Tiny Features Problem: The risk of AI building numerous disjointed features is mitigated by focusing on the "intent side" of product engineering.
  • The Limitations of Current Harnesses: Existing tools like Claude lack conversational product context; PostHog is working on broad harnesses that capture the actual product vision from engineers and leadership.
  • Redefining Roles:
    • Product managers transition into configuring software and setting rules and policies.
    • Support team roles evolve into writing manuals, rule books, and guiding the AI agents.

Founder Dynamics & Co-CEO Roles

  • Job Swapping: James Hawkins and co-founder Tim swapped roles (switching between CEO/CTO and product/sales responsibilities) to regain energy and leverage their evolving strengths.
  • Division of Labor for AI Pivot: To execute the AI pivot smoothly without creating misaligned bottlenecks, Tim handled operations, finance, scaling, and hiring (more than doubling the company size that year), while James went "all in" on AI engineering.
  • Avoiding Burnout: Building a big company requires long-term growth, which James and Tim believe is unsustainable if the work is not fundamentally fun.

European vs. American Ambition & Fundraising

  • Breaking Imposter Syndrome: Early on, the founders suffered from intense imposter syndrome, but Y Combinator normalized the reality that even massive companies are run by ordinary human beings.
  • Cultural Differences in Risk:
    • European founders often focus heavily on downside risks (e.g., failure, social perception, early GDPR compliance).
    • American culture encourages aiming for massive upside—such as declaring an intent to cure all disease.
  • Venture Capital Realities: Startups are "default screwed anyway," meaning investors care exclusively about the 99.99% upside (e.g., Y Combinator investing in 7,000 companies where 90% of total returns come from just 5 companies).

Building a Cult Brand & The Pivot Journey

  • Intentional Differentiation: In crowded markets, the challenge is standing out rather than creating demand; PostHog used distinct branding (like hedgehogs and bus ads), transparent pricing, and direct credit-card sign-ups to attract engineers.
  • The Pivot History: PostHog executed 5 pivots before landing on product analytics during the YC Winter 20 batch.
  • Validation Discipline: The founders committed blindly and worked hard on each idea—visiting early customers in person via multiple trains and buses—before moving on when they repeatedly hit insurmountable product validation hurdles.

Notable Quotes

"You're default screwed as a startup. So, it is all upside. You just can't win on downside. The investors we want to work with are focused on the 'what if it all works out' because they know that 99.99% of your startup's upside hasn't been done yet." — James Hawkins
"The role of our support team is becoming—and I really mean this, not just to sound nice—you're supporting the agents to do their job. So you're writing out the manual, the rule book, and you're iterating and improving, and you're kind of becoming a philosopher for support basically." — James Hawkins
"Don't be a lemming. I think people have a really high propensity to do what society expects of you, which is to follow a career path that takes you... you go get a good degree at university, you become a banker, you gradually make a VP and then you retire and die. You really don't have to." — James Hawkins
So it is all upside. You just can't win on downside. It's the biggest thing I found dealing with investors who are stronger; like the investors we want to work with are focused on the "what if it all works out" because they know that 99.99% of your startup's upside hasn't been done yet. I think you're probably one of the most interesting developer companies to come out of YC in recent years. PostHog was a really, really popular YC dev tool, but it seems like you're reshaping the company now to build AI-native self-driving software. Can you talk a little bit about that, how that happened, and where you're up to right now? Sure. So yeah, PostHog now provides self-driving software; it's our new kind of one-liner. We started off by, as you say, launching open-source product analytics during the batch. Obviously, there's been a platform shift with AI coming in. In terms of what that's meant for us, we upped the ambition of what we're doing. From first principles—which is a very overused term but still—we decided it was fundamentally better to entirely solve someone's job for them rather than giving them a rough idea of what's important. We felt it was better to be a company that does stuff for people rather than an analytics company that gives you this academic idea of how things are performing. We want to be able to improve your graphs and make them go up, not just tell you that they're going up or down. We've been working on clustering, doing a whole bunch of stuff. We're looking to model AI research and all sorts of things to understand across all the data types that we have where your problems are in your product, what your customers are complaining about in support, what's happening in your logs and errors and session recordings, and then shipping pull requests just to fix as many of the simpler engineering tasks that we can. We're also doing a bunch of stuff at the top end too, but that's further out from now. Okay, this is an idea I'm really, really interested in. I'm going to give a talk later today about building AI-native companies. And this idea of basically sort of self-contained recursive loops is what I'm really, really fascinated in. The idea that you take some input data and feed it to an AI with a bunch of rules and policies, the AI does something, feeds it back into the world, sees how it impacts the world, and just iterates on that loop. That sounds like exactly what you're describing at PostHog. Yeah, and we've got a way to go. We do have the basics of this in production. As of last week, we're finally getting all the components together. And I think the step beyond that is kind of interesting because there are more and more data types you can pull from. It might be a support ticket, it might be a video clip of a user using your product, or it might be what you're talking about in Slack. We're working on trying to grab the intent behind the product. So, as you're prompting, we're basically building a harness that grabs all of the intent data from you as an engineer. Instead of you unblocking an agent every four minutes, it iterates with you quickly to understand why this product exists, rather than starting with just the technical requirements. So it's got something to optimize towards. Okay. Yeah, I could see it being present in—or at least listening to the recordings or transcripts of—the product meeting where you might have a chief product officer and a CTO talking with a designer and engineer about what you're trying to achieve, and actually feeding that in as an input into the loop. Yeah, exactly. I think there's no reason you can't build it. One thing that PostHog did in the early days is that my co-founder and I were actively bad at product, which is a tough spot to be in if you're a product founder. We realized in our pivoting process that we used to build lots of products that had never existed at all. Then we had this moment where we went, "Hang on, we're better at outcompeting and building product growth, and doing a better job on the marketing and execution side." So we started building software products that already had competition. In the AI world, we're like, "Well, there are so many things possible that don't exist in the world." We started looking at what are the jobs happening inside a company, because they're already happening, and we feel very confident we'll have product-market fit if we can just accelerate them and do them to a better standard. A product manager usually decides what to build in most companies. I think a computer can do a better job; it can do a superhuman job of understanding everything that's happening in your customer base across every sales call, every internal meeting, every email you get from your customers, and all their user behavior too. And so I do think there's no reason why we can't build something that's far superior to a product manager essentially on that particular part of the product life cycle. Yeah, I totally agree. I think that's a great heuristic for finding startup ideas and also for identifying human jobs that your product can replace in part or in whole. It sounds like you've done that with product management. Yeah, and I think the nature of a product manager just completely changes to being the person that configures the software and goes, "Hey, this is how I think this should work, these are some of the rules and policies in place." For example, we shipped a support product quite recently and it's got quite a lot of traction; about 1,100 companies are using it. Our view with this was, "Well, if we've got all this data, we can also just solve loads of the tickets better because we really understand the user in more depth than anyone else can without the data." The role of the support team, though—it's not replacing them; it's going into, and I really mean this, the role of our support team is becoming supporting the agents to do their job. You're writing out the manual, the rulebook, iterating and improving, and you're kind of becoming a philosopher for support. Before we move on, I have a bunch more questions, but I want to ground this. The product you launched last week or so, what is the most impressive or canonical example of turning some product data into an actual pull request that gets shipped? Have you seen something so far that's been mind-blowing? We're just repeatedly seeing in Slack someone go, "Oh, we're having problems. This thing's not working, this thing's broken, whatever." And then they're like, "Oh, there's already a pull request for it. We just need to click merge at this point." Hopefully, that isn't a thing anymore in the future at some stage. But it's just repeatedly getting there. I can see human beings at PostHog getting there after the robot has decided there's a problem and entirely fixed it. It's just so far ahead of the game. I think some of the implications of this are going to be most interesting. We need to do more work on this data type, but it's the trace data for AI-first products where you can iterate more quickly because you don't have to wait for the next human being to show up. You can backward-test changes to a product to see what would have happened had its prompting been set up like this. It's already looking super promising. A chunk of our own pull requests are generated this way, which just means our developers are spending more and more time on big features, big new services, and new products. Where my mind goes with that is, how do you avoid a product that has a thousand tiny features that the AI has built, but they don't fit together into a holistic product vision? I think this is the intent side and why the harness matters so much. I think the current state of harnesses is not where it will end. At the moment, if you ask Claude, "Build me a to-do list app," the first question will probably be something like, "Well, do you want me to mock up the backend, should I provide data, or which database do you want?" It doesn't talk to you at all. If I talk to a human member of our team—we consider them product engineers at PostHog—and I said, "Hey, can we build a to-do list app, please?" they won't disappear and come back with a bunch of pull requests. They'll ask me questions like, "Why? Is it going to be simpler? More powerful? Is it for a particular audience? What's special about this thing?" I think as an industry, no one's focused that much on grabbing the product side of product engineering, which I think has a ton of value. So we're doing work, but I suspect other people will too, on a different type of harness that's a bit broader, and there will probably be different modes depending on the task. Yeah, I agree. At the moment we've automated the engineer and the CTO job, but we need to bring in the CPO or a product manager with vision to say, "This seems out of scope or way off," or at least come back and clarify if it fits the vision. How long ago was it that you decided to make this change? Probably about a year and a half ago. And was the mood inside PostHog like, "We have to bet the whole company," or was it, "We'll place one or two developers on this"? How did you think about it? If I'm really honest, it didn't come from inside PostHog. I went on vacation for a couple of weeks and had the longest break I've had at PostHog. I was reading, and it just suddenly—quite nerdily—I started thinking about the difference between an LLM and a human brain. I created a list, and I realized there were a lot of differences in a way that made me feel LLMs could go much, much further. The reason for this whole thought process was reading why some of the co-founders of OpenAI joined in the first place. The thought process was super engineering; for several of them, it was basically, "Humans have a brain, it's a physical object, so we can just build one. It's just a bunch of engineering." Obviously, it's an insanely difficult task, but that attitude is why they were successful. So I started thinking in more detail: What's the next-level detail? What's the gap between a brain and an LLM right now? And I just concluded there's a long way still to go. I think other things are starting to constrain the economic value that comes from a model. I think as the models are getting better, many are already way past where they need to be to provide a lot of value, and the challenge is gluing the model to the use case, which is also a good place to look for startup ideas. A lot of our product thinking comes from there too. Letting the LLM have tools, send emails, do analytics, or instrument your product to collect data—that's the challenge. Anyway, I run the company with Tim, my co-founder. We're co-CEOs, which I would super recommend for early-stage companies. We started off where I was CEO and Tim was CTO because he was more technical than me. I have a mixed background; I've run a sales team, done some very bad engineering, whereas Tim is an engineer through and through. But we realized after a while that I'm a little bit better at pushing for things on the product side, and Tim is actually better at running sales and treats it like a system much more. So we swapped. As we got bigger, I literally just do stuff that I want to work on and that I'm good at. Anything else that doesn't fit those two things, I just won't work on. When it came to this, I was like, "I need to make PostHog very AI-first." I talked to Tim, he agreed, and then we talked about how to execute this well. We decided if we both try to do this at the same time, we're going to get in each other's way. So Tim said, "Okay, I'm just going to look after everything else—finance, hiring, scaling up," and I just hung out with engineers building AI stuff. He let me go all-in on AI and making the whole company go in that direction, so I didn't have to worry about anything else. I didn't do any one-on-ones or anything like that. I'm just randomly doing offsites or meeting up with engineers to get them to build things with me. That sounds really fun. It has been, by definition. I want it to be fun, because building a big company requires growth for a long time, and if it's not fun, we'll just leave. I 100% agree with you. I took the other route and then left because I killed myself doing all the day-to-day things, and I'm not sure I had someone like Tim I could rely on to run the entire company while I went off to do the fun future-vision stuff. But the fact that you've got that partnership allows you to do that; it's super powerful. Yeah, and you can swap around. You also find the energy is very different. I had been running sales, I started getting a little bit bored, and I got much more interested in product. Tim was feeling the other way around. We often found that whatever you're not doing, you start worrying about. You think, "I'm not in control of product," and Tim was thinking, "I'm not in control of sales." So we just swapped for a bit, and it was great. We both had tons of energy; it was like a new job. So I would say, don't be a lemming. People have a high propensity to do what society expects—you get a degree, become a banker, make VP, and then you die. You really don't have to. You can swap around, you can run sales, you can do product, you can do engineering—whatever is the bottleneck to the company being successful. Changing tack a little bit: you've argued that founders should be much more ambitious, especially founders in Europe. Do you have examples of ideas that sound too big or too hard, but might actually be more fundable or recruitable than another narrow SaaS tool? How do you think about how much ambition is too much? I think there isn't a ceiling. Generically, what I have learned as PostHog has gotten far more ambitious over time is that at the start, we were almost apologetic, trying to find a little place in the market hiding away from bigger companies. We were scared of competition. Eventually, we learned to start embracing it, and we realized we can build something much better. PostHog went from one product to wanting to build 20, to now wanting to build hundreds, and now saying, "Actually, these tools don't even matter anymore; an agent should be using all of them." The more ambitious we've gotten, the easier it's been to hire really good people. Strong people want to work for companies working on something inspiring; it's just obvious. It's far easier to do go-to-market for ideas that are very ambitious, too. If you're more ambitious, you're more remarkable. As the world gets busier, there's more software coming out, so getting someone's attention gets harder. If you're building something remarkable, people will remark upon it on the internet. You'll get word-of-mouth growth. If you're building a narrow point solution, you can be successful, but you have an easier time if you're building something fundamentally more compelling and bigger. In terms of fundraising, it's easier to fundraise for very large ideas. A bad investor is less likely to work with you, but I think the really strong ones are more likely to. I basically agree. But I think the nuance is that you have to be split as a founder. You have to have the "Sam Altman mode"—what is the most ambitious version of this idea I can conceive and say with a straight face—and simultaneously direct your company to the next practical step you can take today to advance towards that goal. Having that short-term practicality combined with long-term intense ambition kind of hurts your brain sometimes, but you need both. Yeah, you must ship. Even all these ideas we're working on, we're breaking them down into small bits and getting things out the door. I would argue the more ambitious your idea is, the more important it is to ship, because you're more likely to screw up since it's more ambitious. If you disappear into the wilderness for three years and come back with a perfect product, I don't think you will succeed. You'll get there by building it with a bunch of people in real life. We're more focused than ever on shipping the more innovative an idea is. Everything's very busy at the moment because everything's become so innovative, which is creating pressure to ship things into our users' hands. I think this is such an important point. European founders often ask, "How do these Americans who have nothing claim to be working on all this insane stuff? Are they just liars?" I think a few things have struck me as we've grown. In the early days, before YC, we looked up to people doing YC as superstars, and we had really strong imposter syndrome—which I think you're more likely to get the more European you are. When we got into YC, we realized, actually, a lot of the stuff is very underwhelming. People are much earlier than we thought; it's more chaotic than you would expect. But we saw companies rocket and take off from there. As we grow, we realize that even when we meet people running companies with 100 billion in valuation, they're all just human beings underneath. Our goal is to go into meetings and just treat them like they're our peers and see through the veneer. Imposter syndrome really crumbled. I think that's what I was struggling with most as someone who had never worked for a "sexy" company. YC normalized people aiming really high, which sounds fluffy and like a non-satisfactory answer to an engineer, but honestly, it made us 100 times more confident. I think this is a more normal culture to have in America than in Europe. In the US, it's not looked down upon if someone declares they're going to solve cancer. In San Francisco, that's an entirely normal and potentially plausible thing to hear, because you can't get there without believing it at the start. Yeah, or "cure all disease ever." I've had founders say that, and we've funded them. Probably it won't work, but if it did, that would be pretty impactful. I think the Americans just have this "what if it all goes right?" mentality. There's a tendency in Europe to ask yourself too often, "What if it fails, or what are my friends and family going to think of me?" There's this weird cultural deprogramming you have to do at YC when we get European founders over. I know, it does sound a bit cult-like. I would also say the other thing I've realized is that you're just default screwed as a startup. So, it is all upside; you just can't win on downside. It's the biggest thing I found dealing with investors who are stronger. There's no point worrying about downside or how it goes wrong, because you can't; a successful business can make thousands of times more money than someone else in theory. I think it's like that with upside versus downside risk. Yeah. As an investor, you can only lose your money once. For Y Combinator, we've invested in 7,000 companies, and I would guess 90% of our total returns come from five companies. It doesn't matter if 6,995 companies fail, as long as five companies reach 100 billion. Exactly. When you're fundraising, you've got to be pitching like that. Not like, "Oh, we'll save the money and be super careful." You need to not die, but that isn't the model investors are after. Investing in a company that gets to a 50 million outcome is a waste of time, whereas investing in something that has a 1% chance of being a 100 billion company—sign me up. I'll take that bet every single day. Just ask yourself, "What if it all goes right?" and pitch that vision. You've got a cult-like developer brand—the hedgehogs, "You deserve PostHog" on buses—how intentional is all of that? Did it develop, is it a joke, or is it effective? It took work at the start. I think if you're going into a busy market, your challenge in marketing is standing out. You're not trying to create demand; you're just trying to show that this is a company that stands for something more interesting. When we launched, every other company had a blue website, used long words, and had pricing pages that didn't have pricing. We were getting annoyed trying to access these products as technical people. We just wanted to click "sign up," put in our credit card, and go. We got traction in the early days by doing stuff that wasn't product-related better. Our product was super terrible compared to the competition for a long while, but people wanted us to win on the internet. We targeted engineers, and they cheered us on until we got there. Now, our product is much stronger, but we've built the muscle internally where the marketing team does whatever they want, provided there's a common philosophy. I'm having a lot of fun running PostHog, and our team is as well. You're unlikely to get hired if you show up wearing a suit to your interview, or if you really, really want to be a manager. We look for people who are going to be genuinely entertained by what we're working on, and it helps. It just comes across naturally. I've noticed that about you and Tim. You seem to really enjoy your job. It's one of the most important things for stopping founders from burning out over 15 years. Patrick Collison said he wants to be running Stripe for 40 more years, so his role needs to reflect that. It's not purely an external question of what he does for customers; he knows that by having a good time, he will produce better output. Last question on pivots. You guys pivoted five times before you landed on PostHog. What did that feel like? How did you think through those first few pivots, and how did PostHog feel different? To do a good job of learning, we needed to learn something each week: Do people value this concept? We'd set goals like that, not "hit 20k MRR" at the start. It was, "Can we get one single user to use this in production?" Luck with product-market fit is a function of how many shots you're taking and the quality of each shot. We tried for four or six weeks, and if we couldn't get traction, we moved on because it felt unnatural. I would go out validating the idea with users. We didn't phone anything in; we always went to meet people in person, even if it looked stupid to spend 300 dollars on travel for a 300-dollar contract. Later, when we tried to charge more for those earlier ideas, we couldn't get the price up, which showed us they didn't value it. We committed to each idea blindly and hammered it out until we either realized people wouldn't talk to us, wouldn't use it, or wouldn't pay. Once we hit that hurdle over and over, we either ran out of ideas or decided it just didn't feel right. We were decisive. We were probably too much on the side of abandoning things early, but that's because we were bad product people at first. We had to do a few products to build that knowledge. Amazing. Well, it all worked out in the end. I have a million more questions, but we are out of time. I'd love to give a huge round of applause to James. Thank you.

Building a Company in Stealth | Travis Kalanick with a16z

a16z(281.0K)·92:17·30.8K views·2026-07-22
Travis KalanickFounder, Atoms Ben HorowitzCo-founder, a16z Erik TorenbergHost, a16z
TL;DRTravis Kalanick, Ben Horowitz, and Erik Torenberg reflect on Uber's legendary early days, the 2011 Series B investment that nearly happened with a16z, and Kalanick's 8-year stealth return building Atoms—a company digitizing the physical world across ...
food, mining, and autonomous...

TL;DR

Travis Kalanick, Ben Horowitz, and Erik Torenberg reflect on Uber's legendary early days, the 2011 Series B investment that nearly happened with a16z, and Kalanick's 8-year stealth return building Atoms—a company digitizing the physical world across food, mining, and autonomous transport using industrial AI.

The Deal That Got Away: Uber's Series B (2011)

  • The A16Z Pitch and Breakdown: Travis Kalanick describes running a high-momentum "winner takes all" auction for Uber's Series B in 2011, securing an uncapped anchor at a $375M pre-money valuation with a16z. Ben Horowitz explains that after the pitch, he passed the deal to Mark and John O'Ferrell, but the partnership backed off the $375M price tag, counter-offering $210M—collapsing Kalanick's momentum and forcing him to restart the auction.
  • The Butterfly Effect: Horowitz and Kalanick both agree that if a16z had been on Uber's board in 2011, the company's tumultuous 2017 would have been avoided, and Uber would be significantly larger today, likely dominating food delivery and autonomy outright.
  • The Lift Parallel: Horowitz shares that a16z subsequently backed Lyft, a competitive startup that launched into extreme difficulties largely because of Uber, eventually forcing Horowitz to take a board seat to help steady it.

The Evolution of Leadership: From Pirate to Navy

  • Managing Growth and Governance: Reflecting on his 2017 departure from Uber, Kalanick notes that while he stands by every decision, he pushed too close to the line and failed to realize that big enterprises must transition from an upstart "pirate" mentality to a disciplined "navy" framework.
  • Aggressive Tactics: Kalanick shares that internal programs like "shoplifting" (aggressively recruiting drivers away from Lyft) had to be rebranded to harmless names like the North American Championship Series (NACS) after pushback from board members like David Drummond and legal training on antitrust rules.
  • Post-Uber Hell: Kalanick spent 7-8 months post-Uber fighting lawsuits and investigations before finding his next venture.

Building in Stealth: The 8-Year Journey of Atoms

  • Origins as Cloud Kitchens: Following his exit from Uber, Kalanick partnered with a real estate savant who had started Cloud Kitchens (originally incorporated as City Storage Systems), a multi-tenant delivery-only commercial kitchen concept. Kalanick acquired the company and scaled it into a global operation.
  • Operating in Hard Mode: To escape the 150 negative media articles a day from his final days at Uber, Kalanick operated in strict stealth for nearly 8 years, growing to thousands of employees across 30 countries (operating under various local names like Kitchen Valley in Korea, Flash Kitchen in China, and Food Stars in London) without the New York Times knowing.
  • The Cultural Advantage of Stealth: Operating in stealth starved the company of external validation and fame-seeking, forcing internal correctness, emotional intelligence, and genuine fulfillment from the daily work rather than press clippings.

Digitizing the Physical World: The Atoms-Based Computer

  • The Atoms Framework: Kalanick explains his thesis that physical world industries can be modeled using computer science architecture:
    • Manufacturing = CPU (manipulates atoms)
    • Real Estate = Storage (stores atoms)
    • Transport & Logistics = Network (moves atoms)
  • The Food Computer Vision: The ultimate goal for Atoms Food is to make the preparation and delivery of a quality meal so efficient that it approaches the grocery store cost, effectively doing to the kitchen what Uber did to the car. This requires automated manufacturing (food robotics) and robotic couriers to drop distribution costs from $12 per meal down to $0.50–$1.00.
  • Expanding into Mining (Pronto): Atoms acquired Pronto, an off-road autonomy and mining automation company where Kalanick was an early investor. The business targets a 20% increase in productivity and critical safety improvements for gold and mineral mines globally.

Industrial AI and the Future of Physical Automation

  • Industrial AI Stack: Kalanick defines the emerging category of Industrial AI as a practical stack of software, sensors, robotics, and machinery designed to automate heavy, multi-trillion-dollar physical industries one at a time—starting with food, mining, and transport.
  • Parallels to the Second Industrial Revolution: Horowitz and Kalanick draw strong parallels between today's regulatory and public resistance to physical AI, data centers, and industrial automation and the struggles faced by historical industrialists like Carnegie, Rockefeller, and Ford.

Notable Quotes

"The meaningful thing about Atoms food... can you make the preparation and delivery of a quality meal so efficient that it approaches the cost of going to the grocery store? If it does, you do to the kitchen what Uber did to the car." — Travis Kalanick
"When you fall in love again, you don't think about the ex very much." — Travis Kalanick
"He's one of the very rare guys who made as much money as he did and wanted to keep playing... There's only one non-fungible, very rare person in that equation who could do that." — Ben Horowitz (on Travis Kalanick)
"Everybody loves progress. It's change they can't stand." — Ben Horowitz

You're dealing with the lawsuits. You worry about the lawsuit and you say, "Hey, let's build again." It's not as much about where you start; it's about why you start. So, this will be interesting because I think Ben and I have maybe discussed this once or twice. Yeah, painful. And I think there could be a difference of opinion on what happened, but I'll tell you what I thought happened. So, I'm in fundraising mode. This is our Series B. This is 2011. Uber started 2010, June 2010. I'm raising our Series B. Earlier that year, I did Series A with a VC I'll remain nameless. I was hardcore. It's interesting; I went through such tough times as an entrepreneur pre-Uber that once I'm in Uber and things are working, I'm still acting like I'm not going to eat tomorrow. So, I would do everything right up to the line, like be perfect. And so I have this whole fundraising process that I would do just to perfection. And that means I'd run an auction. So I met with the A16Z guys, met with lots of folks. And it was what I would call a "winner takes all" auction, meaning there's one major lead. They're going to set the price and then everybody else will fall in. So I'd go to the one guy, tell him what the story was, and they're like, "Oh, you know, we're interested." He's leaning forward. And I would do this thing where I would say, "We don't know—this is going to be one of the hottest deals in Silicon Valley this year. We don't know where the price is going to be, where it's going to go to, but it's at least blank." And I call this the uncapped anchor. Meaning, I'm never in a place where I'm negotiating with somebody where I'm here and they're here and we meet in the middle. I'm always saying it's at least this and it can go up, but I start low. Everybody's pumped, leaning forward. You can see body language; you can read it, it's good. You go to the next guy. He literally comes to your office an hour later. Same thing happens. He's fired up. I'm showing all the analytics. It's great. Okay, so we're interested. What are you thinking about price? I'm like, if the last one was at least 2x our previous round, it just became 3x. I'll say, "We don't know. It's going to be one of the top deals in Silicon Valley this year, but it will be at least 3x our last round." And he's like, "Okay." You then go call the guy before and you're like, "Hey, just went from 2x to 3x." They start getting nervous. Anyways, you get all the way to the top and then you go going once, going twice. It's kind of funny in this particular case, Yuri was almost coming over the top. So we got to 375 million pre with A16Z. Yuri almost came in at 400, but he was like, "No, I can't do it." And so I'm like, "Okay, going once, going twice, sold." And it's funny because that's a high-momentum deal, and that's how you do it. At the time, you almost should feel a little shy about doing something like that. It's a little bit crass, let's just say, but that's how I was wired back in the day. Like, just go all the freaking way. Like, run the touchdown, score the touchdown even when you're going to, you know, it's a little much. So we're starting to work, you're going to get a term sheet going, the whole thing. And I get an email from Mark. It was like, "Hey, let's go to dinner." I think it was like tomorrow night or something like that. Let's go to dinner. And I said, "Send me the term sheet, and then yeah, we can go to dinner." And he responded, "Let's go to dinner." And I'm like, "Oh, I didn't like that." So I came down south, this neck of the woods, because I was in San Francisco and this little sushi place—I don't remember the name of it. And he said, "Look, I know we talked about 375, but I went to the partnership. They don't agree or they just didn't think it was the right number. The best we're going to be able to do is 210." So that—I'm just telling you how it went down for me. Wow. Well, and so then I'm like—the story was definitely different on the other side, but it'd be interesting to hear it. I've got more than the hour we have scheduled here if we want to take time. But anyways, I had a high-momentum deal. It went all the way to the top. I auctioned it, cleared the market, and the bottom came out from it. And so I had to go back to everybody else and say 210 is the new number. Like, "Hey guys, the guy who won backed out. I'm now back at 210. We're starting the auction over." It was super awkward and weird because you lose credibility in that moment. I built it back up, and basically it ended up being Menlo Park. This is how Shervin got involved in Uber. And at some point I told Shervin to stop negotiating against himself because he just kept driving it up without anybody else. I'm like, "Dude, you won, it's fine." I didn't want the same thing to happen again. So I am very clear about the 375 and the 210 and the experience I had, but there may be another side of that story. Yeah. Well, you know, okay, let me just tell you what I actually remember, and then what I kind of remember. So, I remember you came in for the pitch and I think it was just you. Yeah, that's unusual. Normally, people don't—particularly in those days—it was very rare that an entrepreneur would come in with no team members on a deal that size. That was a big deal, by the way. It was a high-price deal at 375. The pitch was super impressive. And then the other thing I recall pretty closely was like, Travis had definitely read some of my blogs, so I felt like, "Oh, we can win this." We had the discussion, and it was clear, like, "No, we want to do the deal." And at the time, I was like, "I should really do this one," but I wasn't the consumer guy. And I had a lot of board seats—I think it was like 16 board seats, something crazy. It was just so much, so I gave it to Mark and John O'Farrell to do. And then all I remember—I remember something different at this point, but I got it all from them, so I wasn't there—there was something about the employee option pool or some like... I just assumed we were doing the deal, and then the next thing I knew, Shervin had the deal, and I was like, "Geez." I regret I can't tell you how long, because that was the beginning of my torture on this thing. I was like, "Fuck, I know we should do that deal. We lost the deal." Meanwhile, Scott Weiss did a deal for the other company in the space, Lyft. And by the way, that kind of got off to a very, very difficult start, largely because of Uber. So, in order to catch up, I ended up having to go on the board; I took over that board seat to kind of help them through that. And just so you understand my experience on the Lyft thing, it happened I believe a year later. Almost to the day, something like this, and the deal on 210. Was that it really? I don't know, I'm just saying this is what the entrepreneur is going to remember, dude. You know what I'm saying? Holy cow. But anyway, because if a company is in a lot of trouble, that's generally when I get called, given my history with that. Oh man, I was working so hard on that. I was working hard on that trouble. Oh my god, it was a lot of trouble. But anyway, I'm living with that. And then by the way, I would see Travis now and again. I had him over to my house, CEO barbecue, like all kinds of stuff, because I knew who he was. We were kind of friends, and there was a respect there because we knew who each other were. But he was winning and he wasn't going to let me not hear it. It was just horrible. I had to listen; I was like, "God damn it." There was also this other thing that would happen. This started around 2014 or 2015. Emil and I would meet up with Mark and Ben at this super undercover restaurant right next to our office, which was in the same building as Square. By the way, Emil is also remarkably amazing and currently the CTO of the Department of War, but super talented. The best. We would do these sessions; we'd have dinner, break some bread, talk shop. Emil and I would always walk out going, "Damn, it would be so great to have these guys involved." Of course, I didn't because they were at Lyft. And so at some point, an even bigger vision for this company emerges to digitize the physical world. You're category-creating; you're creating a new category: industrial AI. Talk about when this bigger vision starts to culminate. So look, the vision for digitizing the physical world for me started at Uber. Treating atoms like bits started at Uber. It was sci-fi at one point; I promise you, you'd touch glass and a car would come to you. You had satellite view, you're watching this car come to you. That was crazy. Now it's just like, whatever. But it was crazy at the time. Everybody's first Uber experience was a magical moment. So digitizing the physical world was the thing. And this idea of what I would call an atoms-based computer was something I was working on in terms of a framework at the end of Uber: CPU manipulates bits, storage stores bits, network moves bits from point A to point B. Okay. Well, CPU manipulates bits. What manipulates atoms? That's manufacturing. Storage stores bits. What stores atoms? That's real estate. Network moves bits from point A to point B. What moves atoms? That's transport, logistics, and those now are your three core computing resources in an atoms-based computer. Everything you learn in your computer science curriculum, everything you learn in engineering, it plays in the atoms world. Same frameworks, all the data structures, all the algorithms; it works exactly the same. Routing algorithms... oh, totally. In fact, we use TCP to basically manage how much demand to send to certain kitchens based on capacity and reliability as an example. But when I'm pitching this to a tech person who wants to understand that 10,000-square-foot facility, I call it a 10,000-square-foot semiconductor. It's a 30-core processor, 30 kitchens. They're computing atoms, not bits. The corridors where the food moves, that's a network bus. The processing center is like an L1 cache or something similar. The cold storage where you have ice cream and juice—okay, that's precomputed items that sit on the edge. Those are your Akamai servers. The courier who comes and picks up the food and goes on the road and brings the meal to you—he's a TCP packet. This goes forever. It's excellent. So the vision started at Uber. The point is, atoms-based computation is a thing, and honestly, it's always been a thing. And the idea that Uber was network for the physical world—it digitized transportation. And by the way, it's almost done. Why almost done? Because it's almost fully software. Right? Waymos are cruising. It's almost fully software.
