And then by the way, we've seen it so many times where somebody gets to the penthouse and they're like, "Oh, I want to do a new thing," but they don't want to go all the way down to the ground floor and start at the bottom putting the building together. They want to jump all the way to the top, which never works. You can't start at the top. Well, and the bottom thing also is about a humility thing. There's like an anti-fame thing to it. And what I mean by that is, imagine you start from the bottom, you also do it stealth. It means you plus everybody in your company gets fulfillment from the work they do every day—not because they're going to get famous, but because they enjoy the people they're working with and what they're doing day in, day out. By the way, if you can pull it off, that's a very significant cultural advantage because as soon as the company feels like it's doing something for outside status, it's a dangerous, volatile situation. That's right. You start making decisions based on external validation versus internal correctness. You lose true north. And that's part of the upside of stealth and part of what I was doing because remember, I wanted to build without worrying about what the New York Times was saying. So it starts with internal correctness versus external validation. Once the company starts doing that, it's hard to stop. You already see this with the big labs. People keep going, "Oh, why do the big labs smack themselves in the face?" Well, it's because they need that external validation for their employees because their employees are addicted to it. I want to be a good guy, I want to be a good AI researcher. So, go ahead and tell them that we're going to take all the jobs. It's like, really? You want to say that? You don't even know if it's true, but you want to say that. Well, why are you doing that? It's that external validation is so important to them, and it's so important not just to the people running it, but to all the people in the company. So they almost have to do it, even though if you think that, then work on those problems. If you don't want to reward hack, well, why don't you try and solve that as opposed to running your mouth because you care about the external validation more than the actual thing. It's very easy to slip into that. Many companies slip into that, and it's hard to stop once it gets out of the bag. There's a human nature aspect to being proud of what you do, wanting your mom to be proud of what you do, and your community at large. So there is a human nature piece which I was malnourishing by being stealth, but it forced an emotional intelligence that's very powerful now that we're moving out of stealth. It will be interesting to see how the coming years go. I'm that's going to be a very interesting cultural case study. So we've talked about what books were to Amazon; food is to Adams in the sense of it's where you start, and where you start matters. So why was it important that you started at food in terms of what's generalizable? Here's the thing: what Cloud Kitchens was all about—the way I saw it, and this was what I brought to the party—was digitized manufacturing and digitized real estate. I called it what people know as Cloud Kitchens because that's what we'd sell restaurant customers on, but the name of the company was City Storage Systems, storage for the physical world. This is digitized real estate. This is a food computer with a basis in real estate storage. That means a network. This was the name of the company from the beginning, or once I came in. It was purposely boring because I was going stealth. You imagine selling something called City Storage Systems? People are like, "What is that?" They'll forget literally 20 minutes after you talk to them. They're thinking HTML, they're confused, they don't know what's going on. So for me, the framework was already there. I already thought about this as digitized manufacturing and real estate—an atoms-based computer, a food computer—and that's why I was excited. The idea captured me because of that. And so it wasn't like, "Why did you start with food?" It was like I saw something different than even maybe the original team that was working on it saw. I saw where it was going in the way that I just described, and that's what the romance was about. And yes, there are lots of digitized manufacturing problems out there—like mining, like a whole bunch of other things—but this just captured me at the right time. I was sitting at the bar, somebody came up and we had a great conversation, went to Cabo the next weekend and then just put a ring on it. Let's go. Serendipity is a real thing. And that's okay, because if you believe that you can find something, make it work, just grind it out, and will it into working, your imagination can keep going. So for me, it's not as much about where you start; it's about making sure you're passionate about where you start. And then where you go, you have no idea. This is very much the Bezos style: your imagination will just keep coming up with new, cool ideas. It's not about where you start; it's about why you start and how you build a culture that allows you to keep going into new places over time and lets your imagination flourish. It's not an idea, it's an idea maze. The more you learn about the idea, the more you realize you know so little when you start. I mean, he didn't even know 1% of the problems he was going to run into with Cloud Kitchens. Because you've got to really get into it. What tech guy knows anything about real estate? Zero. The whole thing is a learning process, and the best entrepreneurs can take those learnings and multiply the applications. It's just a very rare thing. Look, the fact that Elon has taken SpaceX to Starlink to data centers in space, nobody would start there or think about that. The first Roadster is now Optimus. Letting the imagination flourish is a beautiful thing. Understanding what the core principles are and applying them. With that said, paint us a picture about the future of atoms, or maybe say some of the guiding principles that will dictate which industries you enter next or how you think about where you go from here. Look, I tell people I'm very focused right now because it's only food, mining, and transport. There's so many things I haven't done yet; I'm so constrained. But it goes back to that thing I was saying before: you've got to find that beachhead, make it flourish, build management capacity to solve 98% of the problems that are going on, and then you have the room to go do a new thing. Really, it's the marathon analogy. At some point you might be doing a business and it just starts working. I started seeing that on rides at Uber where I had my leads in different geos and I'd call them up because we would jam on hard ideas and crank, but we didn't have much to talk about because it just worked. They were like, "Travis, we got this. I don't know why you're calling me." Actually, this brings up one of the things he did best at Uber: having to deal with these guys. If you look at most big tech companies that emerged in the 2000s and 2010s, the management team were professional middle managers. He had so many guys where you would go, "No, that's a founder," that's an actual entrepreneur. It's amazing that he's not building his own company because he's working for Travis, but that enabled them to get to a multitude of ideas. You need a team like that, and there's not that many people who can build that. It's very hard to attract and maintain that level of capability. Unless you're building something absolutely spectacular, they're not going to stay, and even if you are, you have to be able to manage that. The management of it becomes an empowerment puzzle. I'm running Uber, let's say I have five continents going at any given time. You have an engineering team that's multiplexed across a finance team, multiplexed across a product. There's a lot going on, and the only way it works is if you have that entrepreneur there, but they have to have alignment. Empowerment starts with alignment. Are we aligned on the strategy? It's alignment on the front end, accountability on the back end. We would call this "let builders build." Alignment is multi-dimensional because it's alignment on goals and objectives, but also on culture—what is the behavior you guys are doing over there? Because that's going to affect us over here. We can't dilute the culture because we gave you autonomy. That combination is a very high level of management skill. That leader has to be good enough to do it, and they have to work with the CFO, the head of product, and engineering to make sure it works. If I'm a single point of failure, it isn't scaling on 24 time zones. There's no way. I think that's the difference between what we did at Uber versus how a lot of other companies are expanding internationally. They're buying things. I was like, "We could just do this. A city's a city. Let's go." If you buy it, guess what? You brought in another culture, another reputation, and that reputation is going to seep back. Then you don't have a culture. You're left wondering, "How are they getting away with that in Korea when we can't get away with that in the US?" By the way, your technology is a reflection of your culture. The product decisions are made, and now you have to fuse these products and technologies together, which is very hard. Two years later, maybe. That's why I didn't buy Lyft. That was a very different culture. So you're constrained now by three different industries. What is the criteria that's going to determine if and when you go into another? It's the beachhead thing. Back to the marathon analogy: if it's getting easy, you must make it hard again. But you have to get it to easy first. It's a feeling where I'm constantly creating problems. I'm a problem creator. Creating a problem might be, "Hey, let's do Uber in China." Okay, that's creating a real problem. And you don't have a full understanding of that problem when you create it. Why is that hard? Because if you create a problem, you have to solve it. I have this framework I call the meta-problem: the derivative of your problem creation must be less than or equal to the derivative of your problem solving. If it's not, you're underwater. When you create a problem, you're guessing at what your capacity is to solve problems, 6 to 18 months ahead, with only a certain understanding of the nature of the problem. When you get it really wrong, you're underwater, and then you must stop all problem creation so that you can get back above water. When you're at your waist in terms of how underwater you are, you think, "Oh, I could get to here," and you create more problems. You let the problem creation spot open. When you talk about capacity, a lot of it is his personal capacity because he has to be able to—if he's creating a new large problem—not pay attention to the same level of detail in the other parts of his business. It's not just the capacity of the team; it's also personal capacity. I never felt like rideshare was solved because what happened? Oh, it's time for autonomy. Okay, all right, let's go. If you had still been running Uber in this other universe, could you have gone to mining with Uber? It's a continuation of the same vision. Digitizing the physical world, the atoms-based computation—that was already set. If the future of food is about infrastructure, automated production, and automated logistics, that's the future of Uber Eats, too. It doesn't mean when you're an OEM and you make a food computer, you have to make all the parts, but the future is the same. Once you have that framework, it tells you what the future is going to be; it's correct and it's compelling. I built like 500 Cloud Kitchens facilities around the world. You could just look at it as, "Why are you building kitchens?" Of course, I would sort of go around and tell people, "I'm just your local humble kitchen builder," just sort of messing around. But when you're hiring, it's like, why does this matter? It's a food computer. It points the way towards where the interesting things are and helps you describe this to top-quality talent who now can see what you see. There's another interesting thing we went through understanding the business: unlike a computer where you have storage that becomes obsolete, in the physical storage world, if you have the computer optimizing the storage, the storage becomes more valuable. You build the food computer, and the storage is essential to deliver the end service. That food computer multiplies the value of the real estate. It's weird. Imagine a computer that multiplied the value of its storage. It's like in AI, where data has become valuable in a way that it wasn't really before. Think of it this way: we go to a data center, that's real estate. How many operations are now happening at a data center today versus 20 years ago? The value of a data center in terms of what it provides society is going up dramatically. There are a lot of happy data center owners right now because of it. Speaking of painting this vision to talent and where the future is going, talk more about the emerging category that Adams sits within, which you're calling industrial AI. I did a vision letter when we launched Adams that outlined a vision for physical AI and where we sit in it. I went into what a physical AI stack is; it was philosophy about where the tech industry in the physical world is going. But I didn't get to the brass tacks. When you look at software—bits—affecting atoms, how they're assembled, where they go, what function they do in the physical world, we wanted to create an easy way to understand what it means. Adam's mission: physical automation to transform industries. That kind of gets you there, but what does it mean? It means we take entire industries that are ripe for automation and AI and completely transform them. In a sense, you've had one idea, like Uber, and Adams is the evolution. You said you're the Cisco of the physical world, and you extended that vision into the computer. But the computer is around an industry. We make computers for industries. Those are industry-specific computers, but generalized networks in some sense. Why are we doing transport? Because you've got to have wheelbase for robots. Every industry is moving things, so you must have wheelbase as part of what they do if you want to automate the full stack. Peter Thiel a decade ago famously talked about how we've had so much progress in bits but not in atoms. Here we are, finally getting to it in a real way. Entrepreneurs coming up since the Uber time are not scared of regulation. That was a breakthrough in a large sense. Once bits start controlling atoms in the physical world, the regulators are in the game big time. Generally, regulation is about our physical world; that's how it's been for thousands of years. Once you get in the physical world, you say, "When in the past have we seen something like this?" There are so many parallels between the Carnegies, the Rockefellers, the Fords, and the massive, rapid change they dealt with at that moment. The political climate is very similar. The New Dealers were not that different from the current in terms of their complete hatred of the industrialists. There's a guy, Frick, who was Carnegie's partner, dealing with steel. An anarchist broke into his office in the early 1900s, shot him in the neck, and he got it stitched up and went back to work that day. You get the vibe of what's going on and how those guys rolled. Not all entrepreneurs today roll that way; the "bits" guys don't necessarily, but the "atoms" guys do. There's an interesting parallel to what's happening now with the coming industrial age—the Atoms Age. There's a book called *Freedom's Forge* about the building of the war machine by the industrialists, which won us World War II, and the resistance they got. This guy Bill Knudsen, who was a complete badass, figured out mass production at Ford, went to GM, built Chevy, then went to work for the government for a dollar to industrialize the nation. The way he got fought by the press and Eleanor Roosevelt—everybody was out to kill this guy because he proposed "cost plus 7%." That kind of resistance is very similar to what we're seeing in data centers today. There's a lot of parallels politically, which I would call resistance to change. The most important truth-seeking is the seeking of valuable unknown truths. But if you're good at finding those, you're creating change. The faster the change comes, the more nature will do what it does: resistance. You have to figure out how to bring progress to overwhelm that resistance and build trust as best as you can so you have more advocates versus adversaries. Everybody loves progress; it's change they can't stand. On industrial AI, my whole vision note was about physical AI as a theoretical thing. Industrial AI is basically this stack of software, sensors, robotics, and machinery that automates an industry. One industry at a time. It became much more real and clear. People hear "physical AI" and think, "Oh, so you're doing a humanoid?" I'm like, "No, we're the non-humanoid guys. I don't have a problem with them, but that's not what we do." It's clean about what we're about. One thing we completely forgot how to do was manufacture. We were the best in the world, we taught the world, and now we've forgotten how to do it—other than Elon. If you need someone who knows how to manufacture, you have to get them from Elon. We're going to have to relearn that. Data centers are a manufacturing problem to a large extent. There's so much resistance to that from the exact people who were harping for years about how manufacturing jobs are going away. They're back, but we don't want them. Thank God there are still places welcoming data centers; the federal system is a wonderful thing because of the competition between states. Talk to us about the team you're building now. We just multiplied the number of industries we're going after by three. Each is a multi-trillion dollar opportunity. It's hard to hire rich people, so I've never had great success there. Emil Michael was my right hand on epic deal-making; I need the next Emil Michael. We're in the market for a general counsel, someone who can ride shotgun. I like to go to the line; we should be a few inches off the line. I need a CEO for my mining business, and I need technical leadership across the board—autonomy, heavy-duty robotics. The talent game is the game. Because we were stealth for so long, we weren't in the flow; we were cold-calling. Being out there now lets people understand who we are and allows me to put teams together that can do great work. If you're joining now, you're joining with the benefit of the lessons learned with Cloud Kitchens and Uber, but early enough that there's enormous upside. You can do something super meaningful—save kids from working in the mines, make it so everybody can have healthy food, give people their time back while saving money. It's a hell of a mission. We're having a lot of fun.

Alphabet 2026 Q2 Earnings Call

Alphabet Investor Relations(61.7K)·61:58·2026-07-22
Sundar PichaiCEO, Alphabet Anat AshkenaziCFO, Alphabet
TL;DRAlphabet reported Q2 2026 revenue of $119.8 billion, up 24% year-over-year, driven by strong momentum in Cloud (up 82%) and Search (up 17%).
%). Despite operating income of $40.8 billion, capital expenditures rose to $44.9 billion for the quarter, leading to a negative free cash...

TL;DR

Alphabet reported Q2 2026 revenue of $119.8 billion, up 24% year-over-year, driven by strong momentum in Cloud (up 82%) and Search (up 17%). Despite operating income of $40.8 billion, capital expenditures rose to $44.9 billion for the quarter, leading to a negative free cash flow of $5.9 billion as the company accelerates technical infrastructure investments to meet surging AI demand.

Financial Performance & Capital Allocation

  • Consolidated Financials:
    • Revenue reached $119.8 billion, up 24% year-over-year (23% in constant currency), marking the 12th consecutive quarter of double-digit revenue growth.
    • Operating income grew 30% to $40.8 billion with an operating margin of 34%.
    • Net income and earnings per share increased significantly, driven primarily by unrealized gains in the equity securities portfolio ($98 billion in other income/expenses).
    • Operating cash flow was $39.1 billion for Q2 and $185.7 billion for the trailing 12 months.
    • Cash and marketable securities ended at $242.5 billion, including $87.1 billion of marketable equity securities; long-term debt stood at $98.2 billion.
  • Capital Expenditures & Cash Flow:
    • Q2 CapEx was $44.9 billion, with ~60% spent on servers and 40% on data centers and networking equipment.
    • Free cash flow was negative $5.9 billion in Q2 due to high CapEx investments.
    • The Board declared a quarterly cash dividend of $0.22 per share, payable in September.
  • Capital Structure Philosophy:
    • Operating cash flow is the primary funding source, supplemented by debt expansion (growing the debt portfolio from ~$16 billion to ~$100 billion over the past 12 months).
    • Equity was raised recently to maintain a resilient balance sheet, though Alphabet does not plan to return to equity markets outside of an "at-the-market" (ATM) offering dedicated to covering stock-based compensation taxes.

Google Cloud & Infrastructure Momentum

  • Segment Results:
    • Cloud revenue surged 82% to $24.8 billion, with core GCP growing faster than the Cloud overall.
    • Cloud operating income more than tripled year-over-year to $8.8 billion, with operating margin expanding from 20.7% to 35.6%.
    • Google Cloud backlog increased by over $50 billion sequentially to $514 billion, with just over 50% expected to be recognized as revenue over the next 24 months.
  • Enterprise & Token Adoption:
    • Nearly 90% of Fortune 100 companies use Gemini Enterprise.
    • Nearly 500 cloud customers each processed more than 1 trillion tokens in the last year; over 2,000 enterprises consumed over 100 billion tokens over the last 12 months.
    • Gemini Enterprise Adoption: Agent Development Kit reached nearly 70 million total downloads in Q2. Named customers include PepsiCo, Intel, HSBC, Bell Canada, Macy's, and Signal Aduna.
  • AI Infrastructure & Hardware:
    • Broad range of accelerators offered, including Google TPUs (AT and ATI) and Nvidia platforms (including the new Nvidia Verarummin platform).
    • Virgo Network: Connects a million AI accelerators across multiple data center sites into a unified supercomputer.
    • Axion CPU: Agent-optimized CPU providing 30% better performance per dollar compared to peer offerings.
    • TPU System Sales: Alphabet began recognizing revenues from TPU system deliveries to customer data centers in Q2.

Google Services & Search Performance

  • Segment Results:
    • Google Services revenues rose 15% to $94.5 billion.
    • Search and other advertising revenues grew 17% to $63.3 billion, led by retail and finance.
    • YouTube advertising revenues increased 13% to $11.1 billion, driven by direct response and brand advertising.
    • Network advertising revenues fell 1% to $7.3 billion.
    • Subscription, platforms, and devices revenues increased 15% to $12.9 billion, driven by YouTube subscriptions (Music and Premium) and Google One (AI plans).
  • Search & AI Integration:
    • AI mode surpassed 1 billion monthly active users globally since its launch last October, driving incremental search queries and billions of weekly clicks to websites.
    • AI overviews and AI mode were combined into one seamless search experience.
    • Gemini-powered shopping ads drove a 20% improvement in showing highly relevant ads.
    • Cost of AI mode responses was reduced to its lowest level since launch.
  • YouTube Scale & Monetization:
    • FIFA World Cup 2026 became the most-viewed World Cup in YouTube history, with over 1.7 billion unique viewers globally watching related videos, and over 550 million watching on televisions.
    • Ask YouTube: Over 140 million users engaged with conversational AI summaries on the watch page in June 2026.
    • YouTube subscriptions are growing faster than ads, led by YouTube Music and Premium.

Ecosystem, Developers, & Other Bets

  • Model Ecosystem & Developer Traction:
    • Released Gemini 3.6 Flash, 3.5 Flashlight, and 3.5 Flash Cyber (paired with code mender agent). Pre-training has begun for the ambitious Gemini 4 base model.
    • Model APIs are processing approximately 22 billion tokens per minute (up from 16 billion last quarter). Over 9 million developers build each month.
    • Jemma Family: Open models downloaded over 900 million times total, with latest Jemma 4 models downloaded over 300 million times since April.
    • Antigravity: Agentic development platform with over 2.4 million weekly active users.
    • Gemini App: Reached 950 million monthly active users, with daily active users tripling over the last year. Daily video-creation active users on the app increased 40% since May.
  • Ads & Commerce Tools:
    • AI Max: Adopted by half a million advertisers, delivering an average of 15% more conversions or value on search at a similar ROAS. Triple A Autoclop Enterprises saw a 17% conversion volume improvement and 11% cost-per-lead decrease using AI Max.
    • Over half of global SMB customers use AI to create or optimize ad creatives.
  • Other Bets:
    • Waymo: Introduced the newest vehicle, OHA, to public riders, powered by the sixth-generation Waymo driver.
    • Wing: Safely completed over 1 million home deliveries through partnerships with Walmart, DoorDash, and Papa Johns.
    • Isomorphic Labs: Raised over $2 billion to power its AI drug design engine.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) / Sundar Pichai (CEO) on CapEx Guidance: Alphabet updated its full-year 2026 CapEx guidance range to $195 billion to $205 billion (up from the previous estimate of $180 billion to $190 billion), driven by an acceleration in capacity delivery. CapEx is expected to increase significantly further in 2027.
  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) on Q3 Revenue FX & Growth Lapping: At current spot rates, a slight foreign exchange headwind is expected for consolidated revenue in Q3 (compared to a 1 percentage point tailwind in Q2), impacting Search and YouTube ads primarily. In Q3, Alphabet will also begin lapping an acceleration in Search performance that began in Q3 of the prior year.
  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) on TPU Revenue Recognition: A relatively small portion of revenues from existing TPU system sales agreements will be recognized in 2026, ramping as the company exits 2026, with the vast majority of revenues realized in 2027.
  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) on Cloud Margin Pressures: Due to supply constraints, Alphabet plans to expand the use of third-party capacity in Q3 as a bridging strategy, which will create modest near-term margin pressure for Google Cloud. Near-term P&L pressure will also persist from higher depreciation and energy costs, ongoing hiring in AI and Cloud, marketing investments, and the integration headwinds from the Wiz acquisition in 2026.
  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) on Cloud Backlog Realization: Just over 50% of the total $514 billion cloud backlog is expected to be recognized as revenue over the next 24 months.
  • Anat Ashkenazi (CFO) on ATM Equity Offerings: Alphabet does not plan to return to the equity markets for funding, with the sole exception of continuing its at-the-market (ATM) equity offering for some period of time to address the tax obligations on stock-based compensation (SBC).

Notable Quotes

"We've started our most ambitious pre-training run yet for Gemini 4 and are excited by the progress we are seeing at the frontier." - Sundar Pichai on model development

>

"Our cloud backlog grew to $514 billion... Just over 50% of the total backlog [is expected] as revenue over the next 24 months." - Sundar Pichai and Anat Ashkenazi on cloud demand

>

"We are updating our full-year 2026 CAPEX guidance range to $195 to $205 billion, up from our previous estimate of $180 to $190 billion." - Anat Ashkenazi on capital expenditure acceleration

Our momentum starts with search, where people are adopting one seamless search experience across AI overviews and AI mode. We saw 17% revenue growth in search and other and YouTube ads grew 13%. Cloud revenue grew 82% powered by strong demand for AI infrastructure and AI solutions. And cloud backlog grew to $514 billion. It's great to see the wide adoption of Gemini Enterprise with nearly 90% of Fortune 100 using it. Yesterday, we announced new models, Gemini 3.6 Flash and 3.5 Flashlight, which are cost-effective and highly efficient. We are seeing tons of demand for our workhorse Gemini Flash series because it hits the sweet spot of performance and cost. We also launched Gemini 3.5 Flash Cyber, which I'm really excited about. Paired with our code mender agent, it finds and fixes vulnerabilities and delivers performance at the frontier, compatible to far bigger cyber models. Gemini 3.5 Flash Pro is currently in testing and our team is already building the next generation of models. We've started our most ambitious pre-training run yet for Gemini 4 and are excited by the progress we are seeing at the frontier. Demand for our models is translating to strong token usage across developers and enterprise customers and we continue to be supply constrained, a sign of momentum and rapid adoption. More than 9 million developers are building each month with our models across our APIs and key developer products. Our model APIs are now processing approximately 22 billion tokens per minute. That's up from 16 billion just a quarter ago. Additionally, this quarter, we launched Omni. It allows users to create anything from any input, starting with video. Since launching at IO in May, there's been a 40% increase in daily active users creating videos on the Gemini app. Our JEMMA family of open models, small enough to run on local devices, are hugely popular. These models have been downloaded over 900 million times and our latest Jemma 4 models have been downloaded over 300 million times since launching in April. Our agentic development platform, Antigravity, allows anyone to build in the agent first era. It has more than 2.4 million weekly active users. Antigravity is a powerful tool for users and enterprises and it's completely accelerated how we build internally. As just one example, a team in Chrome is now on track to accelerate delivery by eight times, compressing a two-year timeline into three months through model-driven refactoring. Moving on to our global product footprint, which brings AI to more people than any other company. Starting with search, AI continues to drive an expansionary moment, with new experiences resonating with users and driving growth in queries. As a big football fan, I was particularly excited to see search usage hit an all-time high during the World Cup this year. This really highlights how much people turn to Google in moments that matter. We continue to make search more helpful and intuitive. We recently brought together AI overviews and AI mode into one seamless search experience that combines our frontier capabilities with the best of the web. And we are continuing to incorporate more frontier capabilities into search with agents, personal intelligence, and notebooks. Our AI-powered features are driving increased search usage. Since expanding AI mode globally last October, we've surpassed 1 billion monthly active users. And just like AI overviews, AI mode is driving an incremental increase in search queries overall and we are now sending billions of clicks to websites every week through AI features in search. As we serve more of these queries, we've continued to drive efficiencies. Thanks to our engineering and hardware optimizations, this quarter, we reduced the cost of AI mode responses to its lowest level since launch, even as we've brought more advanced AI capabilities. Phillip will also talk more about how we are innovating the ads experience. Next, the Gemini app, which now has 950 million monthly active users, with daily active users tripling in the last year. Users love new agentic features like daily brief and our personalized agent, Gemini Spark, which is now available in the US and internationally. We've been shipping helpful new features like this at an incredible clip. On to YouTube. Month after month, when a big event happens in the world, the world turns to YouTube. Our continued momentum is driven by our integrated AI portfolio consisting of chips, models, data, security, and agent platforms, all designed to work together. Gemini continues to be a key driver of growth and is deeply integrated across all of our cloud products, including Gemini Enterprise, data analytics, cyber security, and Google Workspace. We are seeing strong, diversified demand across products, customers, geographies, and industries. Our product differentiation is driving expansion in three ways. One of the strongest parts of our growth comes from the rapid adoption of our Gemini Enterprise platform. It's differentiated with easy-to-use tools to build agents and automate processes, connectivity to enterprise systems, cost management, and governance tools. In Q2, Agent Development Kit, our framework for building and deploying enterprise AI agents, reached nearly 70 million total downloads. As I said, nearly 90% of Fortune 100 are using Gemini Enterprise. More broadly, Gemini is transforming how millions of businesses use AI to build custom agents, automate processes, improve cyber security, manage customer relationships, streamline data analytics, collaborate effectively, and more. All of this momentum is driving growth in our paid token usage. Nearly 500 cloud customers have each processed more than 1 trillion tokens in the last year and usage is so much deeper than that. Over the last 12 months, more than 2,000 enterprises consumed over 100 billion tokens. We are also seeing strong interest in our AI-powered security platform, which is differentiated because it integrates threat intelligence, cyber response prioritization with Vess, AI-automated scanning, code remediation, and monitoring. Keynote by Maor Shlomo, Founder & CEO of Base44 | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·13:04·5 views·2026-07-22
Maor ShlomoFounder & CEO, Base44
TL;DRBase44, an 18-month-old vibe coding platform, scaled to over $150 million in ARR with zero outside capital by operating primarily as a one-person team leveraging AI agents—leading to its recent acquisition by Wix.
Founder & CEO Maor Shlomo attributes this hyper-growth to...

TL;DR

Base44, an 18-month-old vibe coding platform, scaled to over $150 million in ARR with zero outside capital by operating primarily as a one-person team leveraging AI agents—leading to its recent acquisition by Wix. Founder & CEO Maor Shlomo attributes this hyper-growth to organizational restructuring around "teams of one," a strict 30% rule for building internal tools, and compounding ambitions.

Company Background & Growth Metrics

  • Base44 Metrics:
    • Age: 18 months old
    • Revenue: Over $150 million in ARR
    • Funding: $0 raised from investors
    • Growth Rate: Grew 50x over the past year
    • Team Structure: For most of its existence, operated as a one-person company (Maor Shlomo plus agents handling engineering, marketing, and support)
    • Exit: Recently acquired by Wix
    • Execution Speed: Pushes code to production hundreds of times a day; has 41 direct reports reporting to the CEO; manages end-to-end billing with a single person
  • Origin Story:
    • Started as a casual experiment by Maor Shlomo to see how far he could push AI development without following traditional startup playbooks
    • Named "Base44" after cycling through numbers to find an available domain name when base.com was taken
    • Previous Venture: Shlomo co-founded Explorium eight years prior, which followed the traditional VC playbook of heavy hiring, layers of management, and functional silos (product, design, engineering)
    • The ChatGPT Disruption: The 2023 shift to LLMs broke Explorium's traditional market; the traditional multi-layered corporate structure proved too slow to pivot effectively, prompting Shlomo's post-burnout realization that the old startup playbook was obsolete

Organizational Structure: Teams of One

  • Individual Conductors (ICs):
    • Traditional ICs ("individual contributors") have been redefined as "individual conductors" who partner with AI agents to deliver end-to-end value without cross-team dependencies
  • Flat and Lean Scaling:
    • Base44 avoids management layers, communication bottlenecks, and traditional communication friction
    • Mirrors broader industry trends where top research labs pay hundreds of millions of dollars for outlier individuals rather than assembling massive engineering headcounts

The 30% Rule & Working on Working

  • Reinvesting Time:
    • 30% of the team's time is reinvested directly into building custom tools, automations, and agents that accelerate workflows
  • Exponential Productivity:
    • Rather than relying on generic software that competitors also use, Base44 builds personalized tools on its own vibe coding platform tailored specifically to its operational bottlenecks
  • Custom Tooling Examples:
    • Built proprietary task management platforms optimized for daily-only meetings (Base44 maintains a strict "no meetings" policy outside of a single morning check-in)
    • Developed custom tools for managing experiments, production agents, content writing, and enterprise sales/go-to-market agents

Compounding Ambitions

  • Mindset Shift:
    • AI dramatically increases operational throughput, meaning leadership must recalibrate how ambitious their product roadmap can be instead of merely executing the same backlog faster
  • Recent Output Highlights:
    • Trained and released a proprietary LLM within a few months
    • Launched a mobile app for Base44
    • Extended capabilities to website games, presentation slides, agents, and automations

Notable Quotes

"I tried everything. We had to go back to being a very lean startup mode... And this was a company that just followed the right playbook, the playbook of how to build a startup before 2023." — *Maor Shlomo*
"Before the age of AI, we were building companies to be efficient teams that communicate well and have the right structure. I think now everything is changing. I think now the way I like it to look at organizations is we should build them around teams of one." — *Maor Shlomo*
"Every day there's like a new stuff, but I think the key thing that unlocked our own productivity is looking at our own bottlenecks and understanding what are the things that we should build customized solutions for that are going to accelerate 10x than the existing solutions." — *Maor Shlomo*
Okay, cool. So, yeah, thank you for the introduction here. We've been growing quite fast at Base44, but I think we're doing it in quite a unique and special way that has definitely taught me a lot about what it takes to build a company at this stage and what it actually means to build a company around AI. So, I'm going to speak about this and some of the learnings that we had. I know it sounds like a bit of a cringey LinkedIn post, but I promise I'll make it as interesting as I can. Before I go ahead, just a quick introduction to Base44. We're a vibe coding platform. We're 18 months old. We grew quite fast, so we're doing now over $150 million in ARR. We were recently acquired by Wix. I think all of those are nice stats, but the interesting piece is we raised $0 from investors. And I think, more interestingly, for most of its life, Base44 was actually a one-person operation. It was just me and a bunch of agents doing as much as I could—engineering, marketing, support, and not doing a lot of sleep. The reason it was like that is because Base44 actually started as just an experiment. It wasn't ever supposed to be this serious company or a fast-growing one. It was just me experimenting with how far away I can go with AI. The company still operates in this way; we're very flat, we're not a lot of people, and we're moving very, very fast. I'll speak about some of the principles that we have internally, but before I do, I want to provide some background on how and why I got to build a company with just me and a bunch of agents and why I decided to structure it this way. Eight years ago, we started a company called Explorium that went the usual venture capital playbook. We raised a lot of money, we had some decent growth, I hired a lot of people, I hired VPs, and we had layers of management and dependency. We had product people, designers, and software engineers—a really big team. Everything had been going pretty great, at least for the first five or six years of the company, until ChatGPT hit. Overnight, our market completely changed, and we had to pivot fast. The champions in organizations that we were selling into were not listening anymore. Everything was around LLMs and AI. The things that they were willing to pay for in 2021 just wasn't the case anymore. I did everything I could to pivot the company fast and figure out product-market fit again to try and find what new products we were going to offer. Then, I stumbled upon one of the things that I think humbled me the most in my career: the company that I had built following the playbook—the company that I thought was the right structure—wasn't moving fast enough. I tried everything. We had to go back to being a very lean startup. We had talented people, but every decision had to trickle down layers of management. Everything had to be tested, there were many dependencies between the teams, and communication was broken. This was a company that followed the "right" playbook, the one used to build a startup before 2023. Long story short, I stabilized the company, burned out a little bit, took a step back, and tried to rethink everything. I wanted to see what it would look like if I ignored everything I was taught about building a startup and instead focused on the shape of a new company in these crazy times with AI. That's how I got to this experiment called Base44. It started as an experiment to see what it means to build a company around AI and to throw away every lesson in company building. I wanted it to be called base.com, but obviously, base.com was taken. So, I started throwing numbers until I got to a free domain, and that's how Base44 came to life. The idea was pretty simple: let's build a company that can move as fast as I can, even if it's just myself. Let's remove anything else from the table regarding how company structure should look and see where we can get to. We're still operating by those principles. We're a very small team, we're very flat, and it's been going pretty well for us. In the past year, we've grown 50x, and we're pushing to production hundreds of times a day. I'm going to speak about three things. In the life cycle of the company, we tried a bunch of different things that we thought were going to be interesting and work. Many of them didn't, but I'll try to crystallize the top three things that I feel were the right choices that helped us move this fast and get these results. The first thing—and I think this is the biggest difference in how I think of organizations these days—is that before the age of AI, we were building companies to be efficient teams that communicate well and have the right structure. Now, I think we should build them around teams of one. Teams of one means one person working with a lot of agents. The idea is to get those unique individuals that can run far and allow them the ability to do things end-to-end with no dependencies. Inside Base44, we are building the organization around ICs. I think "IC" used to mean individual contributor, but these days it is probably better defined as "individual conductor" because every person works with AI agents and is able to deliver way more. To give an example of how things look inside Base44, I have 41 direct reports, which I think two or three years ago would have been an organizational disaster and against everything a management playbook tells you. Another interesting example is that we have one person who runs our billing end-to-end. Think about it: a company doing $150 million in ARR, moving very, very fast, with one person who knows the billing system end-to-end and manages that. I don't think Base44 is a freak show; I think it's a preview of how some companies are going to be built in the future. If organizations were previously built around efficient teams and good communication structures, I think the new organizations are built around over-delivering individuals. You can see this in the market. Look at those famous stories about top research labs paying hundreds of millions of dollars for researchers, where they could have taken that money to hire hundreds of different engineers and researchers. I don't think it's only me; it's a preview of how organizations will look, where every person today, with the right tools, can deliver 10x, 100x, or 1000x for the organization and really move the needle. The second thing is what we internally call the 30% rule, or "working on working." Basically, it means 30% of our time is invested back into building tools, automations, and agents that accelerate our work. This is a relic from when Base44 was just me. I was fighting teams 100x bigger than my size. If I used the same tools they did, or if I waited for the next version of a tool I was using, I would probably only get the same productivity gains as them. So, I constantly tried to free up time to build stuff customized to my needs, to identify bottlenecks, and to accelerate with better tools. The time I got back, I invested into even better tools. To some degree, it's exponential. If you buy time with AI and invest it into better tools, you get even more time back. We're seeing this exponential growth, and it's been working pretty well for us. For us, it's an easy job because we're a vibe coding platform, so we build a lot of our tools on top of our platform. For instance, we built our own task management platform, not because there were no good tools out there, but because we could build something highly customized to our specific workflow. For example, at Base44, we have no meetings other than a daily meeting in the morning. Everything in our task flow and product is centered around that. This is one of the things that vibe coding allows us to do. We use tools to manage experiments, to run production agents, to write content, and to handle sales. We have go-to-market agents that help us sell to the enterprise. I think the key for teams that want to move faster than the competition is realizing you can't just use the same tools everyone else uses. There are a lot of cool tools today, but the key thing that unlocked our productivity was identifying our own bottlenecks and understanding where we should build customized solutions that are 10x better than existing options. The last thing, which is less of a trick and more for those of you who are product leaders or building organizations, is a mindset change. Even if you don't do the things I mentioned, AI is going to save you a lot of time. It's going to enable you to do much, much more. The biggest mindset shift for me is asking: how much more ambitious can I be? What are the biggest things I can do now instead of just doing the same old things? AI will make you super fast, and you can leverage that to build the same thousand features you planned for the year. For us, it's about thinking bigger. Consider the stuff we've done in the past few months that would have been crazy to think about a year or two ago. We have our own LLM that we trained recently. We released a mobile app for Base44. We've extended our platform into website games, slides, agents, and automations. It's just me constantly trying to push myself and the team to think bigger because, whether you like it or not, AI is going to be incredible for increasing throughput. The key is to ask how ambitious we can be. That's what we call compounding ambitions. In conclusion, the three biggest things that changed how we operate are: first, think about your organization as teams of one—unique individuals who can go far—and build around them. Second, the 30% rule: invest the productivity gains you get back into customized, personalized tools for your organization. Third, when AI enables those productivity gains, think about the biggest thing you can possibly do for your product and how ambitious you can be. Thank you so much.

Tesla Q2 2026 Financial Results and Q&A Webcast

Tesla(3.0M)·69:55·90.6K views·2026-07-22
Elon MuskCEO, Tesla Vaibhav TanejaCFO, Tesla
TL;DRTesla reported record Q2 2026 vehicle deliveries and a massive 53% sequential increase in energy storage deployments (13.5 GWh), driven by surging demand for Full Self-Driving (FSD) and energy infrastructure, even as capital expenditures doubled to…
over $25 billion annualized...

TL;DR

Tesla reported record Q2 2026 vehicle deliveries and a massive 53% sequential increase in energy storage deployments (13.5 GWh), driven by surging demand for Full Self-Driving (FSD) and energy infrastructure, even as capital expenditures doubled to over $25 billion annualized and operating expenses rose due to R&D for Robotaxi, Optimus, and AI chips.

Financial Results & Business Performance

  • Automotive Metrics:
      • Achieved record Q2 deliveries with sequential growth across the Americas (60%), APAC (27%), and EMEA (12%), exiting the quarter with the largest order backlog since 2023.
      • Model Y set records in key markets including the Netherlands, Australia, and New Zealand.
      • Automotive gross margins (excluding regulatory credits) declined sequentially from 19.2% to 16.3%, impacted by higher interest rate subvention costs and the non-repetition of Q1 warranty true-downs ($230M) and tariff benefits — Vaibhav Taneja
  • Energy Storage Business:
      • Deployed 13.5 GWh of energy storage in Q2 (a 53% sequential increase), marking the second-largest quarter in the business's history — Vaibhav Taneja
      • Energy gross margins dropped from 39.5% to 20.4% due to a $240M legacy vendor cell warranty true-up, the absence of Q1 tariff benefits ($200M+), and declining industrial storage ASPs amid rising competition — Vaibhav Taneja
      • Long-term normalized energy margins are expected in the mid-to-low 20% range — Vaibhav Taneja
  • Service & Corporate Financials:
      • Service and other margins reached an all-time high of 14.1% (up from 9.2%), driven by used car volume, supercharging, service centers, insurance, and Robotaxi infrastructure investments — Vaibhav Taneja
      • Operating expenses grew sequentially due to R&D and pre-production ramp costs for the Semi, Optimus, Cybercab, litigation charges, and AI compute appreciation — Vaibhav Taneja
      • Net income was boosted by a $1B mark-to-market gain on SpaceX holdings, offset by $300M in FX losses and $100M in Bitcoin losses — Vaibhav Taneja
      • Capital expenditures more than doubled sequentially, keeping full-year 2026 CapEx guidance above $25B, with capacity to borrow up to $30B via new debt facilities — Vaibhav Taneja

Autonomy, FSD & Robotaxi Expansion

  • FSD Adoption & Monetization:
      • FSD attach rates reached nearly 1.5 million paid customers globally (55% upfront purchases, 45% subscriptions) — Vaibhav Taneja
      • In North America, 55% of Q2 deliveries had an FSD subscription enabled at delivery — Vaibhav Taneja
      • FSD is serving as a primary demand driver; the bulk of future FSD monetization growth will come from subscriptions as the standalone purchase option has been removed in most markets — Elon Musk, Vaibhav Taneja
  • Robotaxi Fleet & Safety Record:
      • The unsupervised Robotaxi program has driven over 380,000 miles across six cities in two states with zero notable safety incidents — Ashok Elluswamy
      • Weekly unsupervised miles are compounding at high double-digit growth rates; the fleet is expanding to new U.S. markets (including Florida and Texas) — Elon Musk, Ashok Elluswamy
      • Fleet is operating on early V15 FSD software builds (which have merged 40% of planned major improvement tracks) — Ashok Elluswamy
  • Cybercab & Regulatory Positioning:
      • Cybercab manufacturing targets are aligned with the projected growth rate of unsupervised miles; vehicles are currently accumulating chassis-specific driving data using retrofitted pedals and steering wheels — Ashok Elluswamy, Elon Musk
      • Tesla maintains a collaborative partnership with NHTSA and federal regulators (specifically administrator Morrison) regarding autonomous vehicle rules — Lars Moravy
      • Starlink connectivity is being integrated into Cybercabs and all Tesla vehicles to ensure continuous connectivity and eliminate cellular "dead zones" for Robotaxis — Elon Musk

Robotics: Optimus Development & Scaling

  • Manufacturing & Supply Chain Hurdles:
      • Optimus will be Tesla's hardest product ever to scale manufacturing due to the complete lack of an existing external supply chain for its parts, requiring massive in-housing and PCB work — Elon Musk
      • Production will follow a standard S-curve, but the initial phase will be flat and extended — Elon Musk
  • AI Training & Data Strategy:
      • Optimus leverages human form factor and dexterity to learn directly by observing factory workers and dedicated data collection teams, supported by internet-scale demonstrations — Ashok Elluswamy
      • The "Optimus Academy" provides reinforcement learning loops where bots practice, fail, and master tasks autonomously — Ashok Elluswamy
      • Optimus shares the same end-to-end AI strategy (pixels in, controls out) as FSD — Ashok Elluswamy

Infrastructure, Chips & Energy Expansion

  • Semiconductor Fabs & AI Compute:
      • Tesla has placed equipment orders for its Austin development fab, designed to integrate lithography, mask production, logic, memory, packaging, and chip testing under one roof — Elon Musk
      • Suppliers like Samsung and TSMC (building Fabs in Texas and Arizona) and Panasonic are investing tens of billions to support Tesla's AI compute and battery production — Elon Musk
  • Digital Optimus & Mega Pods:
      • "Digital Optimus" uses Tesla's low-cost AI computer to drive computer screens in real-time video, managed by SpaceX's Grok model — Elon Musk
      • "Mega Pods" combine Tesla AI4 computers with X86 architecture in portable boxes placed at Supercharger sites (which total ~7 GW of power) to decentralize AI compute and leverage distributed electricity — Elon Musk
  • Vertical Integration & Solar Build-out:
      • Tesla is pursuing a massive build-out of solar cell and panel manufacturing in the U.S. (scaling from silicon refinement to deployment) to support future AI data center and transport electrification energy needs — Elon Musk, Vaibhav Taneja

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • "We'll soon start production with Mega Pack 3." — Elon Musk
  • "Optimus will be the biggest product ever." — Elon Musk
  • "Optimus 4 will be much more vertically integrated... with an order of magnitude more production of Optimus 4 than Optimus 3. So, sort of aspirationally, 10 million units a year versus a million units a year of Optimus 3." — Elon Musk
  • "The next generation AI, AI board... either... a moderate improvement over AI 4, probably will reach its production around the middle of next year... and then it's also AI 5 which, hopefully, is in line production, around the middle of next year... AI 5 will initially go into Optimus." — Elon Musk
  • "I think the AI 6 chip is just so good... I think it's going to be the best edge computing chip in the world." — Elon Musk
  • "This is going to be a, a great year for Tesla, I think one of our best years ever, and then I think next year will be even better." — Elon Musk
  • "We expect that the time to launch to a new city will continue to trend towards zero, towards an end where we operate in entire states as a whole, instead of going by city-by-city." — Ashok Elluswamy
  • "We expect to um, do, get get self-driving working on the the the Tesla Semi, probably around the end of this year or early next year." — Elon Musk

Notable Quotes

*"The Tesla team has performed incredibly well. The sub cap has started production, and we also have started production and a ramping in, well, we will soon start production with the with, with Optimus. We have started production with the the the Tesla Semi truck. We'll soon start production with Mega Pack 3."* — Elon Musk

So, I'm, I'm for, not trying to aim for like some extremely high efficient efficiency capital spend because it, that would slow things down."* — Elon Musk

Our Q2 results were announced at about p.m. Central Time in the update deck we published at the same link as this webcast. Actual events or results could differ materially due to a number of risks and uncertainties, including those mentioned in our most recent filings with the SEC. During the question-and-answer portion of today's call, please limit yourself to one question and one follow-up. Please use the raise hand button to join the question queue. Before we jump into Q&A, Elon has some opening remarks. Elon? Oh, thank you. So, yeah, it's been a great quarter. We achieved a record Q2 deliveries. Model Y is now I think it's the best-selling car of any kind in the world, and is setting records across the board, so its popularity is increasing tremendously. And we're seeing in locations that have FSD approved, we're seeing a very high take rate of FSD. In fact, I think for a lot of people, they're actually buying Tesla full self-driving with a car attached, as opposed to a car with FSD. They're coming into our stores in the US and saying that they want the full self-driving, and with whatever car it comes with, essentially. So, clearly this is a significant demand driver, and as we get approval for FSD in different parts of different countries, I think we'll see a similar uptick in demand. So yeah, it's looking very good on that front. The energy business is also growing incredibly fast and I think will be crucial for the scale-up of artificial intelligence, the data centers. We're investing a lot in growing the core business and really preparing for the future. So, this is a massive CapEx year, but I'm confident that all the things that we're investing in are what will yield incredible returns. Really, maybe I think the best CapEx returns that we've ever seen. Just so you know, the Tesla team has performed incredibly well. The sub cap has started production. We also have started production and a ramping in—well, we will soon start production with Optimus. We have started production with the Tesla Semi truck. We'll soon start production with Mega Pack 3. We've started production with the Lithium refinery and the Cathode refinery. We also are scaling up our battery cell production, and we're also preparing to do a massive solar panel like solar cells and panel production. Actually, this is going all the way from silicon refinement, to producing the solar cell, and then the deployment of solar, because there's going to be tremendous need for electricity in the future due to electrification of transport and AI obviously. So, we're working on what we believe is the most ambitious build-out of advanced infrastructure and manufacturing capacity ever in history. It's a big deal. So, with robotaxi, our goals are very ambitious, but we do need to be cautious about causing any accidents or causing any harm to anyone. Although there are I think 30 to 40,000 automotive deaths per year in the United States alone, most of those do not generate any press; you never really read about almost any of those. But if we injure even one person, it will be worldwide headline news, and regulators will immediately clamp down on our activities. We don't want to injure anyone. So, we're going as fast as humanly possible in scaling robotaxi, while trying to ensure that we do not harm anyone at all, and ideally do not even run over a pet. That is really the constraint. We want to grow as fast as possible with Robotaxi without harm to anyone. We're providing live updates for Robotaxi launch as we open up in a city. We immediately post that on X. So if you just follow the Tesla account on X, we'll keep you informed as we scale to new cities. We've opened up in a number of cities in Florida and in Texas and obviously in the Bay Area. So, we'll continue to scale I think very rapidly—more than 10% a week in terms of miles driven. It's a very high compound growth rate. Optimus, you know, as you've heard me say before, I think Optimus will be the biggest product ever. But it is a very complex problem to solve. It's one of the hardest things to solve to make an autonomous humanoid robot that can do tasks; if you simply ask it to do something or show it a video, it can do the task without any programming. No one has ever achieved this. There are many challenges in the electromechanical design of the robot to achieve sufficient dexterity and reliability. It needs to be very reliable and have long wear and tear. That means it needs to be out in the field and not break down, otherwise you've got a 70 kg robot that just flopped over and you've got to carry it out like a body, you know, there's no wheels. We don't want Optimus to go haywire. It is a lot of work to scale, to get the design right, and to scale production. I really want to emphasize here that the production scaling challenge is very substantial. This is going to be the hardest product to scale manufacturing that we've ever made at Tesla, because everything on the robot is new. The difficulty of scaling the production ramp is proportionate to the newness of the parts in the robot. At least with electric cars, the non-electric powertrain portions of the cars had an existing supply chain. You could go to existing suppliers for wheels, side mirrors, windshield glass, and body panels. But with Optimus, there is no supply chain. We've had to build up a supply chain in its entirety, or in-house the production. We've actually in-housed a tremendous amount. The Optimus production line that we're building out in Fremont in place of what used to be the Model S/X production looks incredible. It's quite stunning to see. I want to be sure to calibrate people correctly. Optimus will follow the sort of normal S-curve of a manufacturing ramp, but the initial portion of the S-curve will be quite flat and long, because of the newness of the parts. You've probably seen lots of impressive demonstrations of robots on the internet, but those demonstrations you're seeing are pre-programmed or remote-controlled. There is no humanoid robot that is actually able to do generalized tasks. Optimus will be the first one that is capable of doing that, where it's not just a demo, it's genuinely useful in day-to-day life. Optimus is designed to have full human dexterity—a hand that has the same level of dexterity, if not higher than, a human hand. The human hand is an incredible thing. The more you study the human hand, the more amazing you realize how amazing hands are. It's more than just opposable thumbs; the nuances of how human hands work are amazing. The closer you look, the more amazed you are. Optimus will have that capability. It will have human and then superhuman dexterity. Truly going to be quite something. Terrafab: we expect to announce the location soon and provide more details about our plans in that regard. We will leave that to the launch announcement rather than try to squeeze it into an earnings call, because this is a very big announcement and it deserves to have its own day in the spotlight. I do think Terrafab is going to be an amazing initiative and a necessary one, without which we will be constrained in our ability to scale Optimus production, because we simply won't have enough AI chips. We'll have to solve memory, logic, and packaging in order to scale Optimus. We've placed equipment orders for our development fab in Austin. That development fab is pretty cool because it's intended to have lithography, mask production, and then logic, memory, and packaging and chip testing all under one roof. You can have a very fast iterative cycle and try out new chip designs very quickly and see if they work. I don't think such a building exists anywhere on earth. This is really going to be super helpful as we try some exciting, adventurous, high-risk, high-payoff bets on AI chips. This focus is primarily on Optimus—how to make it as useful as possible. Then we have Digital Optimus, which is basically driving a computer screen in the same way that you drive a car. For the car, it's pixels or photons in, and controls out. The same thing is true for Optimus and Digital Optimus: photons in, controls out. We feel confident that we can adapt the same Tesla AI technology that we developed for self-driving cars to have a self-driving computer screen. The Tesla AI for computers can handle all of the short-term tasks by doing real-time video control of the screen. It's not screenshots; everything is real-time video at high frame rate. Digital Optimus will be before physical Optimus because physical Optimus needs to be able to operate computers. It's got to have a generalized touchscreen and computer-use capability. That's looking promising, and this is integrated with SpaceX's Grok, the big model that acts as the manager of Digital Optimus and provides it with a series of tasks. We're also building out a mega pod design that has Tesla AI for computers with X86. It's a pairing—an X86 computer with the Tesla AI for computer in a box. It's kind of just Digital Optimus in a box, like the mega pack packaging. We put a large number of AI4 plus X86 combos in a giant box, and these boxes can be placed anywhere in a country or outside the country. This allows us to scale AI compute using aggregated electricity production. We have something like 7 gigawatts of power at our superchargers and it's growing. We can place Mega pods at many of these superchargers and have distributed power for AI. I think that could be quite a big deal. In conclusion, we're super excited about our autonomy and robotics roadmap. There's so much awesome stuff coming. It's hard to squeeze into an earnings call, but we'll have a lot of product announcements. This is going to be a great year for Tesla, I think one of our best years ever, and next year will be even better. In conclusion, I want to thank the Tesla team for their excellent execution and all of our supporters for joining us on this journey. Thank you. Great. Thanks so much, Elon. One of the main reasons for this approach is that having the human form factor and function allows us to learn from humans on how to perform a wide variety of tasks. This is all on top of any training we can do off of the entire internet of data that shows demonstrations of people doing various tasks, both in industrial settings and home settings. The second flywheel is when we have a large number of Optimus robots practicing their tasks in what we call the Optimus Academy. The data from the bots experiencing the tasks themselves will be invaluable and help us close any minor form factor gaps that may exist between the bot and the humans. This is also when the reinforcement learning loop kicks in where the bot initially attempts some task, fails sometimes, learns from both the successes and failures, and eventually learns to master those tasks at perhaps a superhuman level. Our AI strategy for Optimus is aligned with the same end-to-end strategy that drives FSD: pixels in, controls out. And just like FSD, we expect it to work broadly. There’s far more power available from the sun than anything else, by far. We think power constraints are going to be—they already are—a major issue for AI. Just turning on the AI computers, the AI compute demand is so high that even the so-called hyperscalers are having trouble turning on their AI compute, and finding the power, and then smoothing the power for the training runs where the power cycles dramatically in a very short period of time. You can have, during a training run, the power consumption drop by 70% for 100 milliseconds. You really need fast-acting advanced power electronics to be able to smooth out these massive changes in power, especially during their training runs. And that’s why SpaceX has bought so many Megapacks for the data centers. It’s actually mostly for smoothing out the power for the training runs. But it will also be useful for being able to tap more power from the grid by, if you tell utilities that they will not need to supply power during the worst hours or the worst days of the year because the batteries can handle it, then that makes it much easier for them to give you power. In fact, the probably the best way to scale total energy production in the United States is simply using batteries. Because there’s I think around 1.2 or 1.3 terawatts of power production in the US, but the average power usage is only half a terawatt. So, you’ve got two and a half times as much power generation as the average usage. That means you could potentially double the energy output of the United States just with batteries. So, we really see demand for Megapack being very, very high in the future. Alrighty. That is all the time we have for today for Q&A.
