AI Has a Memory Problem | Gelsinger, SK hynix & More | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·38:00·106 views·2026-07-22
Pat GelsingerPartner, Playground Global Hoshik KimSVP & Fellow, SK hynix Vijay ShilpiekandulaInvestor, Dilation Capital
TL;DR"HBM is a lousy…
AI demand is outstripping memory supply, creating a "memory wall" bottleneck where high-bandwidth memory (HBM) is thermally and bitwise inefficient yet remains the necessary stopgap until advanced stacked architectures arrive later this decade. Insatiable Token Demand:...

TL;DR: AI demand is outstripping memory supply, creating a "memory wall" bottleneck where high-bandwidth memory (HBM) is thermally and bitwise inefficient yet remains the necessary stopgap until advanced stacked architectures arrive later this decade.

The AI Memory Bottleneck: Why Memory is the Limit

  • Insatiable Token Demand: Pat Gelsinger noted a threefold problem: insatiable demand for AI, rapidly growing context windows and model sizes, and the necessity for extremely low first-token latency.
  • The von Neumann Architectural Tax: Vijay Shilpiekandula explained that modern AI inherits an 80-year-old von Neumann architecture where processors sit far from memory, creating a narrow highway (data bottleneck) between the computational "factory" and the memory "warehouse."
  • Systemic Memory Shift: Hoshik Kim stated that memory is no longer viewed merely as a commodity cost, but as a core investment and foundational infrastructure for AI data centers.

HBM's Limitations and the "Waffle Maker" Production Problem

  • HBM Imperfections: Pat Gelsinger called HBM a "lousy memory" that is thermally constrained (creating a thermal sandwich), shoreline bandwidth-limited, and bitwise inefficient (sacrificing four bits of traditional DRAM for every HBM bit created).
  • The "Waffle Squeeze": Andrew Homan used a waffle maker analogy to illustrate that manufacturing HBM is significantly more complex and slower than commodity DRAM, severely limiting output.
  • Compound Yield Losses: Hoshik Kim explained that HBM requires significantly larger die spaces (roughly three times bigger, known as "HBM squeeze") and stacks DRAM 12 to 16 levels high, resulting in compounding yield losses across front-end wafers, TSV stacking, and micro-bumping.

Industry Consolidation and Changing Business Models

  • Survival of the Fittest: Louis Delahaye highlighted that the memory industry has consolidated from roughly 20 players down to three primary leaders: SK hynix, Samsung, and Micron.
  • Custom Designs vs. Commodities: Andrew Homan pointed out that tight co-optimization between logic (GPUs) and memory makes suppliers harder to swap, moving HBM away from a pure commodity. Pat Gelsinger added that the shift toward custom stacked memory designs will disrupt traditional JEDEC-governed business models.

Geopolitical Realities and Supply Chain Vulnerabilities

  • Concentrated Geopolitics: Vijay Shilpiekandula noted that front-end memory is concentrated in Korea, packaging is in Taiwan, and NAND is in Japan.
  • Lack of Resilience: Pat Gelsinger warned that the tech supply chain lacks resilience, noting that a three-week blockade of the Taiwan Strait would brown out the industry, and observed that building a major semiconductor factory takes about four years and roughly six years to achieve profitability.

Predictions & Forward Calls

  • Breakthrough Memory Architectures: Pat Gelsinger predicts that novel memory architectures (such as stacked memory directly on or integrated into compute layers by companies like Dmatrix, Next Silicon, and acquisitions like Nvidia's purchase of Grock) will achieve 10x to 50x better bandwidth than shoreline-limited HBM, fundamentally changing the industry by the end of the decade.
  • Persistent Shortages: Pat Gelsinger and Andrew Homan assert that supply limits and strong demand for HBM will persist for several more years, with no quick fix for supply constraints regardless of capital investments.
  • Long-Term Contracting: Hoshik Kim notes that memory makers are shifting away from pure commodity models toward contract-based, long-term agreements lasting five years or more.

Notable Quotes

"HBM is a lousy memory... You're stacking DRAM. DRAM doesn't like to be stacked because essentially you're creating a thermal sandwich... It is just the best one we have right now." — *Pat Gelsinger*
"We semis investors sleep like a baby... but we wake up every 3 hours to check what's going on in the Asian markets." — *Vijay Shilpiekandula*
"Memory is no longer a cost anymore; we see memory as an investment to AI infrastructure." — *Hoshik Kim*
So today, I think we are going to discuss one of the most critical issues in the AI space: what we call the memory wall. Basically, we are looking at why memory is a bottleneck right now in the development of AI. I am very glad today to be joined on stage by Pat Gelsinger, who is a partner at Playground Global and was the Intel CEO from 2021 to 2024. Please applaud him. Pat doesn't need much introduction, but I will give you a few biographical points for you to grasp how deep his experience in the AI and semiconductor space is. Pat joined Intel when he was 18 years old in 1979. He got involved in the x86 processor architecture, which is the dominating architecture for all desktop processors. Later, Pat was promoted to CTO of Intel in 2001, left in 2009, and went to EMC before being called back by Intel to head the company in 2021. So, enough with the biography. I think the goal today is to help people understand why memory is an issue in the AI space. Pat, if you could explain to us why it's such an issue, and please speak to me like I were a five-year-old child or a golden retriever. Okay, so let’s dive in. Why is memory a problem today? I call it the threefold problem. First, AI demand is somewhat insatiable; we just have so much demand for AI. The token economics—the value of tokens—is essentially unlimited as they become more and more intelligent. Second, we are now making memory requirements for that computation larger and larger, meaning bigger context windows and model sizes. So, I want more memory. But then the last thing is, when you type in a prompt, how long do you want to wait? You want first-token latency to be dramatically low, so you want it to be large and fast. That trio has led us to a period of time where memory has never had it this good. We used to joke in the semiconductor industry that if you were a memory company, you had one good year out of four, and the other three sucked; that defined the memory industry for essentially 50 years. We are now in five good, becoming great, years for memory. With HBM, memory should never have 90% margin. It is just insanely good that we are seeing this demand today because AI demand is so insatiable. Typically, when I enter a prompt, the latency that we have behind it—you want it fast and you want it to be large. I want to know everything about everything I might query, and I want it to have context on me to make it most appropriate. That just makes memory demand and memory bandwidth extraordinary. Maybe you could explain to the audience in very simple terms the solution the industry has put forward to solve that memory issue. We’ve heard about HBM, high-bandwidth memory. HBM is a lousy memory, and I know my memory friends are about to come on stage and calling their baby ugly is not the best way to make friends. But it’s a lousy memory. You are stacking DRAM, and DRAM doesn't like to be stacked because essentially you are creating a thermal sandwich, which is bad. It’s shoreline bandwidth limited, so I am trying to get bandwidth through this point-to-point connection between the memory and the GPU. It is bitwise inefficient; for every bit that I am creating for HBM, I’ve given up four bits of memory nominally. It is power inefficient and bandwidth inefficient. It is just the best one we have right now. HBM itself is a bad memory, but it’s the tallest that we have to satisfy the near-term demands of AI. Fundamentally, we need much better memory architectures if we are going to realize the full benefits that AI will enable for the future. We have to do better in memory over time if we are going to fulfill our dreams for AI. Since you are in the VC space now, can you give us a hint of the innovation happening to solve this HBM imperfection? Obviously, we now have $3 trillion market-cap memory companies, and with that, we are seeing an enormous amount of R&D investment. Fundamentally, the idea that memory sits horizontal to the compute requirements is changing. I expect that the majority of memory needs to be integrated into or on top of the compute requirements. Different memory architectures are being worked on. Companies like Groq—part of what they were acquired for by Nvidia is because they were solving the memory challenge. We have companies like our company, DMatrix, or Next Silicon, that are doing stacked memory on top of the compute layer as a more efficient way. You are not shoreline limited anymore; you have the full XY, either hybrid-bonded or micro-bumped onto the GPU, as a much more effective way to create memory bandwidth. I think these breakthroughs will start to change the memory industry before the end of the decade. I understand from what you said that until the end of the decade, HBM will stay the best technology to process AI? Supply chains for semiconductors are long, expensive, and capital-intensive. A great innovation today may take three or four years to become volume in the industry. I fully expect that we are supply-limited on HBM for several more years, irrespective of how much capital gets invested. We are going to see that demand be essentially unconstrained. That said, these innovative architectures will solve capacity and bandwidth in fundamentally better ways that are more thermally and bitwise efficient. Memory will get a lot better because, fundamentally, AI is a memory problem. Once we have solved models like Transformers, everything else is about doing those functions over and over again on large memory sets and large context windows. It’s never been a greater time to be a memory person than the age of AI. I think we need to be joined on stage by the other speakers. This was the easy discussion and now it’s going to be a debate. Let’s bring our other speakers on stage. It’s pretty unusual to be as many people on the same stage. Guys, we just had Pat giving his perspective, which was super interesting. Maybe you could each introduce yourself very quickly so that people can understand how complementary you are to the discussion. I’m Andrew Hman, managing partner of Maverick Silicon. We are a private-focused semiconductor and AI infrastructure fund based in Santa Clara. Hi, I’m Vijay Shalpandala. I’m an operator turned investor. I’ve been doing this for the last 11 years in the public markets. Before that, I was a hardware engineering researcher and operator. None of this constitutes investment advice; you should understand my employer has positions in the space. I have a focus on systems co-design, which is how you integrate hardware and software systems from the get-go. Hello, my name is Hoshik Kim. I’m senior vice president of SK Hynix. I do system architecture research and design for memory solutions. My job is unique because memory is such a critical component in the overall AI computing infrastructure that we now have to understand the whole system: AI models, software, hardware accelerators, and memory. Every time you type a request into ChatGPT, the brain—the GPU—is waiting for the memory to give the data that it requires to write the proper answer. That is the issue we have in memory. The solution the industry has invented is HBM, which is increasing the amount of information that is flowing to the GPU. I’m going to open on a provocative question since Pat said HBM was an imperfect solution. Hoshik, what do you think of this statement? I agree with Pat. HBM is not the answer. SK Hynix is admitting that HBM is a bad memory? No, I’m not saying it's a bad memory. It’s a good memory, but it’s not the final answer. HBM will not solve the memory wall problem. The memory wall is an inherent AI problem which you cannot avoid. We try our best to provide higher bandwidth with HBM technology, even though it has limitations like thermal, I/O cost, and so on. But that’s the only thing we have right now to help ease this problem. At the same time, we are working in different directions with different solutions. HBM is believed by many to not be a commodity anymore. Can you explain why so many people have this belief? I’ll kick it off and then pass it over to Vijay. You have a fundamental change happening in terms of the co-optimization between the logic—the GPU—and the memory itself. There is a much tighter integration that frankly didn't exist with the memory you would find in your iPhone. It is much more difficult to swap and replace one supplier with another. You are making design decisions years in advance, working closely between the memory partner and the compute partner, which by definition creates a much tighter relationship. It makes it much less of a commodity. I agree with Andrew. We have inherited a von Neumann architecture from 80 years ago where the processor sits far from memory. Imagine a factory that makes goods very well but cannot store much, and all raw materials are stored in a warehouse far away connected by a narrow highway. The importance of that becomes clear if you look at leaders like Elon Musk. Gavin Baker recently said that memory is the biggest bottleneck out there. You have to pay this memory bandwidth tax at each rung of the scale. It looks like we are moving toward more custom-built designs than commoditized ones, partly because the base die is likely to be a logic foundry die, which means you have a better traffic control system. 40 years ago, you had almost 20 memory players; now you only have three: SK Hynix, Samsung, and Micron. You have had a massive consolidation. Is HBM manufacturing complexity changing the dynamic, and should we expect even fewer players going forward? I do think you have mischaracterized the industry a little bit, as you have second-tier players and Chinese players as well. There is a huge separation between the top three and everyone else, but I do think the comment about the custom aspects of memory is correct. Particularly as you start looking at stacked memory, there is going to be a question of who is going to be innovating in a new business model, because everything has been governed by JEDEC standards until now. All of a sudden, custom designs create a different business model. I like to joke that Jensen is the highest-margin memory controller that has ever shipped in human history with his GPUs, packaging his high-margin memories with his high-margin chips. There is a lot of disruption to be created here. As you said, the number of bits per wafer that goes out of HBM is four times lower than for DRAM. The HBM demand is eating the DRAM. I was working out this morning, and I was trying to figure out a simple way to explain this. Think of SK Hynix as a waffle maker. If your waffle machine usually cooks a waffle in five minutes, you can make 12 waffles per hour. With HBM, it takes 15 minutes, so you can only make four per hour. You have a fixed manufacturing footprint, and given the complexity of HBM, you are significantly limited in your output. You’re right. Manufacturing throughput is very low compared to standard DRAM, and the production yield is low too. As Pat mentioned, HBM has a lot of I/O logic, so it takes a lot of DRAM space. With the same amount of density, we need a bigger die—roughly three times bigger. So with the same wafer, we produce one-third of the DRAM compared to commodity. That’s the "HBM squeeze" problem. Also, the manufacturing process is much more complex, and we stack DRAM in 12 or 16 levels, which suffers from compounding yield loss. It boils down to four times less production. We have talked a lot about the technical aspects, but what about the geopolitical aspect? When you look at the production of memory, it is very concentrated. You have two South Korean players with 70% of the market and Micron with 30%. How do you deal with that risk? As semiconductor investors, we sleep like a baby, but we wake up every three hours to check what’s going on in the Asian markets. Memory is very concentrated in Asia, whether it’s the front-end makers in Korea or the packaging players in Taiwan. Geopolitical tension is something we always think about. The way we think about this is through sizing; we size up our positions based on our understanding of the probability of different events. There is also localization happening in China and the US with new announcements. The thing that’s often missed is that the complexity is increasing, and it’s important to pick players who have the highest business quality and the tailwinds to make this complicated piece of infrastructure. This is not just a semiconductor problem; this is a global S&P 500 problem. Semiconductors touch the entire economy. There is a big push to bring manufacturing onshore, but that is easier said than done. I empathize with the CEOs of these corporations because they are getting pushed incredibly hard by customers, as supply is very tight. They are being asked to build more capacity in specific countries, but these are physical problems you cannot snap your fingers and fix overnight. If you and I had an extra $100 billion, it would still take about four years until we brought a major factory online. You always have the hesitation regarding the durability of demand for these long, expensive cycles. These factories probably don't produce profit until year six. I spent a lot of the last five years focused on the CHIPS Act, creating resilient, not independent, supply chains. In the age of AI, more of our economic capacity has moved to the technology supply chains, and we are simply not resilient. If the Taiwan Straits were blockaded for three weeks, we would brown out the industry. The world needs more resilient supply chains. The price of memory chips has gone through the roof because of this shortage. We are running at a 7% deficit between supply and demand on HBM. How do you see that deficit evolving? It’s very difficult to forecast the long term, so short-term things feel very tight. Apple raised prices across its product line, and they are the most sophisticated memory procurement machine on the planet; they clearly expect this to last for a while. Looking further out, it’s challenging. I would say memory is no longer just a component; it is critical core infrastructure for data centers. Everyone in this room is trying to build AI infrastructure, and they all require memory. There are modest amounts of additional capacity coming online, but those were decisions made three years ago. We know exactly what those expansions are. You have this 4:1 tradeoff for HBM versus other memory, which makes all other memory less available—less for PCs, servers, and iPhones. The years of plenty are many years away. I don't expect this was the last price increase by Apple or others; it’s just going to be constrained for quite a while. The industry has focused on improving capacity, but now we are moving to a focus on bandwidth per watt. Bandwidth goes as a function of shoreline—the perimeter—whereas capacity is a function of area. As we go to future generations, we will still have a memory bandwidth deficit. The demand itself is growing exponentially, while supply is only growing as a linear function. The thing that excites me most is the new architectures. When you get to new inference chips, you get stacked memory and new approaches. That’s what motivated the Groq acquisition. Those memory bandwidths become 10 to 50 times better than shoreline-limited HBM. Those things start to impact the market, but it doesn't really materialize until late this decade. What are the elements that could change the equilibrium between supply and demand? What is the "bear case" that might make the market less supply-constrained? The bear case is a change in memory architecture. If the memory intensity per token falls more than the usage can pick up, that could impair the top line for a lot of these companies. The other bear case is just on the supply side; we’ve seen in past cycles that memory makers get excited about demand projections and overshoot on capex. If that happens, you get the classic "bullwhip effect." I don't think it’s going to be a lack of demand for compute, but rather some external shock—like unrest in the US regarding data centers, or a geopolitical event in Taiwan that screws up the logic supply chain, or power not coming online. It wouldn't be a lack of demand for compute, but rather those types of constraints. When you look at HBM volumes, they are around 23% to 25% of total output; the rest is DRAM. When we see price increases on smartphones and desktops, we could see demand destruction in a market that represents 75% of volumes. How do you frame the risk of demand destruction due to DRAM price increases? It’s a difficult question. As I said, this AI infrastructure is a structural change. As long as the investment into the AI ecosystem continues, memory will continue to be demanded. Commodity DRAM has a serious shortage problem, which is why we’ve started to produce memory on a contract basis—long-term agreements of five years or more. I hope and believe demand will continue. Markets like PCs and cell phones are price-sensitive. We know there is demand elasticity in those markets, and we’ve already seen some of those characteristics. The only answer is to build more factories or get to more efficient architectures that are more token-per-bit efficient than what we have today. Thanks, guys, for your incredible perspective. I hope you in the room have a better understanding of the memory shortage and why we think AI has a memory problem. Give a great round of applause to our speakers.

U.S. Trade Representative Jamieson Greer testifies on Trump's trade policy agenda — 7/22/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M)·95:58·7.1K views·2026-07-22
Jamieson GreerU.S. Trade Representative
TL;DRU.S.
Trade Representative Jamieson Greer testified before the Senate Finance Committee on the Trump administration's 2026 trade policy agenda, defending the use of targeted tariffs and reciprocal trade agreements to drive domestic re-industrialization, while bipartisan senators...

TL;DR

U.S. Trade Representative Jamieson Greer testified before the Senate Finance Committee on the Trump administration's 2026 trade policy agenda, defending the use of targeted tariffs and reciprocal trade agreements to drive domestic re-industrialization, while bipartisan senators pushed back on rising consumer costs, trade disruptions, and agricultural strain.

Economic Indicators & Trade Deficits

  • Trade Deficit Reduction: Ambassador Greer reported that the U.S. trade deficit in goods for the 12 months ending May 2026 decreased by 24% compared to the same period a year earlier, following the April 2025 implementation of the reciprocal trade program.
  • China Dependency: The U.S. goods trade deficit with China fell to $200 billion in 2025 (the lowest since 2005), and China's share of total U.S. imports dropped to approximately 9% (the lowest since joining the WTO in 2001).
  • Manufacturing Metrics: Manufacturing real wages increased by over $2,000 under President Trump, productivity surged 2.4% in Q4 2025, and manufacturing wages rose 4.7%. Capital goods accounted for 33% of imports in 2025 and 40% in 2026.
  • Inflation & Consumer Prices: Greer cited a 0.4% drop in the Consumer Price Index in June (the largest decline since April 2020) and year-over-year core inflation at 2.6%. Conversely, Ranking Member Wyden cited estimates from the Congressional Budget Office and Treasury showing that 2025 tariffs cost the average American family $1,700.

Reciprocal Trade Deals & Market Access

  • Global Agreements: The U.S. currently has 19 framework or reciprocal trade deals covering 32% of global gross domestic product. Countries like Cambodia, Argentina, Malaysia, and Indonesia are implementing or scheduled to implement tariff reductions to zero.
  • USMCA Negotiations: Ambassador Greer stated he is traveling to Mexico City to meet with President Claudia Sheinbaum and Secretary Marcelo Ebrard regarding ongoing USMCA negotiations, aiming for interim arrangements with Canada and Mexico by the end of the year.
  • Agricultural Trade: Greer noted that the monthly agricultural trade deficit under President Biden ($6.2 billion) has fallen to under $3 billion under President Trump. However, Senators Bennet, Grassley, and others raised severe alarms over losses for soybean, corn, and wheat farmers facing high input costs and retaliatory risks.

Tariffs, Authorities, and Legislation

  • Congressional Trade Powers Reform Act: Ranking Member Wyden announced the introduction of the *Congressional Trade Powers Reform Act*, which would overhaul trade authorities and require congressional approval for any presidential tariff.
  • Canadian and Chinese Tariffs: Debate centered on recent 50% tariffs announced on Canadian goods using a provision based on the Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act of 1930. Senators challenged whether Canadian goods would face higher tariffs than Chinese goods in certain sectors.
  • Tariff Refunds and Small Businesses: Senators White House and Welch raised concerns that small businesses struggle to navigate Customs and Border Protection (CBP) online portals for tariff refunds, and that large corporations may retain refunds rather than passing them to consumers. Greer maintained that the CBP portal is user-friendly and that foreign/domestic importers of record can file for refunds.

Sector-Specific Concerns & Supply Chains

  • Critical Minerals: Discussions covered U.S. access to processed critical minerals and rare earths from China, as well as diversification efforts in Central Asia (Kazakhstan and Uzbekistan) and Indonesia (nickel and raw ore exports).
  • Agriculture and Inputs: Senators raised specific regional issues including fertilizer and anhydrous ammonia supply (Grassley), Mexican pork investigations, Colorado farm losses (Bennet), Montana palladium mining under pressure from Russian dumping (Danes), Minnesota taconite and sugar quotas (Smith), Wyoming soda ash in India and beef/sheep markets (Barrasso), and Tennessee whiskey and timber (Blackburn).
  • Tourism and Aerospace: Senators Cortez Masto and Welch highlighted a $14 billion trade deficit in travel/tourism in 2025 and raised concerns regarding Section 232 investigations impacting aerospace and aviation.

Notable Quotes

"The trade policy the president seeks to solve are generational. These things were not broken in a day, and they won't be fixed overnight, but they must be fixed. This is why I'm pleased to report that the combination of tariffs and deals core to the president's trade policy has yielded quick and impressive results." — Ambassador Jamieson Greer
"Like so many other promises Donald Trump has made to working people, the second he set foot in the White House, he did nothing to put American businesses and workers first. What he did is he embarked on a tariff spree that put the cost of living crisis on steroids in America." — Senator Ron Wyden
"The surplus and the deficit is a result of the fact that you have made it more expensive for us to buy products from other places. And it has absolutely nothing to do with the question I'm asking you..." — Senator Michael Bennet

Today's hearing is an opportunity to take stock of where we are and where we need to go. First, US trade policy must reflect our strategic priorities. Market access matters. It remains a fundamental driver of American economic growth. American producers and service providers depend on the ability to compete fairly in global markets. Unfair and discriminatory barriers to US exports must be removed, whether through tariff reductions, the elimination of non-tariff barriers, or the establishment of clear and transparent rules, creating opportunities for businesses of all sizes and supporting high-quality jobs here at home. To understand why we need to do this, we need to look no further than the US-Mexico-Canada Agreement, or the USMCA. The USMCA supports 13 million American jobs, represents a 55% increase in investments in the US from Canada and Mexico compared to pre-USMCA levels, and has led to 60 billion dollars in exports in agricultural goods, nearly one-third of all US agriculture exports. Fair market access and its enforcement is constantly tested by unfair trade practices used against our farmers and ranchers. Idaho producers often tell me about how these practices create significant economic hardships for our agriculture community, including among our dairy, onion, potato, sheep, and sugarbeet industries. Meanwhile, a growing array of discriminatory measures in the form of digital service taxes, online content requirements, and data localization target US digital firms and restrict US competition barriers. These pose an existential threat to American innovation. Similarly, concerns with foreign pharmaceutical price controls are top of mind as the US begins to negotiate pharmaceutical deals. Finally, business certainty in the trade environment is crucial for American industry to take full advantage of market opening opportunities so they can grow and compete effectively. In all these matters, close collaboration and consultation between Congress and the administration is essential to advance the policies needed to promote durable growth and fairness. A combined effort between Congress and the administration is the most effective means by which to identify new opportunities for market access, strengthen enforcement tools, and meet emerging challenges in the global trading system. The challenges and opportunities in global trade are complex, but they present a chance to strengthen our economy and reaffirm our leadership on the world stage. Glad to have a chance to talk about these crucial issues this morning. The Trump trade agenda is dominated by big promises. First, he promised lower costs. Second, he promised to be tough on China. Third, he promised to open up new markets for American farmers and small businesses. These promises haven't panned out. About a year and a half into the Trump second term, farmers are on the ropes. I was just home for six town hall meetings in rural Oregon in communities where Donald Trump got more than 70% of the vote. The small businesses, like a local toy shop in Portland, are closing their doors after high tariffs made their businesses impossible. Uncertainty is costing workers jobs, including at Portland coffee roasters. Supply chains have been disrupted. Small businesses are delaying investments in new employees and manufacturing. Like so many other promises Donald Trump has made to working people, the second he set foot in the White House, he did nothing to put American businesses and workers first. What he did is he embarked on a tariff spree that put the cost of living crisis on steroids in America. The Supreme Court eventually overturned the Trump global tariffs, but the higher costs are here to stay. In 2025, the Trump tariffs cost the average American family $1,700 with grocery and gas prices way up. Americans ought to know that nobody, not even Herbert Hoover, used this provision before, but Donald Trump dug up a zombie law to make things even more expensive for Americans. Trump's next trade scheme is ordering USTR to reconstruct his illegal global tariffs under the guise of addressing forced labor. As I've recently said, if the administration wants to get serious about forced labor, the first step is to look at its own enforcement record. Donald Trump has failed to enforce the laws that we've got on the books and has cut government efforts dedicated to fighting forced labor around the world. Instead of agreements, what I've seen is we have press releases and announcements about supposed deals that haven't been enforced, haven't opened markets, and haven't reduced tariffs. My own take on these proposals is there is no "there there." The one piece of the Trump administration trade agenda that does seem to be progressing is the corrupt enrichment of so many in the Trump orbit: his family, his allies, and himself. Donald Trump lowered tariffs on Switzerland after Swiss billionaires gifted Trump a gold bar worth more than $100,000. A Korean company under investigation for trade cheating paid the Trump Holding Company $2 million. My own take is the way to stop this corruption and chaos is to take unfettered tariff power away from the president. It's well past time to put Congress back in the driver's seat on trade. That's why today I'm able to introduce the Congressional Trade Powers Reform Act. It overhauls existing trade powers to ensure that no future presidential administration can abuse its authority on trade and thrust the country into a cost of living crisis. It would require congressional approval of any tariff proposed by the president. And it would establish an advisory committee to ensure that Congress actually is in a position to exercise its rightful oversight over tariffs and trade. It is time to restore consistency, reliability, and success in our country's trade agenda. That's what my bill is all about, and I look forward to talking to colleagues on both sides of the aisle on that. I look forward to today's question and answer, Ambassador. We're glad you're here, as I've said, and please proceed. Thank you, Ranking Member Wyden, Chairman Crapo, Senator Bennett, and the rest of the committee. I also thank you for keeping in touch with me as USTR has prioritized our consultations with Congress during this busy time for trade policy. As you know, the president's trade policy in 2025 was characterized by a national emergency, a 1.2 trillion dollar trade deficit which had exploded 40% in the prior four years, and an industrial base too atrophied to support our national security and economic competitiveness adequately, let alone support a strong middle class. That national emergency still persists and so our policy remains the same. The specific authorities this administration is using have changed, but the trade strategy has not. We are committed to continuing to use tariffs and to negotiate deals to support the re-industrialization of our economy, protect American workers, and increase their wages and shrink our trade deficit. The problems the president's trade policy seeks to solve are generational. These things were not broken in a day, and they won't be fixed overnight, but they must be fixed. This is why I'm pleased to report that the combination of tariffs and deals core to the president's trade policy has yielded quick and impressive results. For starters, the United States currently has 19 framework or reciprocal trade deals covering 32% of global gross domestic product. Here's one in my hand. It's the deal with Indonesia; it's substantive. There are annexes. There are tariff schedules of all the things that they'll be reducing for the United States. And I have a dozen more deals like this. The new market access secured in these deals and the protection they offer from unfair trade practices has been a boon to our producers. Goods and services exports have marked the highest monthly levels on record in American history from February through May of this year. Everyone said trade would collapse. We've never exported as much as we are now. And on the other side of the ledger, capital goods represent the largest share of our imports ever, accounting for 33% of imports in 2025 and 40% so far this year. In other words, we've shifted the bulk of our imports from consumer goods and autos to things we need to scale re-industrialization like machine tools, injection molding equipment, and computing equipment. The United States has not stopped trading with the world, but the composition of that trade has changed in a way that benefits Americans. We're selling more to the world than ever before because of our increased capacity to produce, and our imports are increasingly the type of goods that help us produce even more here in America. And these dynamics have helped reduce our trade deficit. After the introduction of the president's reciprocal trade program in April 2025, the US trade deficit in goods for the 12 months ending in May 2026 decreased by 24% compared with the same period a year earlier. Instead of an average annual 8% increase in the trade deficit we experienced under President Biden in 2025, under President Trump, that rate of increase slowed substantially and now it's being reversed. For agricultural trade specifically, the story is even better. President Biden left office with a monthly trade deficit of 6.2 billion. Under President Trump, it's under three billion. And we are on the road to an agricultural trade surplus again. Services exports have also taken advantage of new market access. In 2025, the US services trade surplus reached a record high of 327 billion. A large part of the positive change in our trade deficit is due to reduced dependence on China. Our trade deficit in goods with China fell to 200 billion in 2025, the lowest it's been since 2005. And China's share of total US imports fell to about 9%, the lowest it has been since China joined the World Trade Organization in 2001. So these trends are good for our economy and our national security, but they're really good for the individual workers and the families who make up that economy. Real manufacturing worker pay has increased by over $2,000 under President Trump after falling over $800 during President Biden's four years in office. Manufacturing productivity surged by 2.4% in the last quarter of 2025 compared to the same period in 2024, and manufacturing wages increased by 4.7%. In other words, the average American manufacturing worker is producing more goods and getting paid more to do it, and there are over half a million open manufacturing jobs. So, there's a lot of opportunity here. In June, the consumer price index fell 0.4%, the largest decline since April 2020 and far below the expectations of a 1.1% drop. Core inflation was 2.6% year-over-year, down from 3.3% in January of 2025. And again, better than market expectations. I'm looking forward today to talking to you more about our section 301 investigations, about where we are with USMCA, about our critical minerals, and a variety of other initiatives that we have. And I really just want to close with this thought. Fundamentally, all of these initiatives and the trade policy overall aim to answer the question of what should our economy look like. For President Trump and his administration, the answer is clear. We want an economy that is resilient from shocks and not dependent on foreign production for critical goods. We want an economy with an increased level of manufacturing, certainty of food security, and a robust services sector. We want an economy benefiting from an international economic system that promotes fairness and balance. We want an economy that prioritizes American workers by protecting them from unfair trading practices and creating economic conditions that raise their wages. An economy that looks like this is one everyone supports, I'm sure, and I look forward to our conversation today and working with you to continue to make this economic vision a reality. The Golden Age of Software Engineering | Scott Wu, Cognition | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.8K)·13:08·23 views·2026-07-22
Scott WuCEO & Co-Founder, Cognition
TL;DRAI software engineering agents like Devin have advanced from 30-second chatbots to 16-hour autonomous workers capable of handling entire end-to-end tasks, enabling companies to 10x their shipped software output.
Model Capability Explosion: Evaluated using METR study benchmarks...

TL;DR

AI software engineering agents like Devin have advanced from 30-second chatbots to 16-hour autonomous workers capable of handling entire end-to-end tasks, enabling companies to 10x their shipped software output.

The Evolution of AI Coding Capabilities

  • Model Capability Explosion: Evaluated using METR study benchmarks tracking unattended task length, AI models have progressed from 30-second tasks (GPT-3.5 / ChatGPT era) to tasks spanning 16 hours or multiple days.
  • Industry-Wide Progress: Flagship models across all major AI labs have reached capability saturation, consistently executing complex, multi-step engineering tasks.
  • Closing the Loop: Moving beyond tab-complete and code generation (GitHub Copilot era), agents now operate autonomously across the entire workflow:
    • Understanding & Planning: Analyzing codebases and planning changes.
    • Testing & Review: Utilizing computer/browser use to execute tests and perform code reviews independently.

Organizational Transformation & Autonomous Operations

  • Cognition Case Study: Cognition increased its engineering headcount by 40% (from ~45 to ~60) while 10x-ing its shipped software volume over a 6-month period between November 2025 and April 2026.
  • Autonomous Session Kickoff: At Cognition, over 90% of code is committed by Devin, and the majority of Devin sessions are now initiated asynchronously via automated triggers (e.g., performance monitoring, DataDog errors) rather than human prompts.
  • Event-Driven Workflows: Teams are refactoring engineering organizations around parallel, event-driven execution:
    • Bulk CVE Remediation: Automated security vulnerability scanning and patching.
    • Large-Scale Refactors: Handling massive codebase upgrades file-by-file.
    • Bug Triage: Direct routing of customer reports to agents for automated pull request generation.

Shifting Metrics: From Tokens to Business Outcomes

  • The "Token Maxing" Era: Short-lived phase (January to May 2026) where organizations measured AI usage by token volume—noted by Uber burning through its entire annual token budget in just 3 months.
  • Outcome-Based Evaluation: Measurement is shifting away from lines of code or token consumption toward actual business impact and project outcomes.
  • The Software Abundance Reality: Rather than replacing human engineers, 10x productivity addresses the massive global backlog of unbuilt software across industries like finance (Goldman Sachs), automotive (Mercedes-Benz), retail (Lowe's), and healthcare (Elevance).

Notable Quotes

"What would you do with an infinite army of software engineers?" — Scott Wu
"We're getting to a point where there's real saturation on a lot of tasks... all the models are good enough to just go and do these tasks." — Scott Wu
"Every human software engineer is 10 times more productive, but guess what, we have 10 times more things to go build." — Scott Wu
I’m Scott. I’m here from Cognition; we make Devin, the AI software engineer. I wanted to talk a little bit about what we call the golden age of software engineering. So, a little bit about us: we’re Cognition. We got started about two and a half years ago. A lot of what we do, obviously, is we make Devin, the AI software engineer. We work with a lot of the biggest companies around the world. As you can see, Goldman Sachs, Mercedes-Benz, Lowe's, Elevance, etc. We work in all of the different geographies. We’re big in the US and Europe, Latin America, Asia-Pacific, Middle East, and so forth. The natural kind of first question I would ask is: what do all of these folks have in common? What do Goldman Sachs and Mercedes-Benz and Lowe's have in common? Well, the simple answer is, in 2026, all of these companies are software companies, because every company is a software company, right? In the same way that we don't say that companies are internet companies anymore, we don't really say that companies are software companies anymore. All of these folks have tens of thousands of software engineers. They have tons and tons of things that they're building. For Mercedes, it might be all the little details that go into the car, the software, and the process management. For Goldman Sachs, it might be all the transactions that they process or all the internal tools that they're building and using. All of these have tons and tons of software. The first question that I always ask folks in terms of how to think about this is: look, what would you do with an infinite army of software engineers? I would ask all of you to spend a second and just think about that. What would you do with an infinite army of software engineers? I ask this, and then people kind of assume that I'm asking a theoretical question, and yeah, it's cool to imagine what is going to happen in a few years. I think everyone has seen that AI coding has taken off. I don't know if people have understood really deeply just how much it has. For us internally, one of the craziest stats—which is one of my favorite stats to share—is that at Cognition, we use a lot of Devin to go build Devin, unsurprisingly. Our own amount of software that we've shipped has literally exploded. It has gone crazy in the last six to eight months. Over the course of these six months, from November to the end of April, we literally 10x the amount of software that we shipped. We brought in some more engineers; we added about 40% to our engineering head count, which was meaningful. We went from like 45 to 60 or something like that, but obviously, the much bigger impact is in what software has done. Remember, guys, software engineering and AI coding was already a big thing in November 2025. So, what changed exactly over the course of six months that allowed us to ship 10 times more and do 10 times more? Well, I think a few things in particular. First of all, the models and the core capabilities just keep getting better. I think the single biggest thing that I always think of when I think about this is this METR study, which looks at the average length of a task that an AI can do without any interruption. It can do it all in one shot and figure out all the details. Not that long ago, a few years ago, we were talking about 30 seconds of work. If you take a look at GPT-3.5 right in the middle of that chart, 30 seconds of work is not bad, by the way. That was the ChatGPT moment. That was the exciting thing. You have some questions, and it can have that conversation with you. Now, we actually kind of broke the scale. It turns out the longest tasks in the data set don't go that much beyond 16-hour tasks, but the latest models kind of just go and solve all of this. It's worth thinking about because when you're talking about 30 seconds at a time, you are talking about an assistant. You're talking about a chatbot. You're talking about something where you can ask a question and it will give you that answer in 30 seconds. When you're talking about something that can do 16 hours of work or even multiple days of work—which is kind of what we're getting into now—you really are talking about a full AI software engineer. You're giving it an entire task, you're giving it a project, and you're asking it to own things end-to-end. It's not just any one model, which is worth calling out. It's actually all the different models. Every single one of them has obviously gotten much, much better. We're getting to a point where there's real saturation on a lot of tasks; the models are good enough to just go and do these tasks. What does that mean? For us, how you really get the meaningful gains here is by having the agents close the loop. Previously, one of the big things that folks were talking about was writing the code, and the first big breakthrough in AI coding was GitHub Copilot—it was tab complete, you were looking at the files, and you were having these things go through. Now, rather than just writing the code, you are doing every single step autonomously. What that means, obviously, is you can have the agent take on this entire thing. You're tagging the agent onto a ticket, or there's a customer that had an issue, and you're just asking the agent to go solve that issue. You ask Devin to go solve that issue, and it's going to do every single one of these steps. It's going to understand the code base, it's going to plan out the change, it's going to write the code, and it’s going to have computer use, so it's going to be able to test the code by itself and do review. That's how you get the real gains. It means that you can have them initiate their own work. You can start their sessions autonomously and have these things go end-to-end. One of my favorite stats is how we 10x our software development rate: we have Devin write it all. You see the percent of code committed by Devin going up to that 90-plus percent. We basically use Devin for pretty much everything right now. The thing that's even crazier is that Devin actually also does the majority of starting Devins. This is all of the Devin sessions at Cognition by week; this is the fraction started interactively by a human saying, "Hey Devin, can you please go do this?" And this is the fraction started asynchronously, because there was an automated event or a trigger. Every single thing you can imagine—it could be Devin starting sub-agents or planning out specific tasks. For example, if you have API routes that you want to monitor for performance, you run automated triggers to run those routes and check how performance compares to one week ago. If it's somehow noticeably slower, then you spin up a Devin to go take a look. If you have an error in DataDog, you spin up a Devin to go take a look. You run all these automatically. That's what we're seeing as well, where the majority of these sessions can now actually be run and kicked off proactively. All of that is to say that agents can act like real software engineers now. My favorite story on this is, I was in Japan recently to speak to some of our Devin users there. Someone asked me a question during a Q&A session: "Hey, so, what honorific do you think I should be using with Devin?" I said, "What do you mean, what honorific?" And she said, "Well, I talk to Devin all the time, I just don't know: should I say Devin-san? Should I say Devin-kun? Is it Devin-sama?" You can really think of your agents as a full software engineer. They can use the computer themselves, they can work with every single integration you have, they can go test the code, they can do browser use, and they can do all these parts. The next question is how you should reframe your org with that in mind. First of all, you want to do things as parallel and as event-driven as possible. This is what we've seen take off in the last three to six months: almost every mental hook that would make you as a software engineer want to go and do something, you can set up that hook for Devin as well. For example, bulk CVE remediation. All of us have tons of tools that scan the code base to help us figure out potential warnings or security vulnerabilities; you can have Devin go and automatically fix those. You can have Devin tackle big version upgrades or code base refactors at scale, running all these in parallel. The same thing for all these event-driven cases. A customer report comes in, and usually a human has to take that and start off a Devin. Well, you can skip that middle step and just have that go straight to a Devin; you can have a pull request ready for you that you can review. Similarly, for everyday development—working on PRs, handling testing, or building out a feature end-to-end—you can do every single one of these autonomously. The meaningful change that's happened in the last six months is that, by the way, 13% in December of 2025 was a lot. Think about a team of a thousand software engineers, and imagine you could tell them they are getting an extra 130 software engineers' worth of bandwidth to go and build things. You can do a lot with that. That means taking on one-off features or repetitive tasks. The important shift that's happened is that now you can have that work for pretty much everything. 90% is actually not like an extra 130 engineers. You are doing 10 times as much because for a thousand human engineers, you basically have 9,000 AI engineers that go along with that. When you get to that point, you of course care about how to evaluate these based on real business impact. The whole token-maxing era lasted approximately six months. People were talking about how many tokens are you using, which is almost exactly the same as saying how many lines of code did you write. There's a famous story from the '90s or early 2000s about a software engineer whose team was measured on how many lines of code they wrote. At the end of each week, they would submit a report. One engineer submitted a report and said "negative 200." He explained he reimplemented the whole thing way faster, way cheaper, and with a better architecture, and he was able to delete 800 lines of code while writing 600 new ones. They realized lines of code might not be the right metric. We are moving toward what actually matters for your business. This is why we come back to that core question: what would you do with this infinite army of software engineers? What is the additional software that you build? I think it's worth intuiting this, because one of the most common questions we hear is, "What happens to all the human software engineers?" The simple answer is that every human software engineer is 10 times more productive, but we have 10 times more things to go build. Imagine going to the Department of Motor Vehicles, except it works. All of that is software and process that can be better. Imagine logging in and pulling up your medical records, except the product experience is actually good. Imagine logging into your bank and actually being able to do all the things that you want to do. Every company out there has these teams of thousands of software engineers because there's so much software to build, and the reality is there's actually 10 times more of that to go do. A lot of it is holding agents to the standards of project outcomes and end-to-end delivery. We're moving past this token-maxing era, which lasted from January to May or so. There are stories where folks, like Uber, famously said they burned through their entire token budget for the year in just three months. Obviously, the answer is not to say your goal is to do as much with tokens as possible. The answer is to figure out what are the outcomes that you want to drive. You're thinking much more about what outcomes you're seeing and what productivity you're achieving with that capacity. The golden age of software engineering is really here. I've had folks ask me all the time, "All this stuff with AI coding is cool, but when do we really get to abundance?" Guys, we're getting there right now. If your team is doing these things correctly, you literally can do 10 times more. The core question for all of us to think about is: what do you want to build? Thank you, guys.
